\chapter{THE RED PILL}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_jack_in.png}
\end{center}

\section{Welcome to the Machine}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} WELCOME TO THE MACHINE}\vspace{6pt}

You found it. The rabbit hole. \textbf{ZPWR} is not a dotfiles repo. It is not a "framework." It is a fully integrated shell operating system that happens to run inside your terminal.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT IS \textbf{ZPWR}?}\vspace{4pt}

A \$\{\#\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}\}-verb command dispatcher built on top of zsh, tmux, vim/nvim, fzf, and git. Every verb is a surgical tool. Every keybinding is a weapon. Every alias is a shortcut through the matrix.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PHILOSOPHY}\vspace{4pt}

1. Verbs, not scripts. Everything is zpwr <verb>. 2. Discoverability.   Tab completion on everything. 3. Symmetry.          vim* and emacs* verbs mirror each other. 4. Speed.             Compiled zwc files. Benchmarked startup. 5. No magic.          Read the source. It is all zsh.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE DIRECTORY TREE}\vspace{4pt}

\$\textbf{ZPWR} ........................ root of the kingdom \$\textbf{ZPWR\_ENV} .................... env vars and shell init \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS} ................ executable scripts \$\textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD} ............... autoloaded functions \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL} .................. local config (gitignored) \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL} ................ installer scripts \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX} ................... tmux configuration \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TEST} ................... test suite


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} YOUR FIRST COMMAND}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr about    \# behold the banner\\
zpwr help     \# list every verb in color\\
\end{codebox}

Pro tip: zpwr is aliased to many short forms. Type zpwr <tab> and let the completions wash over you.

WARNING: Side effects may include mass alias adoption, involuntary tab-completing in other people's terminals, and an irrational hatred of vanilla bash.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Your First 5 Minutes}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} YOUR FIRST 5 MINUTES}\vspace{6pt}

You just installed zpwr. The terminal blinks. What now? Here is your survival kit for the first 300 seconds.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STEP 1: SEE THE BANNER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr about              \# the ASCII art welcome screen\\
zpwr banner             \# same thing, different alias\\
zpwr bannercounts       \# banner + environment statistics\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STEP 2: COUNT YOUR ARSENAL}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcounts  \# how many aliases, functions, etc.\\
\end{codebox}

This prints a breakdown of your loaded shell environment: aliases, functions, builtins, parameters, completions. If the numbers are big, congratulations. You are armed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STEP 3: DISCOVER THE VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr help               \# colorized list of ALL verbs\\
zpwr verbs     \# fzf fuzzy search through verbs\\
\end{codebox}

The verbsfzf verb is your panic button (alias: subcommandsfzf). Forgot what a command is called? Fuzzy search it. This pipes every verb and its description into fzf. Type a few characters. Watch the list collapse. Hit enter. Execute.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STEP 4: VERIFY YOUR ENVIRONMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr doctor             \# run environment health check\\
zpwr envvars            \# list all zpwr env vars\\
zpwr colortest          \# verify your terminal colors\\
zpwr colortest256       \# the full 256-color palette\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STEP 5: TAB COMPLETE EVERYTHING}\vspace{4pt}

Type zpwr and press <tab>. You will see \$\{\#\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}\} verbs. Type zpwr git and press <tab>. Filtered to git verbs. Type zpwr vim and press <tab>. Filtered to vim verbs. The completion system knows every verb. Trust it.

If you only remember one command from this page: zpwr verbs It is the cheat code to everything else.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The Anatomy of a Verb}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE ANATOMY OF A VERB}\vspace{6pt}

Every zpwr command follows one sacred pattern: zpwr <verb> [args...]


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW DISPATCH WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

When you type zpwr filesearch, here is what happens: 1. The zpwr function receives "filesearch" as \$1 2. It looks up \textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}[filesearch] in the verb table 3. The table entry maps to a function or alias 4. That function is invoked with any remaining args.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE VERB TABLE}\vspace{4pt}

The associative array \textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS} is the brain. Each entry has the format:

\begin{codebox}
ZPWR\_VERBS[verb]='function\_or\_alias=description'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MANY NAMES, ONE FUNCTION}\vspace{4pt}

Verbs often have aliases for muscle memory:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forward  = zpwr f   = zpwr fwd\\
zpwr reverse  = zpwr r   = zpwr rvs = zpwr cdup\\
zpwr github   = zpwr gh  = zpwr mygithub\\
zpwr repo     = zpwr zp  = zpwr zpwr\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TAB COMPLETION IN ACTION}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr git<tab>     \# shows: gitcommit, gitconfig, gitsearch... zpwr vim<tab>     \# shows: vimall, vimrecent, vimctags... zpwr home<tab>    \# shows: home, homeenv, homelocal... zpwr forgit<tab>  \# shows: forgitadd, forgitlog, forgitdiff...


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INSPECT THE TABLE YOURSELF}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr subcommands        \# dump all verbs to stdout\\
zpwr verbs     \# fuzzy search verbs with fzf\\
zpwr verbscount          \# how many verbs exist\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE EDIT SUFFIX PATTERN}\vspace{4pt}

Many verbs come in pairs: filesearch      -> open file after selection filesearchedit  -> type into command line for editing The "edit" variant puts the result on your command line instead of executing immediately. Power and precision.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Configuring Your Rig}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CONFIGURING YOUR RIG}\vspace{6pt}

Your terminal is a cockpit. These are the dials.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FOUR CONFIG SESSIONS}\vspace{4pt}

Each of these opens a curated vim session with the relevant config files already loaded in buffers:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr brc    \# shell aliases file vim session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh} and related files this is where YOUR personal aliases live.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zrc    \# zshrc vim session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens the zshrc and related zsh configs the source of all shell initialization.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vrc    \# vimrc/init.vim vim session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens vim/nvim config files your editor's soul laid bare.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr trc    \# tmux.conf vim session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens tmux configuration pane and window management settings.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE TOKENS FILE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr tokens       \# vim the .tokens.sh file\\
zpwr vimtokens    \# same thing, explicit vim\\
zpwr emacstokens  \# same thing, emacs edition\\
\end{codebox}

Located at \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh} This file is gitignored. Put secrets and custom aliases here. Never commit this file. It is YOUR local override.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{EDITOR} COMMANDS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr edit      \# launch EDITOR\\
zpwr editor    \# same thing\\
zpwr vimconfig \# edit all zpwr configs in vim\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FULL CONFIG STACK}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh} ... your aliases and secrets \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.zpwr\_env.sh} .. env vars and paths \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zshrc} .................. shell entry point \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.vimrc} / init.vim ....... editor config \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.tmux.conf} .............. tmux config

Think of it as: brc for shell, zrc for zsh, vrc for vim, trc for tmux. Four letters, four kingdoms.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{NAVIGATION PROTOCOL}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_the_grid.png}
\end{center}

\section{Directory Warfare}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DIRECTORY WARFARE}\vspace{6pt}

In the filesystem, speed is survival. Typing full paths is for people who have given up on life.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORWARD AND REVERSE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forward <dir>  \# cd into a child directory\\
zpwr f <dir>        \# same thing, shorter\\
zpwr fwd <dir>      \# same thing, medium length\\
\end{codebox}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reverse <dir>  \# cd UP the tree to a parent matching <dir>\\
zpwr r <dir>        \# same thing, one letter\\
zpwr rvs <dir>      \# same thing, three letters\\
zpwr cdup <dir>     \# same thing, cd-up style\\
\end{codebox}

Example: you are in \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/code/myproject/src/lib/utils}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr r myproject    \# jumps UP to \textasciitilde{}/code/myproject\\
\end{codebox}

No more ../../../../ chains. Navigate like a human.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BASIC CD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr cd <path>      \# cd to directory arg\\
\end{codebox}

Seems redundant but integrates with zpwr's dispatch.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} Z: FRECENCY JUMPING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr z <query>      \# jump to frecency-ranked directory\\
\end{codebox}

Z tracks where you go. The more you visit a directory, the higher it ranks. Type a fragment, land at the top match.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zcd            \# fzf list of z-ranked dirs, pick one\\
\end{codebox}

When you cannot remember the exact fragment, zcd shows you the full ranked list in fzf.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRACTICAL COMBOS}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr z proj         \# land in your most-visited "proj" dir zpwr r src          \# climb back up to the "src" parent zpwr f tests        \# dive into the "tests" child.

The pattern is: z for teleportation, f for descent, r for ascent. Three directions. Total coverage.

PROTIP: If you are still typing cd ../../../, this is your intervention.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Home Base Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} HOME BASE OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

Every zpwr directory has a one-word teleporter. No paths. No thinking. Just say where.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE \textbf{HOME} VERB FAMILY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr home                \# cd ZPWR (the root)\\
zpwr homelocal           \# cd ZPWR\_LOCAL\\
zpwr homescripts         \# cd ZPWR\_SCRIPTS\\
zpwr homeenv             \# cd ZPWR\_ENV\\
zpwr homeinstall         \# cd ZPWR\_INSTALL\\
zpwr homeautoload        \# cd ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\\
zpwr homeautoloadcommon   \# cd ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\_COMMON\\
zpwr homecomps           \# cd ZPWR\_COMPS\\
zpwr hometest            \# cd ZPWR\_TEST\\
zpwr hometmux            \# cd ZPWR\_TMUX\\
zpwr homeexercism        \# cd HOME/Exercism\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT LIVES WHERE}\vspace{4pt}

home .............. the zpwr repo root, start of everything homelocal ........ gitignored personal configs and tokens homescripts ...... executable shell scripts homeenv .......... environment variables and init files homeinstall ...... installer scripts for dependencies homeautoload ..... autoloaded functions (the engine room) homeautoloadcommon  platform-independent autoloads homecomps ........ zsh completion definitions hometest ......... test suite files hometmux ......... tmux configuration files


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXAMPLE WORKFLOW}\vspace{4pt}

You want to edit an autoload function:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr homeautoloadcommon\\
\end{codebox}

\# Now you are in the autoload directory \# ls, grep, edit to your heart's content.

You want to check your local tokens:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr homelocal\\
\end{codebox}

\# Lands you right next to .tokens.sh.

Every home* verb is a bookmark you never need to set. The bookmarks are built into the system.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Named Directories}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} NAMED DIRECTORIES}\vspace{6pt}

Zsh has a secret weapon most people never learn: named directories. They turn env vars into tilde paths.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

When you do hash -d \textbf{ZPWR}=\$\textbf{ZPWR}, zsh registers a named directory. Now \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR} expands to \$\textbf{ZPWR} everywhere: in cd, in tab completion, in prompt, in arguments.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR}'S NAMED DIRECTORIES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR ................. ZPWR root\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_SCRIPTS ........ ZPWR\_SCRIPTS\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD ....... ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_ENV ............ ZPWR\_ENV\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_LOCAL .......... ZPWR\_LOCAL\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_INSTALL ........ ZPWR\_INSTALL\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_TMUX ........... ZPWR\_TMUX\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_TEST ........... ZPWR\_TEST\\
\textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_COMPS .......... ZPWR\_COMPS\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT THIS MEANS IN PRACTICE}\vspace{4pt}

Your prompt might show: \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}/common  instead of the full path You can type:

\begin{codebox}
cd \textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_SCRIPTS  and it just works\\
vim \textasciitilde{}ZPWR\_ENV/.zpwr\_env.sh  tilde paths everywhere\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TAB COMPLETION WITH TILDE}\vspace{4pt}

Type cd \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR}<tab> and watch zsh expand the options: \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR}  \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}  \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR\_ENV}  \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL} ... This works in any command, not just cd.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE MECHANISM}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh option AUTO\_NAME\_DIRS turns any exported scalar parameter into a named directory automatically. \textbf{ZPWR} exports its directory variables, so they become named directories for free.

Think of named directories as human-readable symlinks that live in your shell's brain instead of the filesystem. No files created. No cleanup needed. Pure memory.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Quick Jumps}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} QUICK JUMPS}\vspace{6pt}

The home* verbs land you in zpwr directories. These verbs land you in the ecosystem around zpwr.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} INTERNALS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scripts       \# cd to scripts directory\\
\end{codebox}

Shortcut alias: zs.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoload      \# cd to autoload directory\\
\end{codebox}

Shortcut alias: zal.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr completions   \# cd to completion directory\\
zpwr comps         \# same thing\\
\end{codebox}

Shortcut alias: zco.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PLUGIN ECOSYSTEM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr plugins       \# cd to ZSH\_CUSTOM/plugins\\
\end{codebox}

Shortcut alias: zpl This is where oh-my-zsh custom plugins live. Your third-party zsh plugins are neighbors here.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forked        \# cd to ZPWR\_FORKED\_DIR\\
zpwr fp            \# same thing\\
\end{codebox}

Forked repos that zpwr depends on live here. Useful when you need to hack on a dependency.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SPECIAL DIRECTORIES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr hidden        \# cd to ZPWR\_HIDDEN\_DIR\\
\end{codebox}

The hidden directory. Dot-prefixed internal state.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr repo          \# cd to ZPWR\_REPO\_NAME\\
zpwr zp            \# same thing\\
zpwr zpwr          \# same thing (yes, zpwr zpwr)\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE NAVIGATION CHEAT SHEET}\vspace{4pt}

Use case                      Verb --------                      ---- Edit a script .............. zpwr scripts Fix a completion ........... zpwr comps Add an autoload function .. zpwr autoload Debug a plugin ............ zpwr plugins Patch a fork .............. zpwr forked

Each of these is one verb, zero path typing, and instant teleportation to where the work is.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{THE GIT DIMENSION}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_data_stream.png}
\end{center}

\section{Git Basics}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} GIT BASICS}\vspace{6pt}

Git is already a language. \textbf{ZPWR} makes it a dialect you can actually speak without consulting the man page.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE COMMIT PIPELINE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcommit "your message here"\\
\end{codebox}

This is not just git commit. It does: git add -> git commit -> git push All in one verb. The full gacp (git add commit push). Because who commits without pushing? Cowards.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONFIG AND IGNORES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitconfig         \# edit git config file\\
zpwr gitglobalconfig   \# same thing, explicit name\\
zpwr gitignore         \# edit .git/info/exclude (local)\\
zpwr gitignores        \# edit global git ignores file\\
zpwr gitglobalignores  \# same as gitignores\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STASH AND DIFF (VIA FORGIT)}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} delegates interactive stash and diff to forgit:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitdiff        \# fzf-powered git diff viewer\\
zpwr forgitstash       \# fzf-powered stash browser\\
\end{codebox}

See page 13 for the full forgit arsenal.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REMOTE OPERATIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitremotes        \# list all git remotes\\
zpwr gitdir            \# check if pwd is a git dir\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TAGS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitedittag        \# print tag to BUFFER\\
zpwr gitupdatetag      \# print tag and latest msg to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REAL-WORLD EXAMPLE}\vspace{4pt}

\# You just finished a feature. Ship it:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcommit "add quantum flux capacitor"\\
\end{codebox}

\# Stages everything, commits with message, pushes. \# One line. Done. Next feature.

\# Need to check your git config:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitconfig\\
\end{codebox}

\# Opens it in \$\textbf{EDITOR}. Fix, save, done.

Git verbs follow the pattern: git + action noun. If you can name it, zpwr probably has a verb for it.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{GitHub Integration}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} GITHUB INTEGRATION}\vspace{6pt}

GitHub is not a website. It is an API with a website. \textbf{ZPWR} treats it accordingly.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CREATE AND DESTROY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr githubcreate      \# create a remote GitHub repo\\
zpwr hc               \# same thing, two letters\\
\end{codebox}

Creates a repo on GitHub from your current directory. Sets up the remote. Ready to push.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr githubdelete      \# delete a remote GitHub repo\\
zpwr hd               \# same thing, two letters\\
\end{codebox}

This is permanent. It asks for confirmation. But once confirmed, the repo is gone. Poof.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BROWSE YOUR GITHUB}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr github            \# open your GitHub profile in browser\\
zpwr gh               \# same thing\\
zpwr mygithub          \# same thing\\
\end{codebox}

Opens your profile page. Quick ego check.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr githubzpwr        \# open zpwr repo on GitHub\\
zpwr ghz              \# same thing\\
\end{codebox}

Jump straight to the zpwr source. File issues. Star it. You know you want to.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONTRIBUTION STATS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr githubcontribcount \# count GitHub contribs in last year\\
\end{codebox}

Tells you how green your contribution graph is. The number that keeps you pushing commits at 2 AM.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REVEAL: THE BROWSER SHORTCUT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reveal            \# show current repo in browser\\
\end{codebox}

You are in a git repo. You want to see it on GitHub. One word. Browser opens. You are there.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORKFLOW EXAMPLE}\vspace{4pt}

\# Start a new project from scratch:

\begin{codebox}
mkdir new-project \&\& cd new-project\\
git init\\
zpwr hc               \# repo created on GitHub\\
zpwr gitcommit "initial commit"\\
zpwr reveal            \# admire it in the browser\\
\end{codebox}

From zero to pushed repo in four commands. That is the GitHub integration promise.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Git Archaeology}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} GIT ARCHAEOLOGY}\vspace{6pt}

The past is not dead. It is not even past. It is stored in .git/objects and zpwr can dig it up.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SEARCHING THE LOG}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitsearch             \# search for regex in git log\\
\end{codebox}

Uses fzf to grep through your entire commit history. That one commit where you "fixed the thing"? Found.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcommits            \# search git commits with fzf\\
\end{codebox}

Interactive commit browser. Preview diffs inline. Like git log but you can actually find things.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COUNTING COMMITS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcommitcount        \# total number of commits\\
\end{codebox}

Just a number. But what a number. The sum total of your decisions in this repo.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONTRIBUTION ANALYSIS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcontribcount       \# contributions by author\\
\end{codebox}

Who committed the most? The leaderboard of blame.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcontribcountdirs   \# contributions across dirs\\
\end{codebox}

Same analysis but across multiple repositories. Useful for org-wide contribution audits.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcontribcountlines  \# lines contributed by author\\
\end{codebox}

Lines of code per author. The metric that lies to you. But it is a fun lie.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SIZE ANALYSIS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gittotallines         \# total line count of git files\\
\end{codebox}

How big is this repo, really? Now you know.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitlargest            \# largest files in git history\\
\end{codebox}

Shows the biggest blobs stored by git, descending. That 50MB binary someone committed in 2019? There it is. Now you know what to purge (see page 12).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DIG SESSION EXAMPLE}\vspace{4pt}

\# Who wrote the most code in this repo?

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcontribcount\\
\end{codebox}

\# What is eating all the disk space?

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitlargest\\
\end{codebox}

\# Find that commit about the database migration:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitsearch\\
\end{codebox}

\# Type "migration" in fzf. There it is.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Git Surgery}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} GIT SURGERY}\vspace{6pt}

Sometimes you need to rewrite history. Not the geopolitical kind. The git kind. These are destructive operations. Use with intent.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CLEARING THE CACHE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitclearcache     \# clear old git refs and objects\\
\end{codebox}

Runs garbage collection on git's internal state. Prunes unreachable objects, packs loose refs. Your .git directory gets a deep clean.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REMOVING COMMITS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitclearcommit    \# remove matching commits from history\\
\end{codebox}

This is filter-branch territory. Rewrites history. Force push required after. Use when someone committed passwords, API keys, or that embarrassing commented-out code block.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REMOVING FILES FROM ALL HISTORY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitclearfile      \# rm file from all git refs and objects\\
\end{codebox}

Nuclear option for files. That 200MB video someone committed in 2020? This removes it from every commit that ever existed. Your repo size drops. Your clone time drops. Your blood pressure drops.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EMAIL SURGERY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitemail          \# change email with git filter-branch\\
\end{codebox}

Changes both author and committer email across history.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitaemail         \# change author email only\\
\end{codebox}

When you committed with your personal email at work.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitcemail         \# change committer email only\\
\end{codebox}

When the committer field has the wrong identity.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHEN TO USE THESE}\vspace{4pt}

Scenario: you committed with wrong@email.com.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitemail\\
\end{codebox}

\# Follow the prompts to fix every commit.

Scenario: .env file in git history with secrets.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitclearfile\\
\end{codebox}

\# Purges it from every ref and object.

REMEMBER: These rewrite history. Coordinate with your team before force pushing. Or do not coordinate and watch the chaos unfold.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Forgit: Git on Steroids}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FORGIT: GIT ON STEROIDS}\vspace{6pt}

Forgit wraps common git operations with fzf previews. Every action gets a live preview pane. Every choice is informed. No more blind git commands.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STAGING AND UNSTAGING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitadd            \# fzf-powered git add\\
\end{codebox}

Shows unstaged files with inline diffs in the preview. Select files with tab. Enter to stage. Visual staging.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitreset          \# fzf-powered git reset HEAD\\
\end{codebox}

The reverse of forgitadd. Unstage files visually.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VIEWING CHANGES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitdiff           \# fzf-powered git diff viewer\\
\end{codebox}

Browse changed files. Preview shows colorized diffs. Side-by-side comparison in your terminal.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitlog            \# fzf-powered git log\\
\end{codebox}

Commit history with preview. See the diff for each commit without leaving the log view. Beautiful.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STASH OPERATIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitstash          \# fzf-powered stash browser\\
\end{codebox}

Lists stashes. Preview shows what each stash contains. Pick one, apply it. No more "was it stash@\{3\}?"


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CLEANUP}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitclean          \# fzf-powered git clean\\
\end{codebox}

Shows untracked files. Select which to delete. Preview before you destroy. Civilized cleanup.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitrestore        \# fzf-powered git restore\\
\end{codebox}

Discard changes to selected files. With preview.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GITIGNORE MANAGEMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitignore         \# browse gitignore templates\\
zpwr forgitignoreget      \# download a gitignore template\\
zpwr forgitignorelist     \# list available templates\\
zpwr forgitignoreupdate   \# update gitignore templates\\
zpwr forgitignoreclean    \# clean gitignore cache\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXTRAS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitinfo           \# show git repo information\\
zpwr forgitwarn           \# show forgit warnings\\
\end{codebox}

Forgit turns git into a visual experience. Every forgit* verb is a regular git command with eyes.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Git Battalion Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} GIT BATTALION OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

One repo is easy. Twenty repos is a war. These verbs let you run commands across all of them.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE GENERAL: FORALLDIR}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitforalldir <cmd>   \# run cmd in ALL git dirs\\
\end{codebox}

Walks every git directory it can find and runs your command inside each one. The nuclear option.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CACHED REPOS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitreposexec <cmd>    \# run cmd in cached git dirs\\
\end{codebox}

Uses the cached list of known repos. Faster than foralldir because it does not rescan the filesystem.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CLEAN VS DIRTY TARGETING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitreposcleanexec <cmd>       \# refresh + run in clean repos\\
zpwr gitreposcleancacheexec <cmd>  \# run in cached clean repos\\
zpwr gitreposdirtyexec <cmd>       \# refresh + run in dirty repos\\
zpwr gitreposdirtycacheexec <cmd>  \# run in cached dirty repos\\
\end{codebox}

Clean repos have no uncommitted changes. Dirty repos have pending work. Target your commands accordingly.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MASS UPDATE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitupdatefordir      \# run git updates in all git dirs\\
\end{codebox}

Pulls latest, updates submodules, the full sync. Run this on a Monday morning. Coffee. Wait.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BRANCH WIPE OPERATIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitfordirmain        \# wipe to main branch in git dirs\\
zpwr gitfordirdevelop     \# wipe to develop branch in git dirs\\
\end{codebox}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitzfordir <branch> [z]       \# wipe to any branch + z jump\\
zpwr gitzfordirmain [z]            \# z jump + wipe to main\\
zpwr gitzfordirdevelop [z]         \# z jump + wipe to develop\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE REPOS FILE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitreposfile         \# edit the git repos file\\
\end{codebox}

This file lists all known repositories. Edit it to add or remove repos from batch operations.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXAMPLE: STATUS CHECK ALL REPOS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitreposexec git status -s\\
\end{codebox}

Runs git status -s in every cached repo. Instantly see which repos have uncommitted work.

Battalion ops turn you from a developer into a fleet commander.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{THE SEARCH ENGINE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/figlet_scan.png}
\end{center}

\section{Finding Files}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FINDING FILES}\vspace{6pt}

The filesystem is vast. You are small. But you have fzf and a verb for every search strategy.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILESEARCH: LOCAL TREE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr filesearch          \# find and open a file in a sub dir\\
\end{codebox}

Recursively lists files under \$\textbf{PWD} into fzf. Type fragments of the filename. Fuzzy match. Enter. Opens the file in your pager.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr filesearchedit      \# same but puts path on command line\\
\end{codebox}

Instead of opening immediately, the selected path lands on your \$BUFFER for further editing. Append commands, pipe it, whatever you want.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DIRSEARCH: DIRECTORIES ONLY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr dirsearch           \# cd to a sub directory via fzf\\
\end{codebox}

Same idea but only directories. Pick one, land in it. Faster than cd-tab-tab-tab-tab.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FINDSEARCH: FULL DISK}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr findsearch          \# find across the entire drive\\
\end{codebox}

Uses find(1) to search the whole filesystem. Results feed into fzf. Pick one, open it. Slow on first run. Worth the wait.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr findsearchedit      \# same but result goes to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LOCATESEARCH: INDEX-BASED}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr locatesearch        \# search the locate database\\
\end{codebox}

Uses locate(1) which searches a pre-built index. Blazing fast. But only as fresh as your last updatedb.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr locatesearchedit    \# same but result goes to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SEARCH HIERARCHY}\vspace{4pt}

Speed    Scope          Verb -----    -----          ---- Fast ... current tree .. filesearch Fast ... dirs only ..... dirsearch Slow ... entire disk ... findsearch Instant  indexed disk .. locatesearch

Start with filesearch for local work. Escalate to locatesearch when you need the whole disk. Fall back to findsearch when the index is stale.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Searching Content}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} SEARCHING CONTENT}\vspace{6pt}

Finding files is one thing. Finding the line inside the file? That is the real game.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORDSEARCH: CONTENT IN LOCAL TREE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr wordsearch          \# find file by content in sub dir\\
\end{codebox}

Greps through files in the current tree. Results show matching lines with filenames. Fzf preview shows the file with match highlighted. Select one, jump straight to it.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr wordsearchedit      \# same but result goes to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}

Puts the selected file path on your command line. Useful when you want to pipe or chain commands.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GREP: AG INTO FZF}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr grep               \# grep through pwd with ag into fzf\\
\end{codebox}

This is the heavy artillery. Uses the silver searcher (ag) piped into fzf for interactive filtering. Respects .gitignore. Skips binaries. Fast.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILESEARCH VS WORDSEARCH}\vspace{4pt}

Know the filename?     Use filesearch Know the content?      Use wordsearch Know nothing?          Use grep and improvise.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE EDIT SUFFIX PATTERN}\vspace{4pt}

Every search verb has an edit variant: filesearch     -> filesearchedit wordsearch     -> wordsearchedit findsearch     -> findsearchedit locatesearch   -> locatesearchedit.

Without edit: opens the result immediately. With edit: puts the path on your command line. This lets you compose: vim \$(zpwr filesearchedit)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REAL-WORLD EXAMPLES}\vspace{4pt}

\# Find where "\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}" is defined:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr wordsearch\\
\end{codebox}

\# Type \textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS} in fzf, browse the matches.

\# Quick grep for TODO comments:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr grep\\
\end{codebox}

\# Type TODO, see every file that has one.

The search verbs are your eyes inside the codebase. Use them before you reach for a GUI.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The FZF Dimension}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE FZF DIMENSION}\vspace{6pt}

FZF is the nervous system of zpwr. It is wired into search, selection, navigation, git, vim, environment browsing, and verb dispatch. If zpwr has a UI, fzf IS that UI.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VERB DISCOVERY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr verbs     \# fuzzy search all zpwr verbs\\
\end{codebox}

Every verb, every description, in one fzf window. This is the god-mode cheat sheet. Learn it first.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVIRONMENT BROWSING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envaccept          \# accept from fzf env\\
\end{codebox}

Browse all shell environment entries (aliases, functions, parameters, builtins) in fzf. Select one, accept it onto the command line.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envedit            \# edit from fzf env\\
\end{codebox}

Same browsing but puts the result on \$BUFFER for editing.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envcachesearch     \# search cached aliases, functions, etc.\\
\end{codebox}

Searches the pre-built cache of your entire environment. Faster than envedit because it uses the cache file.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF PATTERNS ACROSS \textbf{ZPWR}}\vspace{4pt}

FZF appears in dozens of verbs. The pattern is always: 1. A data source generates lines (files, commits, dirs) 2. Lines pipe into fzf with a preview command 3. You fuzzy-filter, select, and take an action.

Verbs with fzf built in: filesearch     file list -> fzf -> open wordsearch     grep results -> fzf -> open dirsearch      dir list -> fzf -> cd gitcommits     git log -> fzf -> show diff forgitadd      unstaged files -> fzf -> stage forgitlog      commit log -> fzf -> preview zcd            z database -> fzf -> cd subcommandsfzf all verbs -> fzf -> execute.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FZF KEYBINDINGS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimsearch          \# search vim keybindings in fzf\\
zpwr allsearch          \# search ALL keybindings in fzf\\
\end{codebox}

When you want to find a keybinding but do not remember where it is defined. Search them all.

FZF is not optional in zpwr. It is the interface. Master fzf and you master zpwr.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{EDITOR MASTERY}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_neural_link.png}
\end{center}

\section{Vim Integration Overview}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM INTEGRATION OVERVIEW}\vspace{6pt}

Vim is not just an editor in zpwr. It is a first-class citizen with its own verb family. Over 20 verbs, each opening vim in a specific context.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PHILOSOPHY}\vspace{4pt}

Every vim verb answers one question: "What do I want to edit right now?" The answer is always one verb away.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE vim* VERB FAMILY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
vimall .............. open ALL zpwr files (for :argdo)\\
vimalledit .......... pick and edit 1+ zpwr configs\\
vimautoload ......... open all autoloads (for :argdo)\\
vimcd ............... vim edit, then cd to first dir\\
vimconfig ........... edit all zpwr config files\\
vimctags ............ vim zpwr ctags\\
vimemacsconfig ...... vim edit emacs zpwr configs\\
vimfilesearch ....... vim a file found via fzf\\
vimfilesearchedit ... vim edit a file found via fzf\\
vimfindsearch ....... vim a file from find(1) via fzf\\
vimfindsearchedit ... vim edit find result\\
vimgtags ............ vim zpwr GNU global tags\\
vimgtagsedit ........ vim edit zpwr GNU global tags\\
vimlocatesearch ..... vim a file from locate via fzf\\
vimlocatesearchedit . vim edit locate result\\
vimplugincount ...... count vim plugins\\
vimpluginlist ....... list all vim plugins\\
vimrecent ........... edit most recent files\\
vimrecentcd ......... cd + edit most recent files\\
vimrecentsudo ....... sudo edit most recent files\\
vimrecentsudocd ..... cd + sudo edit recent files\\
vimscripts .......... open all zpwr scripts (for :argdo)\\
vimscriptedit ....... pick and edit 1+ scripts\\
vimsearch ........... search vim keybindings via fzf\\
vimtests ............ edit all zpwr tests\\
vimtokens ........... vim the .tokens.sh file\\
vimwordsearch ....... vim a file found by content\\
vimwordsearchedit ... vim edit file found by content\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE :argdo PATTERN}\vspace{4pt}

Verbs like vimall and vimscripts load many files into vim's arglist. Then you can:

\begin{codebox}
:argdo \%s/old/new/g | update\\
\end{codebox}

Mass find-and-replace across the entire zpwr codebase.

If it can be edited, there is a vim verb for it.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Vim File Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM FILE OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

Opening vim is easy. Opening vim with the right files already loaded? That is where zpwr earns its keep.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE BIG OPENS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimall             \# load ALL zpwr files into arglist\\
\end{codebox}

Every .zsh, .sh, .py, .pl file in the zpwr tree. Hundreds of files. Ready for :argdo mass operations. This is the tactical nuke of text editing.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimalledit         \# fzf pick 1+ configs to edit\\
\end{codebox}

Like vimall but you choose which files to open. Surgical precision instead of carpet bombing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RECENT FILES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimrecent          \# edit most recently opened files\\
\end{codebox}

Pulls from vim's file history. Opens the files you were just working on. Resume where you left off.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimrecentcd        \# cd to dir + open recent files\\
\end{codebox}

Same as vimrecent but also changes to the directory. Your shell and editor are now in the same place.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimrecentsudo      \# sudo edit recent files\\
\end{codebox}

When you need root to edit those files.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimrecentsudocd    \# cd + sudo edit recent files\\
\end{codebox}

The full combo: navigate, elevate, edit.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} SUBSYSTEMS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimscripts         \# load all zpwr scripts into arglist\\
zpwr vimscriptedit      \# pick and edit 1+ scripts\\
zpwr vimautoload        \# load all autoloads into arglist\\
zpwr vimtests           \# edit all zpwr test files\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VIM CD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimcd              \# edit file, cd to its directory\\
\end{codebox}

Opens a file in vim, and when you exit, your shell is cd'd to the directory containing that file. Edit something, then be where it lives. Elegant.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORKFLOW EXAMPLE}\vspace{4pt}

\# Resume yesterday's work:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimrecentcd\\
\end{codebox}

\# Opens recent files, lands you in the right dir.

\# Mass rename a function across all scripts:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimscripts\\
:argdo \%s/oldFunc/newFunc/g | update\\
\end{codebox}

\# Done. Every script updated.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Vim Search and Tags}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM SEARCH AND TAGS}\vspace{6pt}

Finding files is step one. Finding definitions inside those files is step two. Tags are step three: jump to any symbol by name.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VIM FILE SEARCH}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimfilesearch          \# fzf find file -> open in vim\\
\end{codebox}

Lists files in the current tree via fzf. Select one, it opens in vim. No path typing.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimfilesearchedit      \# fzf find file -> path to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}

Same search but puts the path on your command line.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VIM WORD SEARCH}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimwordsearch          \# fzf grep content -> open in vim\\
\end{codebox}

Searches file contents, shows matches in fzf. Select a match, vim opens at that location.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimwordsearchedit      \# same but path to BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FULL DISK SEARCH INTO VIM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimfindsearch          \# find(1) -> fzf -> vim\\
zpwr vimfindsearchedit      \# find(1) -> fzf -> BUFFER\\
zpwr vimlocatesearch        \# locate -> fzf -> vim\\
zpwr vimlocatesearchedit    \# locate -> fzf -> BUFFER\\
\end{codebox}

The search escalation ladder from page 15 but with vim as the final destination instead of a pager.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CTAGS: JUMP TO DEFINITION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimctags               \# open zpwr ctags in vim\\
\end{codebox}

Ctags indexes function and variable definitions. With tags loaded, Ctrl-] on any symbol jumps to its definition. Ctrl-T jumps back. Code navigation at the speed of thought.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GNU GLOBAL TAGS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimgtags               \# open zpwr GNU global tags in vim\\
zpwr vimgtagsedit           \# edit zpwr GNU global tags\\
\end{codebox}

GNU Global is ctags on steroids. It indexes not just definitions but references too. Find every caller of a function, not just its definition.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SEARCH MATRIX}\vspace{4pt}

What you know       Verb to use -------------       ----------- Filename fragment   vimfilesearch String in a file    vimwordsearch File somewhere      vimlocatesearch Symbol name         vimctags then Ctrl-] All references      vimgtags.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Vim Config and Sessions}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM CONFIG AND SESSIONS}\vspace{6pt}

Configuration is not a one-time event. It is a lifestyle. \textbf{ZPWR} gives you session commands that open the right config files as a group.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SESSION VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

Each of these opens a curated set of config files in vim buffers, ready for synchronized editing:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vrc          \# vim/nvim config session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens your vimrc or init.vim along with related files. All your editor settings in one session.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr brc          \# shell aliases session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens the tokens file and shell alias configs. Where you tune your personal shell experience.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zrc          \# zshrc session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens the zshrc and zsh init chain. The root of your shell's boot sequence.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr trc          \# tmux.conf session\\
\end{codebox}

Opens tmux configuration files. Pane layouts, keybindings, status bar.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONFIG EDITING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimconfig    \# edit all zpwr configs in vim\\
\end{codebox}

Opens every zpwr configuration file. The master session. Use :bn and :bp to navigate between buffers.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimtokens   \# vim the .tokens.sh file\\
zpwr tokens      \# same thing\\
\end{codebox}

Quick jump to your personal aliases and secrets file. This is the file that is gitignored. Your sanctuary.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PLUGIN INVENTORY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimplugincount  \# how many vim plugins installed\\
zpwr vimpluginlist   \# list all vim plugins\\
\end{codebox}

Know thy plugins. Count them. Name them. If the count scares you, it should.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TYPICAL CONFIG WORKFLOW}\vspace{4pt}

\# Add a new shell alias:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr brc\\
\end{codebox}

\# Edit .tokens.sh, add your alias, :wq \# Source it or open a new terminal.

\# Tweak a vim keybinding:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vrc\\
\end{codebox}

\# Navigate to the mapping, change it, :wq.

Three letters to open any config. No file paths. No guessing. Just intent.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Emacs Mirror Universe}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EMACS MIRROR UNIVERSE}\vspace{6pt}

For every vim verb, there is an emacs verb. Identical semantics. Different editor. Perfect symmetry. \textbf{ZPWR} does not take sides in the editor war. It arms both factions equally.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SYMMETRY TABLE}\vspace{4pt}

vim verb              emacs verb --------              ----------

\begin{codebox}
vimall .............. emacsall\\
vimalledit .......... emacsalledit\\
vimautoload ......... emacsautoload\\
vimcd ............... emacscd\\
vimconfig ........... emacsconfig\\
vimctags ............ emacsctags\\
vimfilesearch ....... emacsfilesearch\\
vimfilesearchedit ... emacsfilesearchedit\\
vimfindsearch ....... emacsfindsearch\\
vimfindsearchedit ... emacsfindsearchedit\\
vimgtags ............ emacsgtags\\
vimgtagsedit ........ emacsgtagsedit\\
vimlocatesearch ..... emacslocatesearch\\
vimlocatesearchedit . emacslocatesearchedit\\
vimrecent ........... emacsrecent\\
vimrecentcd ......... emacsrecentcd\\
vimrecentsudo ....... emacsrecentsudo\\
vimrecentsudocd ..... emacsrecentsudocd\\
vimscripts .......... emacsscripts\\
vimscriptedit ....... emacsscriptedit\\
vimtests ............ emacstests\\
vimtokens ........... emacstokens\\
vimwordsearch ....... emacswordsearch\\
vimwordsearchedit ... emacswordsearchedit\\
vimplugincount ...... emacsplugincount\\
vimpluginlist ....... emacspluginlist\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EMACS-SPECIFIC}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsallserver       \# emacs all zpwr files (server mode)\\
zpwr emacsemacsconfig     \# emacs edit emacs-specific configs\\
zpwr emacszpwr            \# emacs the zpwr directory\\
\end{codebox}

The lesson: zpwr is editor-agnostic by design. Replace "vim" with "emacs" in any verb. It works. Both sides get the same firepower.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{PERFORMANCE CULT}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_bench_help.png}
\end{center}

\section{Benchmarking Startup}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_bench_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} BENCHMARKING STARTUP}\vspace{6pt}

A shell that takes 2 seconds to start is a shell that has already lost. \textbf{ZPWR} takes startup time seriously enough to build a benchmarking verb.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE BENCH VERB}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr bench\\
\end{codebox}

Runs multiple shell startup iterations and reports: - Mean startup time - Percentiles (p50, p95, p99) - Baseline diff (zpwr overhead vs bare zsh)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT THE NUMBERS MEAN}\vspace{4pt}

< 100ms .... imperceptible. You are golden. 100-200ms .. noticeable if you open many terminals. 200-500ms .. getting sluggish. Time to investigate. > 500ms .... something is wrong. Check your plugins.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

The bench verb spawns N fresh zsh instances, each running through the full init chain. It measures wall-clock time from fork to prompt. Then it does the same for bare zsh (no zpwr). The diff tells you exactly what zpwr costs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT SLOWS YOU DOWN}\vspace{4pt}

Common culprits in order of likelihood: 1. Too many vim/emacs plugins loading at shell init 2. Nvm/pyenv/rbenv hooking into every prompt 3. Uncompiled completion files (run zpwr compile) 4. Network calls in your init chain (stop that) 5. Massive \textbf{HISTFILE} being loaded synchronously.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} OPTIMIZATION CHECKLIST}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr compile        \# recompile all cache and completions\\
zpwr compilefpath    \# recompile all fpath files\\
zpwr compilefiles    \# recompile all files\\
\end{codebox}

Compiled .zwc files load faster than source. This is the single biggest performance lever.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BENCHMARK REGULARLY}\vspace{4pt}

Run zpwr bench after any config change. If the numbers go up, you broke something. If they go down, you are a performance wizard.

Your shell starts thousands of times a year. Every millisecond is multiplied by every terminal open. Measure. Optimize. Measure again.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Flame Charts}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_flame_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FLAME CHARTS}\vspace{6pt}

Bench tells you HOW LONG startup takes. Flame tells you WHERE the time goes.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FLAME VERB}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr flame\\
\end{codebox}

Generates a startup flame chart from zprof data. Shows you exactly which functions and files are consuming your startup time, in descending order.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} READING THE OUTPUT}\vspace{4pt}

The flame chart shows a ranked list of functions: Each entry shows: - Function/file name - Time consumed (milliseconds) - Percentage of total startup time.

The top of the list is where optimization effort pays off the most. If one function takes 40\% of startup, that is your target.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW ZPROF WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh has a built-in profiler: zprof. When enabled, it instruments every function call during shell init and records the wall-clock time. The flame verb wraps this into a readable report.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMMON HOTSPOTS}\vspace{4pt}

What you will typically see at the top: compinit ........... completion system init compaudit .......... completion security check compdump ........... writing completion cache \_zpwr\_* ............ zpwr autoload functions nvm/pyenv/rbenv .... version manager hooks


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} OPTIMIZATION TIPS}\vspace{4pt}

1. If compinit is slow, run zpwr compile to cache it 2. If a version manager dominates, lazy-load it 3. If an autoload is slow, check for disk I/O in it 4. If compaudit is slow, fix the directory permissions 5. After changes, run zpwr bench to verify improvement


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE OPTIMIZATION LOOP}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr flame      \# identify the bottleneck\\
\end{codebox}

... fix it ...

\begin{codebox}
zpwr bench      \# measure the improvement\\
zpwr flame      \# find the next bottleneck\\
\end{codebox}

Repeat until you hit diminishing returns.

Flame charts turn gut feelings into data. Stop guessing. Start profiling.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Live Dashboard}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_bench_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LIVE DASHBOARD}\vspace{6pt}

Bench is a snapshot. Flame is a profile. Top is a live feed of your shell's vital signs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE TOP VERB}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr top\\
\end{codebox}

Launches a live shell resource dashboard. Think htop, but for your zsh environment.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT EACH SECTION SHOWS}\vspace{4pt}

Shell Environment - Number of aliases currently defined - Number of functions loaded - Number of parameters (variables) in scope - Builtin and keyword counts.

Completion System - Completion functions registered - Cache file sizes and freshness.

Resource Usage - Memory footprint of the shell process - History file size and entry count - Autoload function count.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INTERPRETING THE DATA}\vspace{4pt}

High alias count?    Normal for zpwr. 1000+ is fine. High function count? Also normal. Autoloaded functions are lazy-loaded, they cost nothing until called. Large \textbf{HISTFILE}?      Consider zpwr backuphistory and trim. Stale cache?         Run zpwr compile to refresh.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMPANION VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcounts   \# one-shot environment stats\\
zpwr autoloadcount       \# total number of autoloads\\
zpwr autoloadlist        \# list all autoloads\\
zpwr vimplugincount      \# count vim plugins\\
zpwr emacsplugincount    \# count emacs plugins\\
\end{codebox}

Use these for quick spot checks without the full dashboard.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PERFORMANCE QUARTET}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr bench    -> how fast is total startup? zpwr flame    -> ASCII flame chart of function hotspots zpwr startup  -> per-function zprof breakdown zpwr top      -> what is loaded right now?

Four verbs. Four perspectives. Complete observability. Your shell is no longer a black box. It is an open book.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{HEALTH \& DIAGNOSTICS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_doctor_help.png}
\end{center}

\section{The Doctor Is In (1/2)}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_doctor_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE DOCTOR IS IN}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // every system needs a physician. yours lives at zpwr doctor. //}

Your shell is a living organism. It accumulates cruft, grows tumors in \textbf{PATH}, and occasionally develops broken symlinks. The doctor runs a full-body scan and tells you what hurts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INVOKE THE DOCTOR}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr doctor              \# full diagnostic scan\\
zpwr doctor -h           \# help with detailed check list\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT IT CHECKS}\vspace{4pt}

1. STALE .zwc FILES Compiled zsh files older than their source. Fix: zpwr recompile

2. ZCOMPDUMP FRESHNESS Completion dump older than 1 week. Fix: zpwr regenzsh

3. DUPLICATE \textbf{PATH} ENTRIES Every duplicate is a wasted hash lookup. Fix: zpwr deduppaths

4. DUPLICATE/MISSING \textbf{FPATH} ENTRIES Same disease, different organ.

5. BROKEN SYMLINKS Dangling symlinks in \$\textbf{ZPWR}.

Continued on next page...


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The Doctor Is In (2/2)}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_doctor_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE DOCTOR (continued)}\vspace{6pt}

6. NAMED DIRECTORIES Invalid named dirs and p10k pollution from auto\_name\_dirs leaking gitstatus vars.

7. DEPENDENCIES Checks: git, eza, fzf, tmux, perl, python3, zinit.

8. HISTORY \& BACKUPS Is \textbf{HISTFILE} present? How many backups exist?

9. CONFIG SYMLINKS Checks .zshrc, .vimrc, .tmux.conf are symlinks.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} READING THE RESULTS}\vspace{4pt}

Green checkmark  = healthy yellow warning   = degraded but functional red X            = broken, fix now.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SIGNAL BAR}\vspace{4pt}

\#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#  = HEALTHY \#\#\#\#\#\#----  = WARNINGS \#\#--------  = CRITICAL.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMPANION VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr stale                \# stale .zwc, orphan caches\\
zpwr pathaudit             \# deep PATH/FPATH audit\\
zpwr lint                  \# shellcheck + zsh -n\\
zpwr startup               \# zprof breakdown\\
zpwr ports                 \# listening ports\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRO MOVE}\vspace{4pt}

Add zpwr doctor to weekly cron. Catch rot early.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Dependency Cartography}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_deps_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DEPENDENCY CARTOGRAPHY}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // mapping the call graph. who calls whom. who dies alone. //}

zpwr has hundreds of functions. They call each other in a tangled web of dependencies. The deps verb maps that web. Think of it as Google Maps for your function call graph.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BASIC USAGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr deps                          \# summary overview\\
zpwr deps zpwrClearList             \# what does zpwrClearList call?\\
zpwr deps -h                        \# full help\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

-r, --reverse    Who calls this function? (reverse lookup) -o, --orphans    Functions called by nobody internally -s, --summary    Overview stats (default when no args) -d, --dot        Output dot format for graphviz rendering


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORWARD LOOKUP: WHAT DOES X CALL?}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr deps zpwrDoctor\\
\end{codebox}

Shows every function zpwrDoctor calls, plus depth-2 callees. Output:  zpwrDoctor -> zpwrPrettyPrint -> [zpwrLogDebug, ...]


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} REVERSE LOOKUP: WHO CALLS X?}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr deps -r zpwrPrettyPrint\\
\end{codebox}

Answers: "Who depends on zpwrPrettyPrint?" If the answer is 30 functions, that function is load-bearing. Touch it carefully.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ORPHAN DETECTION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr deps --orphans\\
\end{codebox}

Lists functions that no other zpwr function calls. These are either entry points (user-invoked) or dead code. Orphans are not necessarily bad -- many are top-level verbs or zinit hooks. But if you wrote zpwrFoo and nobody calls it, maybe zpwrFoo was a fever dream.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GRAPHVIZ EXPORT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr deps --dot > graph.dot\\
dot -Tsvg graph.dot -o graph.svg\\
\end{codebox}

Produces a visual dependency graph. Nodes are functions, edges are call relationships. The dot output uses cyberpunk colors: dark background, cyan nodes, magenta edges. Open graph.svg in a browser and behold the nervous system.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SUMMARY VIEW}\vspace{4pt}

With no args, deps shows two ranked lists: TOP 10 MOST DEPENDENCIES (functions that call the most others) TOP 10 MOST DEPENDED ON  (functions called by the most others) Plus stats: total functions, orphan count, total call edges. The bar charts use Unicode block characters. Very aesthetic.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

Scans autoload dirs: common, darwin, linux, comp\_utils. Reads each function body and string-matches against all other function names. Also scans \textbf{\$ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/lib.sh.} It is O(n\textasciicircum{}2) and proud of it. Your function count is not big enough to matter.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Command Autopsy}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_trace_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} COMMAND AUTOPSY}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // when you need to know why a command is slow, fat, or dead //}

The trace verb is a command profiler. It wraps any command in instrumentation and reports wall time, CPU time, memory, context switches, page faults, and more.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BASIC USAGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr trace ls -la                   \# full trace of ls -la\\
zpwr trace -q make                  \# quick: timing + resources only\\
zpwr trace -h                       \# full help\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

-q, --quick      Timing + resource stats only (runs once) -s, --strace     Syscall counts only (Linux; no ltrace) -l, --ltrace     C library calls only (Linux; no strace) -a, --all        Both strace + ltrace (default behavior)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT YOU GET: THE RESOURCE CARD}\vspace{4pt}

Exit code          green = 0, red = nonzero wall time          real clock time with rating: INSTANT (<100ms), FAST (<1s), NOMINAL (<5s), SLOW (<30s), HEAVY (30s+) user/sys time      CPU time in user/kernel space peak RSS           max resident memory (auto-scaled B/KB/MB/GB) ctx switches       voluntary + involuntary context switches page reclaims      soft faults (memory mapped in from cache) page faults        hard faults (actual disk I/O for memory) block I/O          filesystem reads and writes signals recv       number of signals delivered IPC messages       sent/received inter-process messages shell delta        memory change of the parent shell child procs        before/after child process count.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PLATFORM DIFFERENCES}\vspace{4pt}

MacOS:  \textbf{\textbf{/usr/bin/time}} -l for resource stats. SIP blocks dtruss/dtrace on most binaries. You get timing + memory + kernel stats. Linux:  strace -c for syscall counts, ltrace -c for C library calls. Command re-runs for each tool.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRACTICAL EXAMPLES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr trace git status              \# is git status slow in this repo?\\
zpwr trace -q rm -rf node\_modules  \# quick mode for destructive ops\\
zpwr trace -s npm install          \# syscalls only (Linux)\\
zpwr trace zsh -c 'source \textasciitilde{}/.zshrc' \# profile shell startup\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SIGNAL BAR}\vspace{4pt}

Bottom of output: a 10-block performance signal. \#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#\#  score 10 = under 100ms. Lightning. \#\#\#\#\#\#----  score 6  = under 2s. Acceptable. \#\#--------  score 2  = under 30s. Go get coffee.

WARNING: Default mode re-runs the command for strace/ltrace. Use -q for destructive commands (rm, truncate, etc).


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Usage Intelligence}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} USAGE INTELLIGENCE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your shell keeps a diary. these verbs read it. //}

You have \$\{\#\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}\} verbs and thousands of aliases. Which ones do you actually use? Funcrank and aliasrank mine your shell history to answer that question.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FUNCRANK: FUNCTION \& VERB PROFILER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr funcrank                       \# top 20 functions and verbs\\
zpwr funcrank 40                    \# top 40\\
zpwr funcrank --reset               \# nuke cache, rebuild from scratch\\
zpwr funcrank --reset 50            \# rebuild then show top 50\\
\end{codebox}

Funcrank scans your \textbf{HISTFILE} for occurrences of known function names and zpwr verbs. It produces two ranked lists: 1. Top functions by history occurrence count 2. Top zpwr verbs by how often you type "zpwr <verb>" Each entry gets a Unicode bar chart. The biggest user wins.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ALIASRANK: REVERSE EXPANSION PROFILER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr aliasrank                      \# top 20 by expanded usage\\
zpwr aliasrank 50                   \# top 50\\
zpwr aliasrank --reset              \# force cache rebuild\\
\end{codebox}

Here is the trick: zpwr uses zsh-expand to expand aliases before they hit history. So history contains the expanded form, not the alias name. Aliasrank does a reverse lookup: it matches history lines against alias values to figure out which alias you would have typed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE CACHE SYSTEM}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr-aliasrank-cache.zsh}   command counts \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr-verbrank-cache.zsh}    verb counts \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr-rank-stamp}            history line watermark.

The cache auto-rebuilds when history grows by 1000+ lines. The watermark file stores the last-seen history line count. No need to rebuild every time -- incremental is the way. Use --reset to force a full rebuild if cache seems stale.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INTERPRETING THE RESULTS}\vspace{4pt}

If your top verb is help, you are still learning. Good. If your top verb is cleanall, you break things a lot. If your top function is cd, you need more named dirs. If an alias never appears, maybe it is too long to remember.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRACTICAL INTELLIGENCE}\vspace{4pt}

Run funcrank monthly. Notice patterns: - Verbs you never use might need better names or keybindings - Functions with high counts are candidates for optimization - Low-count zpwr verbs might benefit from shorter aliases.

Your history is a behavioral log. These verbs turn it into actionable intelligence. Know thyself, operative.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{ENVIRONMENT MASTERY}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_system_core.png}
\end{center}

\section{Know Your Shell}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} KNOW YOUR SHELL}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // you cannot master what you cannot measure //}

Three verbs to audit your environment. Each one reveals a different cross-section of the machine you live inside.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVVARS: THE \textbf{ZPWR} VARIABLES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envvars                        \# list all ZPWR\_* env vars\\
zpwr ev                              \# same thing, shorter\\
\end{codebox}

Shows every environment variable starting with \textbf{ZPWR}\_. These are the knobs and dials of the framework: paths, flags, plugin manager config, colorscheme, etc. If something is misconfigured, this is where you look.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVVARSALL: THE FULL PICTURE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envvarsall                     \# every env var, all of them\\
zpwr eva                             \# same thing, shorter\\
\end{codebox}

Dumps every exported variable. This includes \textbf{ZPWR}\_*, \textbf{PATH}, \textbf{HOME}, \textbf{EDITOR}, \textbf{LANG}, and everything else. Pipe it to fzf when you are hunting for a specific var:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr eva | fzf\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVIRONMENTCOUNTS: THE NUMBERS GAME}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcounts              \# full environment statistics\\
zpwr envcounts                      \# alias\\
zpwr e                               \# shortest form\\
\end{codebox}

This is the census. It counts everything loaded in your current shell session:

Aliases         regular + global + suffix aliases functions       autoloaded + defined functions builtins        zsh builtin commands parameters      shell variables (scalar, array, assoc) completions     registered completion functions modules         loaded zsh modules named dirs      hash -d entries \textbf{PATH} entries    directories in \textbf{PATH} \textbf{FPATH} entries   directories in \textbf{FPATH}

The numbers tell you how armed you are. A fresh zpwr install might show thousands of aliases, hundreds of functions, and many registered completions. If the numbers are low, something failed to load.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRO TIP: PAIR THEM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr doctor                   \# is anything broken?\\
zpwr e                         \# how much loaded?\\
zpwr ev                        \# are my zpwr vars right?\\
\end{codebox}

Run all three after installing or updating zpwr. They are your post-deployment smoke test.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The Option Matrix}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE OPTION MATRIX}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // zsh has 200+ options. most people know 5. time to fix that. //}

The zsh option system is a maze of boolean switches that control everything from globbing to history to error handling. These verbs help you navigate the labyrinth.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} OPTIONS: THE BOOLEAN SWITCHES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr options                        \# list all set options\\
\end{codebox}

Shows every zsh option that is currently ON. The important ones for zpwr: extendedglob      (\#, \textasciitilde{}, \textasciicircum{} in globs)  -- zpwr needs this autopushd         (cd pushes to dirstack) histignorealldups (no duplicate history) sharehistory      (history shared across sessions) completeinword    (tab complete mid-word) promptsubst       (parameter expansion in prompts)

If extendedglob is off, half of zpwr's glob tricks die. If sharehistory is off, your sessions are islands.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MODULES: THE LOADED EXTENSIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr modules                        \# list loaded zsh modules\\
\end{codebox}

Zsh modules are C libraries that add features: zsh/datetime      EPOCHREALTIME, strftime zsh/stat          zstat builtin for file metadata zsh/zutil         zparseopts for flag parsing zsh/complist       menuselect completion listing zsh/parameter      \$functions, \$aliases, \$parameters hashes zsh/terminfo       terminal capability strings.

If zsh/parameter is not loaded, zpwr env verbs break. If zsh/datetime is missing, timing-based verbs fail. Load a missing module: zmodload zsh/modulename.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ZSTYLE: THE FINE-TUNING KNOBS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zstyle                         \# list all zstyle settings\\
\end{codebox}

zstyle is zsh's configuration registry. It controls: :completion:*     how tab completion behaves :vcs\_info:*       git/svn info in prompts :zpwr:*           zpwr-specific settings.

Example styles zpwr sets: zstyle ':completion:*' menu select zstyle ':completion:*' matcher-list 'm:\{a-z\}=\{A-Z\}' The first enables arrow-key menu selection in tab complete. The second makes completion case-insensitive.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DETECTIVE WORK}\vspace{4pt}

Completions acting weird? Check:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr options    \# is completeinword on?\\
zpwr modules    \# is zsh/complist loaded?\\
zpwr zstyle     \# what completion styles are set?\\
\end{codebox}

The answer is usually hiding in one of these three.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Cache Search}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CACHE SEARCH}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the environment is a haystack. these are your magnets. //}

zpwr maintains an environment cache for fast lookup. These verbs let you search, inspect, and edit that cache without grepping through raw variable dumps.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVIRONMENTCACHESEARCH: FZF ENV NAVIGATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcachesearch         \# fzf through cached env\\
\end{codebox}

Opens an fzf interface over the environment cache. Type a few characters to filter. The cache includes: - All exported environment variables - \textbf{ZPWR}\_* configuration variables - \textbf{PATH} and \textbf{FPATH} entries - Named directory mappings.

This is faster than env | grep because it uses the pre-built cache file rather than querying the live env.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVACCEPT: ACCEPT A CACHE ENTRY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envaccept                      \# accept/apply cached env entry\\
\end{codebox}

After finding a variable in the cache, this verb applies it to your current session. Useful when the cache has a value you want but the live env does not.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ENVEDIT: EDIT THE ENVIRONMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envedit                        \# open env cache in EDITOR\\
\end{codebox}

Opens the environment cache file in your editor. Make changes, save, and the cache is updated. This is the surgical approach: when you know exactly which variable needs tweaking.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE CACHE LIFECYCLE}\vspace{4pt}

1. zpwr regenenvcache   builds the cache from live env 2. environmentcachesearch fuzzy-searches the cache 3. envaccept             applies an entry 4. envedit               manual edits

The cache lives at \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL}. It is regenerated during shell startup and when you run zpwr regenenvcache.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORKFLOW EXAMPLE}\vspace{4pt}

"What is \textbf{ZPWR\_EDITOR} set to?"

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcachesearch    \# type 'EDITOR' in fzf\\
\end{codebox}

Found it: \textbf{ZPWR\_EDITOR}=nvim.

"I want to change a bunch of \textbf{ZPWR}\_ vars."

\begin{codebox}
zpwr envedit                       \# opens in EDITOR\\
\end{codebox}

Edit the vars, save, done.

"Something is off, rebuild the cache."

\begin{codebox}
zpwr regenenvcache                 \# fresh cache from live env\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRO TIP}\vspace{4pt}

If you are debugging a missing env var, check two places: zpwr environmentcachesearch (cached) and zpwr eva (live). If cache has it but live does not, run zpwr regenenvcache.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Snapshots \& Time Travel}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/zpwr_snapshot_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} SNAPSHOTS \& TIME TRAVEL}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // save your universe. restore it when everything goes wrong. //}

The snapshot verb captures your entire terminal environment to a portable directory. The restore verb brings it back. This is git for your shell state.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TAKING A SNAPSHOT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr snapshot                       \# timestamp-named snapshot\\
zpwr snapshot myproject              \# named snapshot\\
zpwr snapshot before-refactor        \# meaningful name\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT GETS CAPTURED}\vspace{4pt}

Hash dirs         named directory mappings (hash -d) aliases           regular + global + suffix aliases env vars          all exported variables (secrets excluded) zpwr vars         \textbf{ZPWR\_VARS} associative array functions         user function count tracking history           full shell history dump tmux             sessions, windows, panes, resurrect state vim sessions      saved vim session files dir stack         current directory and pushd stack git status        branch, commit, dirty state of cwd.

Storage: \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/snapshots/}<name>/ Each snapshot is a directory of plain zsh files. Human-readable. Sourceable. Portable.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RESTORING A SNAPSHOT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr restore                        \# list available snapshots\\
zpwr restore myproject               \# restore everything\\
zpwr restore myproject aliases env    \# restore only aliases+env\\
zpwr restore myproject dirs           \# just cd to saved pwd\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SELECTIVE RESTORE COMPONENTS}\vspace{4pt}

All       everything (default when no component given) hash      named directories only aliases   regular, global, suffix aliases env       environment variables tmux      stage tmux-resurrect file (press prefix+Ctrl-r) vim       vim session files to \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.vim/sessions} history   merge snapshot history into current session dirs      cd to the saved working directory.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHEN TO USE SNAPSHOTS}\vspace{4pt}

Before a big update:     zpwr snapshot before-update Before experiments:      zpwr snapshot stable-state Switching projects:      zpwr snapshot projectA New machine setup:       copy snapshot dir, restore on new host.

NOTE: Sensitive vars (PASSWORD, SECRET, TOKEN, AWS\_SECRET*, GITHUB\_TOKEN) are automatically excluded from env snapshots. Your secrets stay secret.

History merge is additive -- it does not overwrite your current history. It uses fc -R to load entries.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{THE JANITOR'S CLOSET}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_override.png}
\end{center}

\section{Cleanup Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CLEANUP OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // entropy is the enemy. the janitor is your friend. //}

Shell environments accumulate garbage like a hoarder's attic. Cache files grow stale. Temp dirs fill up. Logs balloon. These verbs are the Marie Kondo of your \$\textbf{ZPWR}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CLEANALL: THE THERMONUCLEAR BROOM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
