\chapter{EMACS DIMENSION}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_neural_link.png}
\end{center}

\section{Emacs Integration Overview}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ENTERING THE EMACS DIMENSION}\vspace{6pt}

Where M-x is a way of life and pinky fingers go to die.

You thought there were only vim verbs? Think again, operative. \textbf{ZPWR} mirrors every vim operation with an emacs equivalent. Same power, different religion.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE 28 EMACS VERBS:}\vspace{4pt}

FILE OPS      emacsall, emacsalledit, emacsautoload RECENT        emacsrecent, emacsrecentcd, emacsrecentsudo SEARCH        emacsfilesearch, emacsfilesearchedit emacsfindsearch, emacsfindsearchedit emacslocatesearch, emacslocatesearchedit emacswordsearch, emacswordsearchedit TAGS          emacsctags, emacsgtags, emacsgtagsedit CONFIG        emacsconfig, emacsemacsconfig, emacstokens SERVER        emacsallserver SCRIPTS       emacsscripts, emacsscriptedit META          emacsplugincount, emacspluginlist, emacszpwr.

THE MIRROR PRINCIPLE:

Every vim verb has an emacs twin. The architecture is symmetrical by design. You can swap editors without losing any \textbf{ZPWR} functionality.

vim verb              emacs twin zpwr vimall     -->   zpwr emacsall zpwr vimrecent  -->   zpwr emacsrecent zpwr vimscripts -->   zpwr emacsscripts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PHILOSOPHY:}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} does not care about your editor war allegiance. It arms both sides equally. The real enemy is notepad.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the emacs dimension runs parallel to vim-space}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // choose your weapon, the framework adapts}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EDITOR} controls which universe you inhabit}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Emacs File Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EMACS FILE OPS: OPENING THINGS SINCE 1976}\vspace{6pt}

emacs: an operating system lacking only a decent editor.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsall}\vspace{4pt}

Opens all \textbf{ZPWR} framework files in emacs via fzf. Every autoload, script, config -- the whole kingdom.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsall              \# browse all files\\
zpwr emacsalledit          \# same but editable\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsrecent}\vspace{4pt}

Opens recently edited files. Emacs remembers where you have been, like a browser history for code.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsrecent            \# fzf recent files\\
zpwr emacsrecentcd          \# cd to recent dir\\
zpwr emacsrecentsudo        \# recent with sudo\\
zpwr emacsrecentsudocd      \# sudo + cd combo\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsscripts}\vspace{4pt}

Browse and open \textbf{ZPWR} scripts directory. Where the shell scripts live and breathe.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsscripts           \# browse scripts\\
zpwr emacsscriptedit        \# edit a script\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsautoload}\vspace{4pt}

Open autoloaded zsh functions in emacs. The beating heart of \textbf{ZPWR}'s function library.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsautoload          \# browse autoloads\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // protip: emacsrecent is your daily driver}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it learns your habits and surfaces what matters}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // sudo variants for when /etc needs surgery}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Emacs Search \& Tags}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} SEARCH \& TAGS: FIND ANYTHING, ANYWHERE}\vspace{6pt}

grep is for amateurs, we have fzf-powered search beams.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILE SEARCH (by filename):}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsfilesearch        \# fzf filename search\\
zpwr emacsfilesearchedit    \# search + edit\\
\end{codebox}

Searches file NAMES across the \textbf{ZPWR} tree. Fast, fuzzy, and feeds results into emacs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORD SEARCH (by content):}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacswordsearch        \# search file content\\
zpwr emacswordsearchedit    \# content search + edit\\
\end{codebox}

Searches INSIDE files for patterns. Like ag/rg but with fzf preview and direct-to-emacs piping.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FIND \& LOCATE SEARCH:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsfindsearch        \# find-based search\\
zpwr emacsfindsearchedit    \# find + edit\\
zpwr emacslocatesearch      \# locate db search\\
zpwr emacslocatesearchedit  \# locate + edit\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TAGS -- CODE NAVIGATION:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsctags             \# ctags generation\\
zpwr emacsgtags             \# GNU Global tags\\
zpwr emacsgtagsedit         \# gtags + jump to def\\
\end{codebox}

Tags let emacs jump to function definitions instantly. Ctags for basic tagging, gtags for cross-reference.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the search hierarchy:}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   wordsearch  = grep inside files (content)}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   filesearch  = find by filename (path)}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   locatesearch = system locate db (everywhere)}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   findsearch  = find command (directory tree)}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   tags        = jump to definitions (symbols)}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Emacs Config \& Server}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CONFIG \& SERVER: THE EMACS MOTHERSHIP}\vspace{6pt}

Because starting emacs fresh every time is a war crime.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsconfig}\vspace{4pt}

Edit \textbf{ZPWR}'s emacs configuration files. The bridge between the shell framework and emacs-land.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsconfig            \# edit zpwr emacs cfg\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsemacsconfig}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your personal emacs init files (init.el, etc). Deeper than emacsconfig -- this is YOUR emacs setup.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsemacsconfig       \# edit \textasciitilde{}/.emacs.d\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsallserver}\vspace{4pt}

Opens files using emacsclient, connecting to a running emacs daemon. Instant file opens, no startup penalty.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsallserver         \# open via daemon\\
\end{codebox}

HOW THE SERVER WORKFLOW WORKS: 1. Start emacs daemon:  emacs --daemon 2. Use emacsallserver to open files via emacsclient 3. Files open instantly in the running emacs instance 4. No more 3-second startup tax per file.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacstokens}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your tokens/secrets file in emacs. API keys, env vars, the classified stuff.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacstokens            \# edit ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacszpwr}\vspace{4pt}

Open the entire \textbf{ZPWR} directory tree in emacs.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacszpwr              \# the whole framework\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // emacsclient + daemon = the cheat code}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // startup once, open files forever}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // this is how emacs users actually survive}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{TMUX WARFARE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_hack_the_planet.png}
\end{center}

\section{Tmux Session Management}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TMUX WARFARE: TERMINAL MULTIPLEXER COMBAT}\vspace{6pt}

One terminal to rule them all, split them, and in panes bind them.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr attach}\vspace{4pt}

Attach to an existing tmux session. If no session exists, one will be summoned.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr attach                 \# rejoin the matrix\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr detach}\vspace{4pt}

Detach from current tmux session gracefully. Your processes keep running in the background.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr detach                 \# leave but don't kill\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr killmux}\vspace{4pt}

Kill the tmux server. Nuclear option. Everything dies. WARNING: all sessions, windows, and panes -- gone.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr killmux                \# scorched earth\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr start}\vspace{4pt}

Start a new tmux session with \textbf{ZPWR}'s custom layout. Preconfigured panes, windows, and keybindings.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr start                  \# bootstrap tmux\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr starttabs}\vspace{4pt}

Start tmux with multiple pre-configured tabs. Each tab gets its own purpose -- dev, logs, git, etc.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr starttabs              \# multi-tab setup\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the tmux lifecycle:}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   start --> attach --> detach --> attach --> killmux}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   sessions persist through SSH disconnects}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   your terminal state is immortal}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Key Duplication -- Multi-Pane Workflow}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} KEY DUPLICATION: TYPE ONCE, EXECUTE EVERYWHERE}\vspace{6pt}

The closest thing to being in two places at once.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr startsend}\vspace{4pt}

Send keystrokes to ALL panes in the current window. Whatever you type goes to every pane simultaneously.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr startsend              \# enable key broadcast\\
\end{codebox}

USE CASE: You have 4 panes SSHed into 4 servers. You need to run the same command on all of them. Startsend + type once = executed on all 4.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr startsendfull}\vspace{4pt}

Like startsend, but broadcasts across ALL windows and ALL panes in the entire tmux session.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr startsendfull          \# global broadcast\\
\end{codebox}

DANGER: every pane in every window receives input. Make sure you know what you are doing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr stopsend}\vspace{4pt}

Turn off key broadcasting. Back to single-pane input.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr stopsend               \# disable broadcast\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE MULTI-PANE WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

1. zpwr start                   \# launch tmux 2. Split into 4 panes (Ctrl-b \%) 3. SSH into different servers in each 4. zpwr startsend               \# enable broadcast 5. Type: sudo apt update \&\& sudo apt upgrade -y 6. All 4 servers update simultaneously 7. zpwr stopsend                \# back to normal

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // sysadmins call this poor mans ansible}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // but honestly it is faster for quick ops}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Clipboard \& Buffer Operations}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CLIPBOARD OPS: COPY-PASTE ON STEROIDS}\vspace{6pt}

Because Ctrl-C Ctrl-V is so last millennium.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pastebuffer}\vspace{4pt}

Paste from the tmux paste buffer into the terminal. Tmux has its own clipboard, separate from the system.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pastebuffer            \# paste tmux buffer\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pastestring}\vspace{4pt}

Paste a specific string into the active pane. Programmatic pasting -- useful in scripts.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pastestring 'echo hello' \# paste literal text\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pastecommand}\vspace{4pt}

Paste and execute a command in the active pane. Like pastestring but hits enter for you.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pastecommand 'ls -la'  \# paste + execute\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr google}\vspace{4pt}

Open a Google search in your default browser. Because sometimes you need to leave the terminal.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr google 'zsh completion' \# search the web\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr openurl}\vspace{4pt}

Open any URL in the default browser.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr openurl https://github.com \# open URL\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the clipboard pipeline:}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   terminal output --> tmux buffer --> pastebuffer}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   system clipboard --> pastestring --> pane}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   brain --> google --> browser --> back to terminal}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Autocomplete Integration}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} AUTOCOMPLETE: THE TAB KEY'S FINAL FORM}\vspace{6pt}

Zsh-autocomplete + tmux = sentient tab completion.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr startauto}\vspace{4pt}

Enable the \textbf{ZPWR} autocomplete integration. Completions appear as you type, no Tab needed.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr startauto              \# enable live complete\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr stopauto}\vspace{4pt}

Disable autocomplete. Sometimes you want silence.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr stopauto               \# disable live complete\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS WITH TMUX:}\vspace{4pt}

The autocomplete system hooks into zsh's completion engine and renders suggestions in real-time.

FEATURES: * Live suggestions as you type * History-based predictions * Path completion with preview * Command argument completion * Works across all tmux panes independently.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TMUX + AUTOCOMPLETE TIPS:}\vspace{4pt}

* Each tmux pane runs its own zsh instance * Autocomplete state is per-pane * startauto/stopauto affects current pane only * If completions feel slow, check \$\textbf{ZPWR\_COMPS}\_CACHE

\begin{codebox}
zpwr stopauto               \# when it gets noisy\\
zpwr startauto              \# when you miss it\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // autocomplete is like a copilot for your shell}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it watches, it learns, it suggests}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // toggle it off when you need to think in silence}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Process Monitoring}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PROCESS MONITORING: WATCHING THE WATCHERS}\vspace{6pt}

Every PID tells a story, most of them tragic.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pstreemonitor}\vspace{4pt}

Displays a live process tree. Watch your processes branch, fork, and occasionally die unexpectedly.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pstreemonitor          \# live process tree\\
\end{codebox}

Uses pstree under the hood with continuous refresh. Like htop's tree view but from the \textbf{ZPWR} command line.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr tty}\vspace{4pt}

Show info about the current terminal/tty.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr tty                    \# current tty info\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TMUX + MONITORING WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

The power move: dedicate a tmux pane to monitoring.

LAYOUT EXAMPLE: +-------------------+--------------------+ |                   |                    | |   your work       |  pstreemonitor     | |                   |                    | +-------------------+--------------------+ |         zpwr taillog                   | +-----------------------------------------+


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PROCESS HUNTING:}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} integrates fzf with ps and lsof for killing.

Ctrl-Y Ctrl-K                  \# fzf kill by ps Ctrl-Y Ctrl-L                  \# fzf kill by lsof.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // pstreemonitor in a side pane = instant situational awareness}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // you will see zombie processes before they see you}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{NETWORK \& CONTAINERS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_firewall.png}
\end{center}

\section{Network Recon}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} NETWORK RECON: SCANNING THE WIRE}\vspace{6pt}

Packets don't lie, but DNS sometimes does.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr digs}\vspace{4pt}

Enhanced DNS lookup. Wraps dig with cleaner output. When you need to know where a domain really points.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr digs github.com         \# DNS lookup\\
zpwr digs example.com        \# check any domain\\
\end{codebox}

Shows A records, CNAME chains, TTLs -- the works. Faster than remembering dig +short +noall +answer.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pi / zpwr ping}\vspace{4pt}

Ping a host. Simple, reliable, essential. 'pi' is the short alias because typing matters.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pi google.com           \# quick ping\\
zpwr ping 8.8.8.8           \# verbose ping\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr pir}\vspace{4pt}

Ping with extended options. More detailed network diagnostics for when simple ping is not enough.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pir 192.168.1.1        \# extended ping\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RECON WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

1. zpwr digs suspect-domain.com  \# where does it resolve? 2. zpwr pi <ip-from-dig>         \# is it alive? 3. zpwr pir <ip>                 \# deeper probe

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // three verbs, complete network recon}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // digs for DNS, pi for heartbeat, pir for details}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // when in doubt, ping first -- dead hosts tell no tales}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Tor Integration}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TOR INTEGRATION: ANONYMOUS MODE ENGAGED}\vspace{6pt}

The onion router: because privacy is not a crime.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr torip}\vspace{4pt}

Check your current Tor exit node IP address. Confirms you're routing through the onion network.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr torip                  \# show tor exit IP\\
\end{codebox}

If Tor is running, this shows the exit node's IP. If not, it'll tell you Tor isn't connected.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr toriprenew}\vspace{4pt}

Request a new Tor circuit. Get a fresh exit node and a new apparent IP address.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr toriprenew             \# new identity\\
\end{codebox}

BEFORE YOU START:

Tor must be installed and running as a service.

MacOS:   brew install tor \&\& brew services start tor Linux:   sudo apt install tor \&\& sudo systemctl start tor.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE ANONYMOUS WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

1. Start tor service 2. zpwr torip            \# confirm you're connected 3. Do your thing 4. zpwr toriprenew       \# rotate identity 5. zpwr torip            \# verify new IP

DISCLAIMER: Tor provides anonymity, not invincibility. DNS leaks, browser fingerprinting, and OPSEC failures can still expose you. Use responsibly.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // "I am become anonymous, destroyer of logs"}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} //   -- J. Robert Oppenheimer, probably not}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Docker Management}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DOCKER MANAGEMENT: CONTAINER WARFARE}\vspace{6pt}

It works on my machine --> we ship the machine.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr dockerwipe}\vspace{4pt}

The nuclear option for Docker. Removes EVERYTHING: containers, images, volumes, networks -- all of it.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr dockerwipe             \# purge all docker\\
\end{codebox}

WARNING: This is not gentle. It will: - Stop all running containers - Remove all containers - Remove all images - Remove all volumes - Prune everything.

WHEN TO USE: * Disk space emergency (docker images love eating disk) * Fresh start after a botched experiment * When 'docker system df' shows 50GB of shame.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr dfimage}\vspace{4pt}

Reverse-engineer a Docker image to show its Dockerfile. See how an image was built without the source.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr dfimage nginx:latest   \# reverse Dockerfile\\
zpwr dfimage myapp:v2       \# inspect any image\\
\end{codebox}

Useful for auditing third-party images. Trust but verify what's in that container.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DOCKER TRIAGE:}\vspace{4pt}

1. \$ docker system df              \# how bad is it? 2. zpwr dfimage suspect:latest   \# what's inside? 3. zpwr dockerwipe               \# burn it all down 4. \$ docker system df              \# ahh, fresh disk

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // dockerwipe: for when docker system prune isn't angry enough}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // dfimage: for when you don't trust the README}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Clone \& GitHub Workflow}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CLONE \& GITHUB: THE FULL GIT WORKFLOW}\vspace{6pt}

git clone is just the beginning of the relationship.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr clone}\vspace{4pt}

Clone a git repository with \textbf{ZPWR}'s enhanced setup. Handles auth, directory placement, and post-clone hooks.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr clone user/repo         \# clone from GitHub\\
zpwr clone https://...       \# clone any URL\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr reveal}\vspace{4pt}

Open the current git repo's GitHub page in browser. Instant context switch from terminal to web.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reveal                 \# open repo in browser\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr revealrecurse}\vspace{4pt}

Open GitHub pages for the repo AND its submodules. When you need the full picture of a multi-repo project.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr revealrecurse          \# open all repos\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE GITHUB WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

1. zpwr clone user/repo       \# get the code 2. \$ cd repo \&\& hack hack hack 3. zpwr reveal                \# check CI/issues 4. \$ git push                   \# ship it


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RELATED TOOLS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr killremote             \# kill remote sessions\\
\end{codebox}

Killremote terminates remote SSH/tmux sessions that have gone stale. Cleanup for the disciplined operator.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // reveal is the bridge between terminal and browser}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // faster than typing github.com/user/repo}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your fingers will thank you}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{THE LOGGING SYSTEM}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_data_stream.png}
\end{center}

\section{Log Levels}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LOG LEVELS: SIGNAL VS NOISE}\vspace{6pt}

if a tree falls in the forest and nobody tails the log...

\textbf{ZPWR} has a full structured logging system with four levels. Each level has a file variant and a console variant.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LEVEL 1: TRACE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr logtrace 'entering func'  \# most verbose\\
\end{codebox}

The firehose. Every function entry, variable assignment, and internal state change. Use for deep debugging only.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LEVEL 2: DEBUG}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr logdebug 'cache miss'    \# developer detail\\
\end{codebox}

Detailed info for development. Cache behavior, branch decisions, intermediate values.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LEVEL 3: INFO}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr loginfo 'update done'    \# normal operations\\
\end{codebox}

Standard operational messages. "Started", "Completed", "Loaded 47 plugins". The daily heartbeat.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LEVEL 4: ERROR}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr logerror 'file not found' \# something broke\\
\end{codebox}

Things that should not have happened but did. Missing files, failed commands, broken assumptions.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SEVERITY PYRAMID:}\vspace{4pt}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Console vs File Logging}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CONSOLE VS FILE: TWO DESTINATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

To stderr or to disk, that is the question.

Every log verb comes in two flavors:


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILE VARIANTS (write to \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOGFILE}):}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr logtrace 'msg'          \# --> logfile\\
zpwr logdebug 'msg'          \# --> logfile\\
zpwr loginfo 'msg'           \# --> logfile\\
zpwr logerror 'msg'          \# --> logfile\\
\end{codebox}

These write silently to the log file. No terminal output. Perfect for background operations and scripts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONSOLE VARIANTS (write to stderr AND logfile):}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr logtraceconsole 'msg'   \# --> stderr + file\\
zpwr logdebugconsole 'msg'   \# --> stderr + file\\
zpwr loginfoconsole 'msg'    \# --> stderr + file\\
zpwr logerrorconsole 'msg'   \# --> stderr + file\\
\end{codebox}

These print to your terminal AND write to the log. For interactive debugging when you need to see it live.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE LOGFILE:}\vspace{4pt}

\$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOGFILE} = \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr.log}

\begin{codebox}
cat ZPWR\_LOGFILE | tail -20   \# recent entries\\
wc -l ZPWR\_LOGFILE           \# how big is it?\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CHOOSING YOUR VARIANT:}\vspace{4pt}

SCENARIO                          USE Background script running          loginfo Debugging interactively             logdebugconsole Tracking a rare bug                 logtraceconsole Production error handling           logerror.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // file = silent, persistent, searchable}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // console = loud, immediate, ephemeral}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // use both strategically}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Reading Logs}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} READING LOGS: THE ART OF LOG DIVING}\vspace{6pt}

Somewhere in that wall of text is the answer you seek.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr taillog}\vspace{4pt}

Live tail of the \textbf{ZPWR} log file with ccze colorization. Watch events stream by in glorious technicolor.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr taillog                \# live colorized tail\\
\end{codebox}

ccze automatically highlights timestamps, levels, paths, and errors. Makes raw logs actually readable.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT TO LOOK FOR:}\vspace{4pt}

ERROR  lines = something broke, investigate immediately WARN   lines = potential issue, worth noting INFO   lines = normal operations, sanity check DEBUG  lines = details for troubleshooting.

\begin{codebox}
TRACE  lines = deep internals, very verbose\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILTERING TECHNIQUES:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
grep ERROR ZPWR\_LOGFILE     \# errors only\\
grep -c ERROR ZPWR\_LOGFILE  \# count errors\\
tail -100 ZPWR\_LOGFILE      \# last 100 lines\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TMUX LOG MONITORING SETUP:}\vspace{4pt}

Dedicate a tmux pane to zpwr taillog:

1. Split pane: Ctrl-b \% 2. In new pane: zpwr taillog 3. Work in other pane, watch logs stream 4. Errors glow red thanks to ccze


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LOG MAINTENANCE:}\vspace{4pt}

Logs grow forever if you let them. \textbf{ZPWR} handles rotation, but you can manually truncate:

\begin{codebox}
: > ZPWR\_LOGFILE            \# truncate to zero\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // taillog + ccze = the cyberpunk log experience}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // errors in red, info in green, timestamps in cyan}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Debug Mode}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DEBUG MODE: WHEN THINGS GO SIDEWAYS}\vspace{6pt}

Enabling verbose output is how you stop guessing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR\_DEBUG}}\vspace{4pt}

Set this env var to enable debug-level logging. More detail in the log, more signal for your hunt.

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_DEBUG=true       \# enable debug\\
zpwr reload                  \# reload with debug\\
zpwr taillog                 \# watch debug output\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR\_TRACE}}\vspace{4pt}

Maximum verbosity. Every function entry and exit, every variable, every decision point.

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_TRACE=true       \# enable trace\\
zpwr reload                  \# reload with trace\\
\end{codebox}

WARNING: Trace mode generates MASSIVE log output. Your logfile will grow fast. Use sparingly.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DEBUGGING WORKFLOW:}\vspace{4pt}

1. Reproduce the problem once to confirm it 2. \$ export \textbf{ZPWR\_DEBUG}=true 3. zpwr reload 4. Reproduce the problem again 5. zpwr taillog              \# find the smoking gun 6. Still lost? Escalate: 7. \$ export \textbf{ZPWR\_TRACE}=true 8. zpwr reload \&\& reproduce


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DISABLING DEBUG:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
unset ZPWR\_DEBUG ZPWR\_TRACE  \# back to normal\\
zpwr reload                  \# reload clean\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // debug mode: "trust but verify" in action}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // trace mode: "verify everything, trust nothing"}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // always disable after debugging or your log eats disk}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{INTROSPECTION}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_deep_dive.png}
\end{center}

\section{Counting Everything}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} COUNTING EVERYTHING: THE CENSUS BUREAU}\vspace{6pt}

If you can't measure it, you can't brag about it.

\textbf{ZPWR} tracks the size of every component. These verbs tell you exactly how big your installation is.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr autoloadcount}\vspace{4pt}

Count of autoloaded zsh functions in the framework.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoloadcount           \# how many functions?\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr scriptcount}\vspace{4pt}

Count of shell scripts in \textbf{\$ZPWR/scripts.}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scriptcount             \# how many scripts?\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr zshplugincount}\vspace{4pt}

Count of installed zsh plugins (zinit, oh-my-zsh, etc).

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zshplugincount          \# zsh plugins loaded\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr vimplugincount}\vspace{4pt}

Count of installed vim/neovim plugins.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimplugincount          \# vim plugins loaded\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacsplugincount}\vspace{4pt}

Count of installed emacs packages.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacsplugincount        \# emacs pkgs loaded\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FULL CENSUS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoloadcount \&\& zpwr scriptcount \&\& \textbackslash{}\\
\end{codebox}

zpwr zshplugincount \&\& zpwr vimplugincount \&\& \textbackslash{} zpwr emacsplugincount.

Run all five to get the full picture. \textbf{ZPWR} typically ships with 300+ autoloads, 50+ scripts, and dozens of plugins across all three editors.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // these counts tell you the scope of \textbf{ZPWR}}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // hundreds of functions working in concert}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // like a small city running on your laptop}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Listing Everything}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LISTING EVERYTHING: THE INVENTORY MANIFEST}\vspace{6pt}

Counting is nice, but names are power.

Counts tell you how many. Lists tell you WHAT. Every count verb has a list counterpart.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr autoloadlist}\vspace{4pt}

List every autoloaded function by name.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoloadlist            \# all function names\\
zpwr autoloadlist | grep fzf \# find fzf funcs\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr scriptlist}\vspace{4pt}

List every script in the scripts directory.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scriptlist              \# all scripts\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr zshpluginlist}\vspace{4pt}

List installed zsh plugins with their sources.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zshpluginlist           \# all zsh plugins\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr vimpluginlist}\vspace{4pt}

List installed vim/neovim plugins.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vimpluginlist           \# all vim plugins\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr emacspluginlist}\vspace{4pt}

List installed emacs packages.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr emacspluginlist         \# all emacs packages\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} POWER COMBOS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoloadlist | wc -l    \# quick count\\
zpwr autoloadlist | fzf      \# fuzzy browse\\
zpwr scriptlist | sort       \# alphabetical\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // lists are grep-able, fzf-able, pipe-able}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the raw material for your own exploration}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // pipe into fzf for interactive discovery}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{History Archaeology}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} HISTORY ARCHAEOLOGY: DIGGING UP THE PAST}\vspace{6pt}

Your terminal remembers everything, even the mistakes.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr hist}\vspace{4pt}

Browse command history with fzf. Search, preview, and re-execute past commands interactively.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr hist                   \# fzf history browser\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr histedit}\vspace{4pt}

Edit the history file directly. Surgically remove embarrassing commands or fix typos in saved history.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr histedit               \# edit history file\\
\end{codebox}

Use with care. History surgery is delicate work.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr histfile}\vspace{4pt}

Show the path to the current history file. Useful when you're not sure which histfile is active.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr histfile               \# show histfile path\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr catviminfo}\vspace{4pt}

Display vim's recent file history from viminfo. What files has vim touched recently?

\begin{codebox}
zpwr catviminfo             \# vim file history\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr catrecentfviminfo / catnviminforecentf}\vspace{4pt}

Cross-reference vim/nvim recent files with the recentf list. Where vim and shell history intersect.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr catrecentfviminfo      \# vim+recentf cross\\
zpwr catnviminforecentf     \# nvim+recentf cross\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your history is a goldmine of workflow patterns}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // hist + fzf = instant command recall}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // histedit = revisionist history (use responsibly)}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Verb Discovery}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VERB DISCOVERY: FINDING WHAT'S POSSIBLE}\vspace{6pt}

You can't use what you don't know exists.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr subcommands}\vspace{4pt}

List all zpwr subcommands (verbs). The master index of everything zpwr can do.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr subcommands             \# all subcommands\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr subcommandscount}\vspace{4pt}

How many subcommands exist? The number keeps growing.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr subcommandscount        \# total count\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr verbs (alias: subcommandsfzf)}\vspace{4pt}

Browse subcommands interactively with fzf. The best way to explore when you're not sure what to try.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr verbs         \# fzf subcommands\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr verbslist / verbsfzf / verbscount}\vspace{4pt}

Alternative verb-centric views of available commands.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr verbslist              \# list all verbs\\
zpwr verbs               \# fzf browse verbs\\
zpwr verbscount             \# count verbs\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr wizard / manual / tutorial / docs}\vspace{4pt}

You're reading it right now. The interactive encyclopedia with vim-style navigation. Very meta. All four names work.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr wizard                 \# start the wizard\\
zpwr wizard --toc           \# table of contents\\
zpwr wizard --search docker  \# search pages\\
zpwr wizard 42              \# jump to page 42\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // verbsfzf is the fastest way to discover (alias: subcommandsfzf)}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // type a few letters, fzf narrows it down}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // when in doubt, fzf it out}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Shell State Inspection}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} SHELL STATE: KNOW THY ZSH}\vspace{6pt}

Introspection: when the shell looks in the mirror.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr exists}\vspace{4pt}

Check if a \textbf{ZPWR} component exists (functions, files, etc).

\begin{codebox}
zpwr exists zpwrReload       \# does this exist?\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr existscommand}\vspace{4pt}

Check if a command exists in \textbf{PATH}. Cleaner than 'which' or 'command -v' in scripts.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr existscommand git      \# is git installed?\\
zpwr existscommand rg       \# is ripgrep here?\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr reload}\vspace{4pt}

Reload the entire \textbf{ZPWR} framework without restarting. Re-sources configs, rebuilds caches, refreshes state.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reload                 \# hot reload everything\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ZSH INSPECTION TOOLS:}\vspace{4pt}

These are built into zsh, but \textbf{ZPWR} makes them accessible:

\begin{codebox}
setopt                      \# list active options\\
zmodload                    \# list loaded modules\\
zstyle -L                   \# list all zstyles\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr copycommand}\vspace{4pt}

Copy a command's path or definition to clipboard.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr copycommand ls         \# copy ls path\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr opencommand}\vspace{4pt}

Open a command's source file in your editor. Works for functions, scripts, and binaries.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr opencommand zpwrReload  \# view the source\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // existscommand before you call, reload after you change}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // opencommand when you need to understand the internals}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{INSTALLATION \& RESET}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/figlet_init.png}
\end{center}

\section{Config Sessions}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CONFIG SESSIONS: EDIT EVERYTHING FROM HERE}\vspace{6pt}

One verb to open any config, no path memorization.

\textbf{ZPWR} provides shortcut verbs for every config file you'll ever need to edit. No more 'vim \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zshrc}'.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr brc  -- Bash RC}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your .bashrc. For when bash compatibility matters.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr brc                    \# edit .bashrc\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr zrc  -- Zsh RC}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your .zshrc. The most-touched config in \textbf{ZPWR}.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zrc                    \# edit .zshrc\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr vrc  -- Vim RC}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your .vimrc or init.vim/init.lua.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vrc                    \# edit vim config\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr trc  -- Tmux RC}\vspace{4pt}

Edit your .tmux.conf. Keybindings, statusbar, the works.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr trc                    \# edit tmux config\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr tokens  -- Secrets File}\vspace{4pt}

Edit \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh.} API keys, secrets, env vars.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr tokens                 \# edit tokens/secrets\\
zpwr vimtokens              \# same, force vim\\
zpwr emacstokens            \# same, force emacs\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr zcompdump  -- Completion Cache}\vspace{4pt}

Inspect or regenerate the zsh completion dump file. When completions act weird, this is where you look.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zcompdump              \# inspect comp cache\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // brc, zrc, vrc, trc -- four letters, four configs}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // tokens for secrets, zcompdump for completion fixes}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Install \& Uninstall}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} INSTALL \& UNINSTALL: SETUP AND TEARDOWN}\vspace{6pt}

Easy to install, clean to remove -- no malware energy.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr install}\vspace{4pt}

Run the full \textbf{ZPWR} installation process. Installs dependencies, links configs, sets up plugins.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr install                \# full installation\\
\end{codebox}

WHAT IT DOES: 1. Installs system dependencies (brew/apt) 2. Links config files to their proper locations 3. Installs zsh, vim, and emacs plugins 4. Generates caches and completion dumps 5. Sets up tmux configuration.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr uninstall}\vspace{4pt}

Remove \textbf{ZPWR} configuration. Restores your shell to its pre-\textbf{ZPWR} state. Clean breakup, no hard feelings.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr uninstall              \# remove zpwr config\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONFIG LINK MANAGEMENT:}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} uses symlinks to place configs where tools expect them.

How it works: \textbf{\$ZPWR/configs/.zshrc}  -->  \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zshrc}     (symlink) \textbf{\$ZPWR/configs/.vimrc}  -->  \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.vimrc}     (symlink) \textbf{\$ZPWR/configs/.tmux}   -->  \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.tmux.conf} (symlink)

The source of truth lives in \$\textbf{ZPWR}, the symlinks just point there. Version controlled, portable, clean.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PARTIAL OPERATIONS:}\vspace{4pt}

Sometimes you don't want the full install/uninstall. You just need to fix the symlinks:

Regenconfiglinks = recreate all config symlinks rmconfiglinks    = remove symlinks only.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // install is idempotent -- run it again when things drift}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // uninstall is clean -- your system goes back to stock}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Reset \& Recovery}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} RESET \& RECOVERY: WHEN THINGS GO WRONG}\vspace{6pt}

Every system needs a panic button -- here are yours.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr reset}\vspace{4pt}

Full framework reset. Clears caches, regenerates configs, rebuilds completion dumps, relinks everything.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reset                  \# the big red button\\
\end{codebox}

WHEN TO USE: * Completions are broken or stale * Config changes aren't taking effect * Something feels "off" and you can't pin it down * After a major \textbf{ZPWR} update.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr reload}\vspace{4pt}

Hot reload without resetting. Re-sources all configs and refreshes the environment. Faster than reset.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr reload                 \# hot reload\\
\end{codebox}

Use reload when you've edited a config and want to apply changes without nuking caches.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ESCALATION LADDER:}\vspace{4pt}

Level 1: zpwr reload           \# re-source configs Level 2: zpwr reset            \# rebuild everything Level 3: zpwr install          \# full reinstall Level 4: rm -rf \$\textbf{ZPWR} \&\& clone \# scorched earth.

Start at Level 1. Only escalate if the problem persists. Most issues are fixed by reload. Reset handles the rest.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMMON RECOVERY SCENARIOS:}\vspace{4pt}

"Completions are wrong"    --> zpwr reset "alias isn't working"      --> zpwr reload "new plugin not loading"   --> zpwr reload "everything is broken"     --> zpwr reset "still broken after reset" --> zpwr install.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // reload is ctrl-r for your shell framework}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // reset is the factory reset you hope to never need}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // but when you do need it, you'll be glad it exists}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{BATCH OPERATIONS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_override.png}
\end{center}

\section{Loop Warriors}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LOOP WARRIORS — THE REPETITION TOOLKIT}\vspace{6pt}

Because doing things once is for civilians.

The batch operations verbs are the assault rifles of the \textbf{ZPWR} arsenal. Point them at a problem, pull the trigger, and watch every target fall.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FOR LOOP — YOUR BASIC INFANTRY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr for <cmd> <args...>\\
\end{codebox}

Executes a command for each argument passed to it. Stdin becomes your ammo belt — pipe lines in, each one fires.

Example: run a command against multiple targets.

\begin{codebox}
echo 'file1.txt\textbackslash{}nfile2.txt' | zpwr for wc -l\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FOR10 — THE BURST-FIRE VARIANT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr for10 <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Runs your command 10 times. No arguments, no mercy. Perfect for stress testing, benchmarking, or just asserting dominance over your CPU.

Example: benchmark a command 10 times.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr for10 zpwr bench\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHILETRUE — THE INFINITE LOOP}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr whiletrue <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Runs your command forever. Ctrl-C is your only exit. For monitoring, watching, waiting. The sentinel loop.

Example: watch disk usage forever.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr whiletrue df -h /\\
\end{codebox}

WARNING: there is no break condition. You ARE the break.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHILESLEEP — THE PATIENT SNIPER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr whilesleep <seconds> <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Like whiletrue, but sleeps between rounds. Polling with grace. Surveillance with manners.

Example: check a URL every 30 seconds.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr whilesleep 30 curl -s -o /dev/null -w '\%\{http\_code\}' https://example.com\\
\end{codebox}

TACTICAL SUMMARY: for          iterate over stdin lines for10        run 10 times, no questions asked whiletrue    infinite loop, Ctrl-C to escape whilesleep   infinite loop with naptime between rounds.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // in the loop, there is no time, only iterations}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the loop does not end. the operator ends the loop.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Directory Sweeps}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DIRECTORY SWEEPS — CARPET BOMBING YOUR FILESYSTEM}\vspace{6pt}

One directory at a time, or all of them at once.

Single-file loops are amateur hour. Real operators sweep directories. They glob patterns. They parallelize. These verbs are your air support.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORDIR — THE DIRECTORY CRAWLER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr fordir <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Iterates into each subdirectory of the current dir and executes the command inside it. Cd in, run, cd out. Like a SWAT team clearing rooms one by one.

Example: check git status in every project dir.

\begin{codebox}
cd \textasciitilde{}/repos \&\& zpwr fordir git status -s\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORDIRUPDATE — THE MASS UPDATER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr fordirupdate\\
\end{codebox}

Enters every subdirectory and runs git pull. Your entire project portfolio, synced in one command. Monday morning? Run this first. Coffee second.

Example: update all repos under \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/repos}

\begin{codebox}
cd \textasciitilde{}/repos \&\& zpwr fordirupdate\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXECGLOB — GLOB AND EXECUTE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execglob <glob> <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Finds files matching a glob pattern and runs your command against each one. Pattern matching meets execution. The regex sniper rifle of file operations.

Example: lint every Python file in the tree.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execglob '*.py' pylint\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXECGLOBPARALLEL — CONCURRENT DEVASTATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execglobparallel <glob> <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Same as execglob, but fires all jobs in parallel. Every CPU core gets drafted into service. Your fan will spin up. This is normal. This is power.

Example: format every .rs file in parallel.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execglobparallel '*.rs' rustfmt\\
\end{codebox}

CHOOSING YOUR WEAPON: fordir             run cmd in each subdirectory fordirupdate       git pull in each subdirectory execglob           glob match + execute sequentially execglobparallel   glob match + execute in parallel.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // sequential is predictable. parallel is fast.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // choose based on whether output order matters.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // when in doubt, go parallel. life is short.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Polyglot Execution}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} POLYGLOT EXECUTION — BEYOND THE SHELL}\vspace{6pt}

zsh is the conductor, but the orchestra speaks many tongues.

Sometimes zsh loops aren't enough. Sometimes you need Python. Sometimes you need background jobs. Sometimes you need to poll a service until it wakes up or dies.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXECPY — THE PYTHON BRIDGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execpy <python\_expression>\\
\end{codebox}

Fires a Python expression from the comfort of your shell. No need to open a REPL. No need for a .py file. Quick math, string ops, one-liners — python as a verb.

Example: quick calculation.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr execpy '2**128'\\
\end{codebox}

=> 340282366920938463463374607431768211456.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BACKGROUND — THE SHADOW OPERATIVE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr background <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Sends a command into the background. Detached. Silent. It runs in the shadows while you keep working. Output goes to the void unless you redirect it.

Example: start a long build in the background.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr background make -j8\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TIMER — THE STOPWATCH}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr timer <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Wraps any command in a high-resolution timer. How long did that take? Now you know. No guessing. Benchmarking made trivial.

Example: time your shell startup.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr timer zsh -ic exit\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} POLL — THE WATCHDOG}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr poll <cmd>\\
\end{codebox}

Runs a command repeatedly until it succeeds (exit 0). Waiting for a server to come up? A build to finish? Poll is your patient, relentless sentinel.

Example: wait for a port to open.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr poll nc -z localhost 8080\\
\end{codebox}

THE BATCH OPS PHILOSOPHY: Loops        for, for10, whiletrue, whilesleep Sweeps       fordir, fordirupdate, execglob* Polyglot     execpy, background, timer, poll.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the terminal is not a single-threaded mind}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it is a factory floor. these verbs are the machines.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // feed them work. they do not tire. they do not complain.}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{FORGIT DEEP DIVE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_the_grid.png}
\end{center}

\section{Forgit Add \& Diff}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FORGIT ADD \& DIFF — INTERACTIVE STAGING}\vspace{6pt}

git add, but make it beautiful.

Forgit is the fusion of fzf and git. It wraps the most common git operations in fuzzy, interactive, preview-enabled interfaces. You will never go back to raw git add.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITADD — INTERACTIVE STAGING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitadd\\
\end{codebox}

Opens an fzf interface showing all unstaged changes. Each file gets a diff preview in the right pane. Tab to select multiple files. Enter to stage them.

The workflow: 1. Run zpwr forgitadd 2. Browse files with arrow keys 3. See the diff preview for each file 4. Tab to multi-select 5. Enter to stage selected files.

No more git add -p scrolling through 500-line diffs. No more accidentally staging that debug print.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITDIFF — \textbf{VISUAL} DIFF BROWSER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitdiff\\
\end{codebox}

Opens an fzf browser of all changed files with a live diff preview. Read diffs without committing to anything. Pure reconnaissance.

Example workflow:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitdiff           \# browse all diffs visually\\
zpwr forgitadd            \# stage what you want\\
zpwr gitcommit 'msg'      \# commit with message\\
\end{codebox}

WHY FORGIT > RAW GIT: Raw git add:     type filenames from memory like a caveman forgitadd:       browse, preview, multi-select, confirm Raw git diff:    wall of text scrolling past forgitdiff:      file-by-file preview with fuzzy search.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // staging should not be a memory test}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it should be a visual, interactive experience}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // forgit turns git into a GUI without leaving the terminal}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Forgit Log \& Stash}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FORGIT LOG \& STASH — VISUAL HISTORY}\vspace{6pt}

Time travel with a preview window.

Git log is a river of hashes. Git stash is a pile of forgotten context. Forgit turns both into something you can actually navigate without losing your mind.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITLOG — THE COMMIT BROWSER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitlog\\
\end{codebox}

Opens your git history in fzf with commit previews. Each commit shows its full diff in the preview pane. Search commits by message, author, or hash.

What you see: Left pane:  commit graph with short messages Right pane: full diff of highlighted commit Search:     fuzzy match on any commit field.

"Who wrote this garbage?" — you, using forgitlog, discovering it was also you, three months ago.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITSTASH — THE STASH MANAGER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitstash\\
\end{codebox}

Browse your stash entries with preview diffs. See what is in each stash before applying it. No more git stash pop roulette.

The old way: git stash list              \# cryptic one-liners git stash show -p stash@\{2\}  \# which one was it again? git stash pop               \# pray.

The forgit way:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitstash            \# browse, preview, apply\\
\end{codebox}

LOG + STASH TOGETHER: These two verbs give you eyes into the past. Forgitlog shows committed history. Forgitstash shows uncommitted history. Between them, nothing is lost.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the commit graph is not a wall of text}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it is a timeline. forgit lets you walk it.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // stashes are not forgotten. they are filed.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Forgit Ignore \& Clean}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FORGIT IGNORE \& CLEAN — .GITIGNORE MANAGEMENT}\vspace{6pt}

Because node\_modules should never see the inside of a commit.

The .gitignore is your repo's bouncer list. These verbs manage it with the precision of a nightclub velvet rope.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITIGNORE — THE IGNORE BROWSER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitignore\\
\end{codebox}

Interactive gitignore template browser powered by gitignore.io. Search for languages, frameworks, IDEs. Never hand-write a .gitignore again.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE IGNORE FAMILY:}\vspace{4pt}

Forgitignorelist     list available gitignore templates forgitignoreget      fetch a specific template by name forgitignoreupdate   update your local template cache forgitignoreclean    clean the template cache.

Example: generate a Python + macOS gitignore.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitignoreget python,macos > .gitignore\\
\end{codebox}

Example: browse all available templates.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitignorelist\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITCLEAN — THE UNTRACKED FILE PURGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitclean\\
\end{codebox}

Shows untracked files in an fzf interface with previews. Select which ones to delete. Surgical, not scorched earth. Unlike git clean -fd, you see before you destroy.

The old way: git clean -fd    \# delete everything. Hope for the best.

The forgit way:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitclean  \# browse, select, confirm, then delete.\\
\end{codebox}

IGNORE WORKFLOW: 1. zpwr forgitignorelist    browse templates 2. zpwr forgitignoreget     fetch what you need 3. zpwr forgitclean         purge untracked cruft 4. zpwr forgitadd           stage the clean repo.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // a clean repo is a happy repo}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // an ignored file is a file that cannot hurt you}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // let the templates do the thinking}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Forgit Reset \& Restore}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FORGIT RESET \& RESTORE — UNDO WITH EYES OPEN}\vspace{6pt}

The nuclear options, but with a safety preview.

Git reset and restore are the most terrifying commands in version control. Forgit wraps them in bubble wrap and gives you a preview before you pull the trigger.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITRESET — UNSTAGE WITH PREVIEW}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitreset\\
\end{codebox}

Shows staged files in fzf with diff previews. Select files to unstage. Like git reset HEAD but you can see exactly what you are unstaging first.

Use case: you ran git add -A like a maniac and now you need to surgically remove files.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitreset    \# browse and unstage selectively\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITRESTORE — DISCARD CHANGES SAFELY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitrestore\\
\end{codebox}

Shows modified files with diff previews. Select files to restore to their last committed state. This discards changes permanently. No undo.

The preview saves you. You see the diff before the changes evaporate into the void.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITWARN — THE SAFETY NET}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitwarn\\
\end{codebox}

Displays warnings about uncommitted changes, diverged branches, and other hazardous states. Your pre-flight checklist before doing anything destructive.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORGITINFO — THE STATUS DASHBOARD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr forgitinfo\\
\end{codebox}

Comprehensive repo information at a glance. Branch, remotes, recent commits, dirty state. Everything you need before making decisions.

THE FORGIT COMPLETE ARSENAL: forgitadd        interactive stage forgitdiff       browse diffs forgitlog        commit history browser forgitstash      stash manager forgitreset      interactive unstage forgitrestore    discard changes with preview forgitclean      purge untracked files forgitignore*    gitignore template management forgitwarn       hazard warnings forgitinfo       repo dashboard.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // forgit is not a git replacement}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // it is git with eyes. git with a UI. git for humans.}
