\chapter{ZPWR ENVIRONMENT VARIABLES}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_system_core.png}
\end{center}

\section{Core Paths}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CORE PATHS — THE SKELETON OF THE MACHINE}\vspace{6pt}

Every system has bones. These are \textbf{ZPWR}'s.

\textbf{ZPWR} does not hardcode paths. Every directory is an environment variable. Move the root, and the entire system follows. This is not a dotfiles repo — it is a relocatable operating system.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR} — THE ROOT OF ALL THINGS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR   \# typically \textasciitilde{}/.zpwr\\
\end{codebox}

The root directory. Every other path derives from this. Change this one variable and the whole tree moves.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL} — YOUR PRIVATE SANCTUM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_LOCAL   \# ZPWR/local\\
\end{codebox}

Gitignored. Your tokens, secrets, local overrides. Nothing in here ever touches a remote repository. Your .tokens.sh lives here. Guard it with your life.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS} — THE ARMORY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_SCRIPTS   \# ZPWR/scripts\\
\end{codebox}

Executable scripts. Bash, Python, Perl — whatever gets the job done. These are on your \textbf{PATH}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX} — THE MULTIPLEXER CONFIG}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_TMUX   \# ZPWR/tmux\\
\end{codebox}

Tmux configuration, scripts, and status line logic. The control center for your terminal multiplexer.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL} — THE INSTALLER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_INSTALL   \# ZPWR/install\\
\end{codebox}

OS-specific installation scripts. MacOS, Ubuntu, Fedora, Arch — each gets its own installer. Run zpwr install and the right one fires.

THE \textbf{PATH} MAP: \$\textbf{ZPWR}                \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zpwr} (the root) \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL}          \textbf{\$ZPWR/local} (gitignored secrets) \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}        \textbf{\$ZPWR/scripts} (executables) \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}           \textbf{\$ZPWR/tmux} (multiplexer) \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL}        \textbf{\$ZPWR/install} (OS installers)

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // paths are not strings. they are load-bearing structures.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // hardcode a path and you weld yourself to the floor.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // use the variables. always use the variables.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Autoload System}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE AUTOLOAD SYSTEM — LAZY LOADING FOR HACKERS}\vspace{6pt}

Why load everything when you can load nothing until needed?

Zsh autoloading is the secret to \textbf{ZPWR}'s fast startup. Functions are not loaded into memory until you call them. The shell knows they exist, but their code stays on disk until the moment of invocation. Maximum laziness. Maximum speed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD} — THE FUNCTION WAREHOUSE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD   \# ZPWR/autoload\\
\end{codebox}

The root of the autoload tree. Every file in this tree becomes a function name. The filename IS the function.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}\_COMMON — CROSS-PLATFORM FUNCTIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\_COMMON   \# ZPWR/autoload/common\\
\end{codebox}

Functions that work on every OS. The common denominator. MacOS, Linux, BSD — these functions do not care.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW AUTOLOADING WORKS:}\vspace{4pt}

1. Zsh scans \$fpath directories at startup 2. Each filename is registered as a function stub 3. When you call the function, zsh reads the file 4. The file contents replace the stub 5. The function executes with the loaded code

This means hundreds of autoload stubs cost near-zero at startup. Only the ones you actually use consume memory.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE COMPILE ACCELERATION:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr compile       \# compile autoloads to .zwc\\
zpwr recompile     \# recompile all\\
zpwr uncompile     \# remove compiled files\\
\end{codebox}

Compiled .zwc files load faster than raw source. \textbf{ZPWR} compiles its autoload dirs into wordcode files. It is like bytecode for your shell functions.

INSPECTION VERBS:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr autoload          show the autoload system\\
zpwr autoloadcount     count autoloaded functions\\
zpwr autoloadlist      list all autoloaded functions\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the fastest code is code that never runs}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // autoloading is the art of deferred execution}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // 400 functions, zero startup cost. that is the deal.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Behavior Flags}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} BEHAVIOR FLAGS — THE KNOBS AND DIALS}\vspace{6pt}

Tune the machine without cracking open the engine.

\textbf{ZPWR} is not a take-it-or-leave-it monolith. These boolean and string flags let you control startup behavior, logging verbosity, and debug output without editing a single line of source code.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PROFILING} — STARTUP PROFILER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_PROFILING=true\\
\end{codebox}

Enables zsh startup profiling. Shows exactly where your milliseconds go during shell init. Pair with.

\begin{codebox}
zpwr bench for the full picture.\\
\end{codebox}

Set it, open a new shell, and read the profiling data. Find the bottleneck. Kill the bottleneck. Repeat.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_DEBUG} — DEBUG MODE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_DEBUG=true\\
\end{codebox}

Turns on debug-level log messages. When something is broken and you need the machine to talk, flip this. Check output with zpwr logdebug.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TRACE} — FULL TRACE MODE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_TRACE=true\\
\end{codebox}

The firehose. Every function call, every expansion, traced to the log. Use zpwr trace to run commands with tracing, or zpwr logtrace to read trace logs. WARNING: produces enormous output. Use surgically.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_BANNER}\_CLEARLIST — STARTUP BANNER CONTROL}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_BANNER\_CLEARLIST=true\\
\end{codebox}

Controls whether the \textbf{ZPWR} banner and clearlist show at shell startup. The banner is glorious, but if you open 50 terminals a day, you might want silence.

Related verbs:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr banner          show the banner manually\\
zpwr clearlist       show clearlist manually\\
zpwr bannerlolcat    banner with rainbow colors\\
\end{codebox}

QUICK REFERENCE: \textbf{ZPWR\_PROFILING}        startup timing data \textbf{ZPWR\_DEBUG}            debug-level logging \textbf{ZPWR\_TRACE}            full execution trace \textbf{ZPWR\_BANNER}\_CLEARLIST startup banner toggle.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the flags do not change the code. they reveal it.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // debugging is not failure. it is x-ray vision.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Plugin System}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PLUGIN SYSTEM — THE TURBO LOADER}\vspace{6pt}

zinit: loading plugins at the speed of light.

\textbf{ZPWR} uses zinit (formerly zplugin) as its plugin manager. But not the basic way. The turbo way. Plugins load asynchronously after prompt appears. You see your prompt instantly. Plugins catch up.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER — CHOOSE YOUR LOADER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER   \# zinit\\
\end{codebox}

Controls which plugin manager \textbf{ZPWR} uses. The default and recommended value is zinit. Other managers exist but zinit's turbo mode is what makes \textbf{ZPWR} fast.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TURBO LOADING EXPLAINED:}\vspace{4pt}

Normal plugin loading: source plugin1.zsh  \# blocks source plugin2.zsh  \# blocks source plugin3.zsh  \# blocks \# prompt finally appears after ALL plugins load.

Zinit turbo loading: prompt appears immediately plugin1 loads in background ... done plugin2 loads in background ... done plugin3 loads in background ... done \# user never notices the delay


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PLUGIN INSPECTION:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr zshplugincount     count loaded zsh plugins\\
zpwr zshpluginlist      list all loaded zsh plugins\\
zpwr vimplugincount     count vim plugins\\
zpwr vimpluginlist      list all vim plugins\\
zpwr emacsplugincount   count emacs plugins\\
zpwr emacspluginlist    list all emacs plugins\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} UPDATE AND MAINTAIN:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr updatezinit        update zinit itself\\
zpwr updatedeps          update all plugin deps\\
zpwr plugins             plugin information\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // plugins are not bloat. they are force multipliers.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // but only if they load in zero perceived time.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // turbo mode makes 50 plugins feel like zero.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Display \& Input}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} DISPLAY \& INPUT — THE HUMAN INTERFACE LAYER}\vspace{6pt}

How the machine talks to you, and how you talk back.

These variables control what you see and how fast the shell responds to your keystrokes. Milliseconds matter when your fingers are moving at light speed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND — THE LS REPLACEMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_EXA\_COMMAND   \# exa or eza\\
\end{codebox}

Controls which modern ls replacement \textbf{ZPWR} uses. Exa is the original, eza is the maintained fork. Both give you colored, git-aware directory listings.

\textbf{ZPWR} verbs like zpwr ls and zpwr clearls use this. Set it in your tokens file to override the default.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT} — KEYSTROKE LATENCY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT   \# in hundredths of a second\\
\end{codebox}

How long zsh waits for the next key in a multi-key sequence. Lower = snappier. Too low = missed sequences. Default is tuned for fast typists.

If vi-mode feels laggy on Esc, lower this. If key combos misfire, raise this. Sweet spot is personal. Experiment.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EXPAND}\_PRE\_EXEC — ALIAS EXPANSION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_EXPAND\_PRE\_EXEC\\
\end{codebox}

Controls whether \textbf{ZPWR} expands aliases before execution. When enabled, you type the alias and see the full command in your history. Transparency mode.

With expansion:    you type ga  -> history shows git add Without expansion: you type ga  -> history shows ga.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SEND}\_KEYS\_FULL — TMUX KEY PASSTHROUGH}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_SEND\_KEYS\_FULL\\
\end{codebox}

Controls full send-keys behavior in tmux integration. When \textbf{ZPWR} manages tmux panes, this determines how keystrokes are forwarded to target panes.

TUNING GUIDE: Feeling laggy?       Lower \textbf{ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT} Missing combos?      Raise \textbf{ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT} Want transparency?   Enable \textbf{ZPWR\_EXPAND}\_PRE\_EXEC Using eza?           Set \textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND=eza.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the interface is not decoration. it is velocity.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // every millisecond of latency is a papercut.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // tune these until your shell feels like thought.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Runtime State}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} RUNTIME STATE — THE MACHINE'S SELF-AWARENESS}\vspace{6pt}

The shell knows what it is. Do you?

These variables hold runtime state — what OS you are on, what TTY you are using, where logs go. They are set at startup and consulted throughout the session.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TTY} — YOUR TERMINAL IDENTITY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_TTY   \# e.g., /dev/ttys003\\
\end{codebox}

The TTY device for the current shell session. Used by \textbf{ZPWR} to track which terminal is which, especially when managing multiple tmux panes.

The zpwr tty verb shows your current TTY info.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_OS}\_TYPE — PLATFORM DETECTION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_OS\_TYPE   \# Darwin, Linux, etc.\\
\end{codebox}

Detected at startup. Used throughout \textbf{ZPWR} to branch on platform-specific behavior. MacOS vs Linux vs BSD, the right code path fires automatically.

This is why \textbf{ZPWR} works on macOS and Linux without you changing a single setting. The OS type decides.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOGFILE} — THE BLACK BOX}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_LOGFILE\\
\end{codebox}

Where \textbf{ZPWR} writes its log output. Debug, info, error, trace — all levels go here when enabled.

Read the logs:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr loginfo         info-level messages\\
zpwr logdebug        debug-level messages\\
zpwr logerror        error-level messages\\
zpwr logtrace        trace-level messages\\
zpwr taillog         live tail with colorized output\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_COMPSYS}\_CACHE — COMPLETION CACHE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_COMPSYS\_CACHE\\
\end{codebox}

Controls the completion system cache. Zsh completions are expensive to regenerate. This flag keeps them cached.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_HISTFILE} — HISTORY FILE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
echo ZPWR\_HISTFILE\\
\end{codebox}

Your shell history file location. \textbf{ZPWR} manages history with deduplication and backup. Your commands are sacred.

Related: zpwr backuphistory and zpwr restorehistory.

THE FULL ENV VAR MAP: Core paths:      \textbf{ZPWR}, \textbf{ZPWR\_LOCAL}, \textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}, \textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX} Autoload:        \textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}, \textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}\_COMMON Flags:           \textbf{ZPWR\_PROFILING}, \textbf{ZPWR\_DEBUG}, \textbf{ZPWR\_TRACE} Display:         \textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND, \textbf{ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT} Runtime:         \textbf{ZPWR\_TTY}, \textbf{ZPWR\_OS}\_TYPE, \textbf{ZPWR\_LOGFILE}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the machine knows itself. every variable is a mirror.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // read them to understand the current state of reality.}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{CREATIVE TOOLS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/figlet_zpwr.png}
\end{center}

\section{Media Creation}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} MEDIA CREATION — ART FROM THE COMMAND LINE}\vspace{6pt}

Who needs photoshop when you have a terminal?

The terminal is not just for text. \textbf{ZPWR} includes verbs for generating GIFs, rendering ASCII art, and creating visual content without ever opening a GUI application.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CREATEGIF — SCREEN TO GIF}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr creategif\\
\end{codebox}

Creates animated GIFs from your terminal sessions. Record your workflow, your demos, your flex. Perfect for README demos and pull request previews.

Your terminal is the camera. The GIF is the film. No screen recording software needed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FIGLETFONTS — THE ASCII ART GALLERY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr figletfonts\\
\end{codebox}

Previews all available figlet fonts on your system. figlet turns text into massive ASCII art banners. This verb shows you every font so you can pick one.

Ever wondered what your name looks like in 47 different ASCII art styles? Now you know.

Example output (one of many fonts): \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_  \_\_\_\_      \_\_\_\_  \_\_\_\_  \_\_\_\_ |\_\_\_  \_\_\_||  \_ \textbackslash{}    |  \_ \textbackslash{}|  \_ \textbackslash{}|  \_ \textbackslash{} / /    | |\_) |   | |\_) | |\_) | |\_) | / /     |  \_\_/    |  \_\_/|  \_ <|  \_ < / /      | |       | |   | | \textbackslash{} | | \textbackslash{} \textbackslash{} /\_/       |\_|       |\_|   |\_|  \textbackslash{}|\_|  \textbackslash{}\_\textbackslash{}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ANIMATE — TERMINAL ANIMATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr animate\\
\end{codebox}

Runs terminal animations. Because sometimes the command line needs to look as cool as it feels. Eye candy for the operator who has everything.

THE CREATIVE TOOLKIT: creategif       record terminal to GIF figletfonts     preview all ASCII art fonts animate         terminal animations banner          the \textbf{ZPWR} startup banner bannerlolcat    rainbow-colored banner.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the terminal is a canvas. most people see a spreadsheet.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // hackers see a gallery. these verbs are your brushes.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Color Systems}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} COLOR SYSTEMS — PAINTING THE VOID}\vspace{6pt}

256 colors. true color. the terminal is not monochrome.

Color is not cosmetic. It is information density. A colored diff tells you more in one glance than a monochrome one does in ten seconds. \textbf{ZPWR} gives you tools to explore, test, and master terminal color.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COLORTEST — THE 16-COLOR PALETTE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr colortest\\
\end{codebox}

Displays the basic 16-color ANSI palette. The foundation. Black, red, green, yellow, blue, magenta, cyan, white — plus their bright variants. 16 soldiers in formation.

Use this to verify your terminal theme is sane. If red looks orange, your theme needs fixing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COLORTEST256 — THE FULL SPECTRUM}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr colortest256\\
\end{codebox}

Displays all 256 extended colors. A grid of every color code your terminal supports. From 0 to 255. Useful for picking colors for prompts and scripts.

"What number is that teal?" Run this and find out.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PYGMENTCOLORS — SYNTAX HIGHLIGHTING PALETTE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr pygmentcolors\\
\end{codebox}

Shows available Pygments color schemes. Pygments is the syntax highlighting engine used by many \textbf{ZPWR} tools. Pick a scheme that matches your aesthetic.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRETTYPRINT \& ALTPRETTYPRINT — FORMATTED OUTPUT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr prettyprint <file>\\
zpwr altprettyprint <file>\\
\end{codebox}

Syntax-highlighted file display. Prettyprint uses one engine, altprettyprint uses another. Both turn raw source code into technicolor glory in your terminal.

Like cat, but for people who respect their eyes.

COLOR EXPLORATION WORKFLOW: 1. zpwr colortest        check basic 16 colors 2. zpwr colortest256     explore full 256 palette 3. zpwr pygmentcolors    pick a syntax theme 4. zpwr prettyprint      view files in color.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // monochrome terminals are a war crime}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // color is data. learn to read it.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // 256 colors and you are still using white-on-black?}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Script Management}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} SCRIPT MANAGEMENT — THE FORGE}\vspace{6pt}

Create scripts. Count scripts. Print scripts to PDF. Ship scripts.

\textbf{ZPWR} manages its own script collection and gives you tools to create new ones, inventory them, and even export them to PDF for documentation or review.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SCRIPTNEW — THE SCRIPT GENERATOR}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scriptnew <name>\\
\end{codebox}

Creates a new script with proper boilerplate. Shebang, error handling, argument parsing — all pre-wired. Stop writing boilerplate. Start writing logic.

The script lands in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}, already executable, already on your \textbf{PATH}. Zero friction from idea to tool.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SCRIPTTOPDF — THE DOCUMENTER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scripttopdf\\
\end{codebox}

Converts scripts to syntax-highlighted PDFs. For code reviews, documentation, or printing out and framing on your wall like the art it is.

Send your code to a printer. Assert dominance.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SCRIPTCOUNT — THE CENSUS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scriptcount\\
\end{codebox}

How many scripts live in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}? This tells you. Track the growth of your personal armory over time.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SCRIPTLIST — THE INVENTORY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr scriptlist\\
\end{codebox}

Lists every script in the collection. Names, paths, the full manifest of your executable arsenal.

THE SCRIPT LIFECYCLE: 1. zpwr scriptnew myutil     create the script 2. zpwr vimscriptedit        edit scripts with vim/fzf 3. zpwr scriptcount          check your inventory 4. zpwr scriptlist           browse the collection 5. zpwr scripttopdf          export for documentation.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ALSO RELATED:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr makedir <name>     create a directory\\
zpwr makefile <name>    create a file\\
\end{codebox}

Simple creation verbs. No boilerplate, just mkdir/touch with \textbf{ZPWR}'s logging and validation baked in.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // a script unwritten is a problem unsolved}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // a script uncounted is a tool forgotten}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the forge never closes. the armory always grows.}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{EXERCISM \& EXTERNAL}

\section{Exercism Integration}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EXERCISM INTEGRATION — LEVEL UP YOUR CODE}\vspace{6pt}

Practice makes perfect. \textbf{ZPWR} makes practice frictionless.

Exercism.org is a free coding practice platform with mentor-reviewed exercises in 50+ languages. \textbf{ZPWR} wraps the exercism CLI so you never leave your terminal.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXERCISM — THE LAUNCHER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr exercism\\
\end{codebox}

The base exercism command integration. Manages your exercism workspace, configuration, and submission workflow from within \textbf{ZPWR}.

Your exercism workspace lives under \$\textbf{ZPWR} and is accessible via zpwr homeexercism for quick navigation.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXERCISMDOWNLOAD — FETCH EXERCISES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr exercismdownload\\
\end{codebox}

Downloads an exercise to your local workspace. The exercise appears, ready to solve. Tests included. No browser clicking. No manual downloads.

The workflow: 1. Browse exercises on exercism.org 2. zpwr exercismdownload to fetch it 3. Solve the exercise in your editor 4. Run the tests locally 5. Submit when you pass.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXERCISMEDIT — SOLVE IN YOUR \textbf{EDITOR}}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr exercismedit\\
\end{codebox}

Opens exercism exercises directly in your configured editor. Download, edit, test, submit — all from the terminal. The browser is optional.

WHY EXERCISM + \textbf{ZPWR}:

The exercism CLI is good. \textbf{ZPWR} makes it seamless. No context switching. No browser tabs. No friction.

Languages supported by exercism: Python, Go, Rust, Elixir, JavaScript, TypeScript, C, C++, Java, Ruby, Haskell, Clojure, Kotlin, Swift, Bash, and 35+ more.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // coding practice should be as easy as}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // opening a terminal and typing three words}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // zpwr exercismdownload. done. now solve.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Travis CI \& External Services}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TRAVIS CI \& EXTERNAL SERVICES}\vspace{6pt}

CI/CD from your terminal, not your browser.

\textbf{ZPWR} integrates with external services so you can monitor builds, check statuses, and manage CI/CD pipelines without opening a single browser tab.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRAVIS — THE CI DASHBOARD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr travis\\
\end{codebox}

Travis CI integration. View build status, logs, and pipeline information from your terminal.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRAVISBUILD — BUILD STATUS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr travisbuild\\
\end{codebox}

Check the current build status. Green, red, or pending. No need to refresh a web page in a loop.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRAVISBRANCH — BRANCH BUILDS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr travisbranch\\
\end{codebox}

View build status for a specific branch. When you push to a feature branch and want to know if CI passed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRAVISPR — PULL REQUEST BUILDS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr travispr\\
\end{codebox}

Check build status for pull requests. The merge button is only as good as the CI behind it.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} OTHER EXTERNAL INTEGRATIONS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr goclean          clean Go build cache\\
zpwr google <query>    search Google from terminal\\
zpwr digs <domain>     DNS lookups\\
zpwr torip            check your Tor IP address\\
zpwr toriprenew       renew your Tor circuit\\
zpwr curl             enhanced curl operations\\
\end{codebox}

THE EXTERNAL SERVICES PHILOSOPHY: The browser is a context switch. Every tab you open is a distraction vector. \textbf{ZPWR} brings the services to you, in the terminal, where you already are.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the terminal is the cockpit}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // external services are instruments on the dashboard}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // you read them. you do not visit them.}

\chapter{TIPS, TRICKS \& EASTER EGGS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_hack_the_planet.png}
\end{center}

\section{The One-Letter Verbs}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE ONE-LETTER VERBS — MAXIMUM POWER, MINIMUM KEYSTROKES}\vspace{6pt}

Six letters that do the work of six paragraphs.

\textbf{ZPWR} has \$\{\#\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}\} verbs. Six of them are single characters. These are not abbreviations — they are the distilled essence of the most common operations. One keystroke each. The speed limit of human-shell interaction.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} a — AUTOLOAD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr a\\
\end{codebox}

Quick access to the autoload system. When you need to check on autoloaded functions fast.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} c — CAT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr c\\
\end{codebox}

The cat operation. View file contents. The most basic read operation, compressed to one letter.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} e — EDIT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr e\\
\end{codebox}

Open your editor. The gateway to modification. Uses your configured \$\textbf{EDITOR}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} f — FILESEARCH}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr f\\
\end{codebox}

File search. Find things. The single most common operation after listing and editing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} r — REPLAY}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr r\\
\end{codebox}

Replay operations. Redo what you did before. History is not just for reading.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} z — ZCD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr z\\
\end{codebox}

Z-jump. Frecency-based directory navigation. Type a partial path, z figures out the rest. The single most addictive feature in \textbf{ZPWR}.

THE ONE-LETTER ARSENAL: a   autoload system c   cat / view files e   edit files f   file search r   replay z   z-jump directory navigation.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // every extra character is latency between thought and action}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // one letter is the event horizon of efficiency}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // below this, there is only telepathy}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Alias Chains}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ALIAS CHAINS — COMPOUND OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

One verb triggers another. The cascade begins.

\textbf{ZPWR} verbs do not exist in isolation. They chain. They compose. They call each other. This is not an accident — it is the architecture.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SHORTHAND ALIASES:}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} is aliased to shorter forms for speed:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr   the full command\\
zp     shorter\\
zpz    alternate short form\\
\end{codebox}

All three are equivalent:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr help  ==  zp help  ==  zpz help\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMPOUND VERB PATTERNS:}\vspace{4pt}

Many verbs are compounds that do two things:

Filesearch + edit     = zpwr filesearchedit findsearch + edit     = zpwr findsearchedit locatesearch + edit   = zpwr locatesearchedit wordsearch + edit     = zpwr wordsearchedit vimrecent + cd        = zpwr vimrecentcd vimrecent + sudo      = zpwr vimrecentsudo.

The pattern: action + modifier = compound verb Search, then edit. Browse, then cd. Open, then sudo.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE CLEAN CHAIN:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr cleanall             clean everything\\
zpwr cleancache           clean caches only\\
zpwr cleancompcache       clean completion cache\\
zpwr cleangitcache        clean git repo cache\\
zpwr cleangitcleancache   clean git clean cache\\
zpwr cleangitdirtycache   clean git dirty cache\\
zpwr cleantemp            clean temp files\\
zpwr cleanrefreshupdate   clean + refresh + update\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE GIT CHAIN:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr gitforalldir     run git cmd in all dirs\\
zpwr gitfordirmain    run git cmd in dirs on main\\
zpwr gitzfordir       z-style git for all dirs\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // verbs are words. chains are sentences.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the grammar of \textbf{ZPWR} is combinatorial.}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // learn the words, and the sentences write themselves.}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The Token System}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE TOKEN SYSTEM — YOUR PRIVATE OVERRIDE LAYER}\vspace{6pt}

Secrets, aliases, and local config that never touch git.

\textbf{ZPWR} has sensible defaults. But you are not sensible. You have API keys, custom aliases, machine-specific paths, and opinions. The token system is where all of that lives — safely outside version control.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE TOKEN FILES:}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh} Your main tokens file. Aliases, exports, secrets. This is sourced during shell init. Gitignored.

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens-pre.sh} Sourced BEFORE \textbf{ZPWR}'s main configuration. Override defaults before they are set. Set \textbf{ZPWR}\_* variables here to change behavior.

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens-post.sh} Sourced AFTER \textbf{ZPWR}'s main configuration. Override anything \textbf{ZPWR} set. Final word. Your aliases here trump everything.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE OVERRIDE PATTERN:}\vspace{4pt}

Want to change a \textbf{ZPWR} default? Do not edit \textbf{ZPWR} source. Put it in tokens-pre.sh (before) or tokens-post.sh (after).

Example: override in tokens-pre.sh.

\begin{codebox}
export ZPWR\_EXA\_COMMAND=eza\\
export ZPWR\_KEYTIMEOUT=1\\
export ZPWR\_BANNER\_CLEARLIST=false\\
\end{codebox}

Example: add custom aliases in tokens-post.sh.

\begin{codebox}
alias k=kubectl\\
alias tf=terraform\\
export GITHUB\_TOKEN=ghp\_xxxxx\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MANAGING TOKENS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr tokens           view your tokens files\\
zpwr vimtokens        edit tokens in vim\\
zpwr emacstokens      edit tokens in emacs\\
\end{codebox}

THE LOAD ORDER: 1. Tokens-pre.sh      (your early overrides) 2. \textbf{ZPWR} core config    (the framework defaults) 3. Tokens-post.sh     (your final overrides) 4. .tokens.sh         (your secrets and aliases)

NEVER commit token files. They are gitignored for a reason. API keys in git history haunt you forever.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the token system is your private dimension}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // \textbf{ZPWR} updates without touching your config}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your overrides survive every git pull}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{FZF Everywhere}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FZF EVERYWHERE — THE FUZZY FINDER CONSPIRACY}\vspace{6pt}

fzf is not a tool. It is a religion. \textbf{ZPWR} is its cathedral.

fzf (fuzzy finder) is woven into the fabric of \textbf{ZPWR}. Dozens of verbs pipe their output through fzf for interactive selection. If a verb ends in 'fzf', it is the fuzzy-interactive variant. But many others use it too.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DEDICATED FZF VERBS:}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr verbs     browse subcommands in fzf\\
zpwr verbs           browse all verbs in fzf\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VERBS THAT USE FZF INTERNALLY:}\vspace{4pt}

Forgit* verbs: forgitadd, forgitdiff, forgitlog, forgitstash, forgitreset, forgitrestore, forgitclean All use fzf for interactive selection with previews.

Search + edit verbs: filesearchedit, findsearchedit, locatesearchedit, wordsearchedit — search with fzf, open selection.

Recent file verbs: vimrecent, vimrecentcd, emacsrecent, emacsrecentcd Browse recent files with fzf preview.

Editor verbs: vimalledit, emacsalledit — select files to edit.

Git tag verbs: vimgtagsedit, emacsgtagsedit — browse tags in fzf.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FZF PATTERN:}\vspace{4pt}

The pattern is always the same: 1. Generate a list of candidates 2. Pipe through fzf with a preview command 3. Take the selection and act on it.

This pattern turns every list into a menu. Every menu into a decision. Every decision into action.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF CUSTOMIZATION:}\vspace{4pt}

Set FZF\_DEFAULT\_OPTS in your tokens file:

\begin{codebox}
export FZF\_DEFAULT\_OPTS='--height 80\% --reverse --border'\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // if you can list it, you can fzf it}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // if you can fzf it, you can act on it}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // fzf is the universal adapter between data and decisions}


\vspace{16pt}

\vspace{16pt}

\section{All Verbs T-Z}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ALL \$\{\#ZPWR\_VERBS\} VERBS: refreshzwc through zstyle (3/3)}\vspace{6pt}

The final stretch. The end of the alphabet. The beginning of mastery.

Refreshzwc                 refresh compiled wordcode regen                      regenerate single component regenall                   regenerate everything regenconfiglinks           regenerate config symlinks regenctags                 regenerate ctags regenenvcache              regenerate env cache regengitdirtyrepocache     regen dirty repo cache regengitrepocache          regen git repo cache regengtags                 regenerate gtags regengtagspygments         regen gtags + pygments regengtagstype             regen gtags by type regenhistory               regenerate history regenkeybindings           regen keybindings regenpowerline             regenerate powerline regenzsh                   regenerate zsh config reload                     reload shell config relpath                    get relative path rename                     rename files replacer                   find and replace replay                     replay commands repo                       repository info reset                      reset shell state restart                    restart shell restore                    restore configuration restorehistory             restore history backup return2                    return to previous dir reveal                     open repo in browser revealrecurse              reveal recursively reverse                    reverse input rmconfiglinks              remove config symlinks rvs                        reverse shortcut scriptcount                count scripts scriptlist                 list all scripts scriptnew                  create new script scripts                    browse scripts scripttopdf                convert script to PDF testsall                   run all env tests (alias: testall) upload                     upload file with curl verbsedit                  edit verb selection zpwrCloneToForked          clone zpwr to forked dir zpwrgithub                 open zpwr GitHub page.

Run zpwr verbs for the complete live list.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{POWERLEVEL10K \& INSTANT PROMPT}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_neural_link.png}
\end{center}

\section{Instant Prompt: Zero Latency}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} INSTANT PROMPT: ZERO LATENCY}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your prompt loads before .zshrc finishes. time is a flat circle. //}

The single best UX trick in zpwr: the prompt appears INSTANTLY while .zshrc is still loading behind the curtain. Zero perceived latency. The illusion of speed IS speed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

On previous shell exit, p10k cached the prompt to a file:

\begin{codebox}
\{XDG\_CACHE\_HOME:-HOME/.cache\}/p10k-instant-prompt-\{(\%):-\%n\}.zsh\\
\end{codebox}

This file contains the last rendered prompt as raw ANSI escape sequences. On next shell start, it is sourced BEFORE .zshrc loads. Result: the prompt appears in <10ms. The user can start typing. .zshrc continues loading in the background. Nobody notices.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE LOADING SEQUENCE IN .zshrc}\vspace{4pt}

1. zmodload zsh/datetime (timestamp) 2. Source zpwr-hash-dirs.zsh (cached hash -d entries) 3. Source p10k-instant-prompt-*.zsh (the magic file) 4. promptTimestamp=\$EPOCHREALTIME (measure prompt time) 5. ...rest of .zshrc loads (plugins, aliases, etc)... 6. p10k finalize (at very end of .zshrc) Step 3 must come before ANY console output. If something prints to stdout before the instant prompt, p10k aborts it.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE HASH DIR CACHE}\vspace{4pt}

The prompt uses \%\textasciitilde{} to show named dirs like \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR} instead of /Users/you/.zpwr. But hash -d entries are set in zpwrBindDirs, which runs AFTER instant prompt. Chicken-and-egg problem. Solution: zpwrBindDirs writes a cache file:

\begin{codebox}
ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr-hash-dirs.zsh\\
\end{codebox}

This cache is sourced at line 73 of .zshrc, BEFORE instant prompt, so \%\textasciitilde{} resolves \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR} in the cached prompt correctly.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR\_BANNER}\_CLEARLIST SUPPRESSION}\vspace{4pt}

ZpwrClearList prints a banner + file listing on login. But if it runs before instant prompt finalizes, it corrupts the display. Fix: the banner is deferred until AFTER p10k finalize:

\begin{codebox}
if [[ -n "\_\_p9k\_instant\_prompt\_active" \&\& "ZPWR\_BANNER\_CLEARLIST" == true ]]; then\\
zpwrClearList || true\\
fi\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE promptTimestamp MEASUREMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR\_VARS}[promptTimeMs] records how fast the prompt appeared. Run zpwr environmentcounts to see it. With instant prompt, this is typically 0.010-0.050 seconds. Without it, 0.5-2.0s.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} p10k finalize}\vspace{4pt}

The last line of .zshrc:

\begin{codebox}
(( ! \{+functions[p10k]\} )) || p10k finalize\\
\end{codebox}

This tells p10k that .zshrc is done. The real prompt replaces the cached one. Any deferred output can now safely print.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Powerlevel10k Configuration}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} POWERLEVEL10K CONFIGURATION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the prompt is your cockpit HUD. configure it or fly blind. //}

The p10k config lives at \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.p10k.zsh} -- a 1700+ line file that controls every pixel of your prompt. The wizard generated it, but you own it now.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LEFT \textbf{PROMPT} SEGMENTS}\vspace{4pt}

These appear on the left side, top to bottom across 4 lines:

\begin{codebox}
Line 1: custom\_env, custom\_env\_bat, custom\_env\_editor\\
Line 2: custom\_env\_term, ip\\
Line 3: context, dir, history, ssh, root\_indicator, status, vcs\\
Line 4: custom\_tty, prompt\_char\\
\end{codebox}

The dir segment shows your current directory using \%\textasciitilde{} expansion. The vcs segment shows git branch, dirty status, ahead/behind. The prompt\_char shows green on success, red on failure.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RIGHT \textbf{PROMPT} SEGMENTS}\vspace{4pt}

The right side is information-dense. Across 3 lines:

\begin{codebox}
Line 1: dir\_writable, background\_jobs, virtualenv, node\_version,\\
\end{codebox}

go\_version, rust\_version, java\_version, package, os\_icon...

\begin{codebox}
Line 2: load, disk\_usage, ram, swap, wifi\\
Line 3: battery, time, command\_execution\_time, vi\_mode, custom\_pid\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE DIR SEGMENT AND \%\textasciitilde{} EXPANSION}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh's \%\textasciitilde{} prompt expansion replaces known paths with named dirs. If you hash -d \textbf{ZPWR}=/Users/you/.zpwr, then /Users/you/.zpwr shows as \textasciitilde{}\textbf{ZPWR} in your prompt. Shorter. Cleaner. Professional. POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_MAX\_LENGTH=80 caps the dir segment width. POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_MIN\_COMMAND\_COLUMNS=40 reserves typing space.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW zpwrBindDirs POPULATES NAMED DIRECTORIES}\vspace{4pt}

The function runs hash -d for \textasciitilde{}20 directories:

\begin{codebox}
hash -d ZPWR="ZPWR"\\
hash -d ZPWR\_TMUX="ZPWR\_TMUX"\\
hash -d ZPWR\_SCRIPTS="ZPWR\_SCRIPTS"\\
hash -d TMUXH="TMUX\_HOME"\\
\end{codebox}

Then it writes them all to \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/zpwr-hash-dirs.zsh} for instant prompt to use on next startup. The cache iterates \$\{(kv)nameddirs\} to capture every registered named directory.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE MULTILINE FRAME}\vspace{4pt}

The prompt uses box-drawing characters for the frame:

\begin{codebox}
First line:  \%244F-----  (top connector)\\
Middle:      \%244F-----  (mid connector)\\
Last line:   \%244F-----  (bottom connector)\\
\end{codebox}

Gap fill character is '---' in grey (color 244). Mode: nerdfont-complete. Separators use Powerline glyphs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} prompt\_char SYMBOLS}\vspace{4pt}

Insert mode: ---  Command mode: ---  Visual: V  Overwrite: ---


\vspace{16pt}

\section{P10k Prompt Customization}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} P10K PROMPT CUSTOMIZATION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your prompt is a canvas. paint it with ANSI and Unicode. //}

\textbf{ZPWR} ships custom p10k segments beyond the defaults. These are defined with POWERLEVEL9K\_CUSTOM\_* variables in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.p10k.zsh.}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CUSTOM SEGMENTS}\vspace{4pt}

Custom\_tty       Shows \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TTY} (e.g. \textbf{\textbf{/dev/ttys003}}) Defined as:

\begin{codebox}
POWERLEVEL9K\_CUSTOM\_TTY='echo "\%K\{blue\}\%F\{white\} ZPWR\_TTY \%f\%k\%F\{blue\}\%f"'\\
\end{codebox}

Custom\_env       Environment info (custom shell, OS, etc.) custom\_env\_bat   Shows bat (syntax highlighter) version/status custom\_env\_editor Shows \$\textbf{EDITOR} custom\_env\_term  Shows \$\textbf{TERM}\_PROGRAM custom\_pid       Shows shell PID (right prompt, bottom line)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ADDING YOUR OWN SEGMENT}\vspace{4pt}

1. Define the command that generates output:

\begin{codebox}
typeset -g POWERLEVEL9K\_CUSTOM\_MYWIDGET='echo "hello"'\\
\end{codebox}

2. Set colors:

\begin{codebox}
typeset -g POWERLEVEL9K\_CUSTOM\_MYWIDGET\_FOREGROUND=255\\
typeset -g POWERLEVEL9K\_CUSTOM\_MYWIDGET\_BACKGROUND=24\\
\end{codebox}

3. Add 'custom\_mywidget' to LEFT or RIGHT \textbf{PROMPT}\_ELEMENTS array. 4. Run p10k reload or source \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.p10k.zsh} to apply.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COLORS AND ICONS}\vspace{4pt}

P10k uses 256-color indices. Print the color map:

\begin{codebox}
for i in \{0..255\}; do print -Pn "\%K\{i\}  \%k\%F\{i\}\{(l:3::0:)i\}\%f "; done\\
\end{codebox}

Foreground/background for each segment and state:

\begin{codebox}
POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_BACKGROUND=4        (blue)\\
POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_FOREGROUND=254      (near-white)\\
\end{codebox}

Anchor dirs (like .git roots) get bold + brighter color (255).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRUNCATION}\vspace{4pt}

POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_MAX\_LENGTH=80 caps directory width. POWERLEVEL9K\_DIR\_MIN\_COMMAND\_COLUMNS\_PCT=50 means at least half the terminal width is reserved for your command input. SHORTEN\_STRATEGY can be set to truncate\_to\_unique for smart shortening (commented out by default in zpwr).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE .p10k.zsh FILE STRUCTURE}\vspace{4pt}

The file is a single anonymous function () \{ ... \} that: - Unsets all POWERLEVEL9K\_* vars (clean slate) - Defines LEFT/RIGHT\_PROMPT\_ELEMENTS arrays - Sets mode, icons, colors, truncation per segment - Configures transient prompt, instant prompt, hot reload


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SEEING \textbf{PROMPT} TIMING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr environmentcounts\\
\end{codebox}

Shows promptTimeMs, startupTimeMs, and per-phase timestamps. This is how you prove your prompt loaded in 0.015 seconds.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Transient Prompt \& Visual Tweaks}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TRANSIENT PROMPT \& VISUAL TWEAKS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // old prompts fade into ghosts. only the present matters. //}

Transient prompt collapses previous prompts to a minimal form, keeping your scrollback clean and focused. In zpwr it is currently set to 'off' but easy to enable.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TRANSIENT \textbf{PROMPT}}\vspace{4pt}

When enabled, after you press Enter, the full multi-line prompt collapses to just the prompt\_char symbol (--- or ---). All segments, git status, directory -- gone from old lines. Only the current prompt shows full detail.

\begin{codebox}
typeset -g POWERLEVEL9K\_TRANSIENT\_PROMPT=off\\
\end{codebox}

Change to always or same-dir to enable. 'same-dir' only collapses when you stay in the same directory.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOT RELOAD}\vspace{4pt}

Edit .p10k.zsh and apply changes without restarting zsh:

\begin{codebox}
p10k reload\\
\end{codebox}

This re-sources .p10k.zsh and rebuilds the prompt in-place. No exec zsh needed. No losing your command history buffer.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} p10k configure}\vspace{4pt}

The interactive configuration wizard. Walks you through: - Font detection (powerline glyphs, nerdfont icons) - Style selection (rainbow, lean, classic, pure) - Separator shapes, line count, frame options - Instant prompt mode (verbose, quiet, off) It generates a fresh .p10k.zsh. \textbf{ZPWR} ships a pre-configured one in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.p10k.zsh} (rainbow style, nerdfont-complete). Running the wizard overwrites it, so back up yours first.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INSTANT \textbf{PROMPT} vs zpwrClearList}\vspace{4pt}

The banner (zpwrClearList) prints colorized file listings on login. Instant prompt redirects all stdout/stderr during init to a buffer. If zpwrClearList runs during this window, the output gets swallowed or garbled. \textbf{ZPWR}'s fix: 1. Check if \_\_p9k\_instant\_prompt\_active is set 2. If so, defer zpwrClearList to AFTER p10k finalize 3. The banner appears cleanly after the prompt settles.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE eza --classify=always FIX}\vspace{4pt}

Under instant prompt, fd/stdout gets redirected. eza's --classify flag (append */ to dirs) auto-detects terminal capabilities. When stdout is redirected, it thinks there's no terminal and drops the indicators. Fix: use --classify=always to force classification regardless of fd state. \textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND uses this:

\begin{codebox}
command eza --git -il --classify=always -H --color-scale -g -a\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$TTY IMMUNE TO FD REDIRECTS}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh sets \$TTY before rc files load. Instant prompt redirects fds 0/1/2, but \$TTY still points to the real terminal. \textbf{ZPWR} captures it: export \textbf{ZPWR\_TTY}=\$TTY This is used by the custom\_tty prompt segment.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{ZINIT \& PLUGIN SYSTEM}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_system_core.png}
\end{center}

\section{Zinit: The Plugin Engine}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ZINIT: THE PLUGIN ENGINE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the package manager for your shell. apt-get for zsh plugins. //}

Zinit (formerly zplugin) is a flexible zsh plugin manager with turbo mode, ice modifiers, and completion management. \textbf{ZPWR} uses it as the default: \textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER=zinit.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT ZINIT DOES}\vspace{4pt}

- Downloads GitHub plugins to \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER\_HOME - Sources them into your shell (or loads as completions) - Manages load order with turbo mode (deferred loading) - Handles OMZ snippets (individual files from oh-my-zsh) - Compiles plugins to .zwc bytecode for speed


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ZINIT LOAD vs ZINIT SNIPPET}\vspace{4pt}

zinit load user/repo      Clone full GitHub repo, source .plugin.zsh zinit snippet OMZP::git    Download single file from OMZ plugin zinit snippet OMZL::git.zsh Download single OMZ lib file load = full repo clone. Snippet = single file download. \textbf{ZPWR} uses both: load for GitHub plugins, snippet for OMZ.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PLUGIN ARRAYS}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} organizes plugins into arrays in .zshrc: \textbf{ZPWR\_GH}\_PLUGINS   28+ GitHub repos (fzf-tab, zsh-z, forgit...) \textbf{ZPWR\_OMZ}\_PLUGINS  25+ OMZ snippets (git, python, golang...) \textbf{ZPWR\_OMZ}\_LIBS     4 OMZ lib files (git.zsh, directories.zsh...) \textbf{ZPWR\_OMZ}\_COMPS    12 OMZ completion files (ng, coffee, scala...) Conditional plugins are appended if the command exists:

\begin{codebox}
zpwrCommandExists docker \&\& ZPWR\_GH\_PLUGINS+=( zsh-docker-aliases )\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PLUGIN STORAGE}\vspace{4pt}

Everything lives under \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER\_HOME:

\begin{codebox}
ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER\_HOME/bin/         zinit itself\\
ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER\_HOME/plugins/     cloned repos\\
ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER\_HOME/snippets/    OMZ snippets\\
ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER\_HOME/completions/  symlinked completions\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE 48+ PLUGIN ROSTER}\vspace{4pt}

Total plugins managed by zpwr (from .zshplugins): MenkeTechnologies forks: fzf-tab, forgit, zsh-expand, zsh-autocomplete, zsh-z, fasd-simple, revolver, zunit... zdharma-continuum: fast-syntax-highlighting, zbrowse, zui... zsh-users: completions, autosuggestions, history-substring Plus: hlissner/zsh-autopair, bigH/git-fuzzy, and more.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FIRST RUN BOOTSTRAP}\vspace{4pt}

If \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER\_HOME/bin doesn't exist, .zshrc auto-clones zinit from GitHub. After that, OPTIMIZE\_OUT\_DISK\_ACCESSES=1 skips unnecessary stat() calls on subsequent starts.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Turbo Mode: Deferred Loading}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TURBO MODE: DEFERRED LOADING}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // why load 48 plugins at startup when you can load 1? //}

Turbo mode is zinit's killer feature: plugins load AFTER the prompt appears, using zsh's sched mechanism. Your shell is interactive instantly; plugins trickle in behind the scenes.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE wait ICE MODIFIER}\vspace{4pt}

The 'wait' ice defers loading until after the first prompt:

\begin{codebox}
zinit ice wait            Load after prompt (default priority)\\
zinit ice wait'0'         Same as wait (priority 0)\\
zinit ice wait'0a'        Priority 0, sub-order a (loads first)\\
zinit ice wait'0b'        Priority 0, sub-order b (loads second)\\
zinit ice wait'0e'        Priority 0, sub-order e (loads later)\\
\end{codebox}

The letter suffix controls load order WITHIN the same priority. A loads before b loads before c, etc. This is how \textbf{ZPWR} orchestrates 48 plugins into a precise loading sequence.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE GRADUATED LOADING SEQUENCE}\vspace{4pt}

Synchronous:  powerlevel10k (must load before prompt) wait:          OMZ completions, libs, plugins, GH plugins wait'0':       zsh-autopair (needs ZLE ready) wait'0a':      zsh-expand + late aliases + alias cache wait'0b':      zsh-autocomplete (if \textbf{ZPWR\_AUTO}\_COMPLETE=true) wait'0c':      zsh-history-substring-search wait'0d':      zsh-autosuggestions + FZF late bindings + verbs wait'0e':      zsh-zinit-final (penultimate, final keybindings) wait'0f':      zsh-more-completions (low priority, last resort) wait'\$\{\textbf{ZPWR\_ZINIT}\_COMPINIT\_DELAY\}g':  compinit + cdreplay wait'\$\{\textbf{ZPWR\_ZINIT}\_COMPINIT\_DELAY\}h':  fast-syntax-highlighting.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY STARTUP IS FAST}\vspace{4pt}

Only ONE plugin loads synchronously: powerlevel10k. Everything else is turbo-deferred. Combined with instant prompt, you see the prompt in \textasciitilde{}15ms. The other 47 plugins load in the next \textasciitilde{}200ms while you're still thinking.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} atinit AND atload HOOKS}\vspace{4pt}

These run code at precise moments during plugin loading: atinit   Runs BEFORE the plugin is sourced atload   Runs AFTER the plugin is sourced \textbf{ZPWR} uses these extensively to bind keymaps and aliases:

\begin{codebox}
zinit ice wait'0a' atload'zpwrBindAliasesLate; zpwrCreateAliasCache'\\
\end{codebox}

The atload fires after zsh-expand is sourced, then late aliases override any OMZ defaults. Precise surgical timing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR\_ZINIT}\_COMPINIT\_DELAY}\vspace{4pt}

Default: 0. Controls how long to wait before running compinit. Higher values defer completion system init even further. Set in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.zpwr\_env.sh.} The 'g' and 'h' suffixes on the wait ice ensure compinit and syntax highlighting load last.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Ice Modifiers Deep Dive}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ICE MODIFIERS DEEP DIVE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // ice modifiers are the spice of zinit. season generously. //}

Every zinit load/snippet can be preceded by 'zinit ice' with modifiers that control HOW the plugin loads. Ice melts after one use -- it applies only to the next command.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CORE ICE MODIFIERS USED BY \textbf{ZPWR}}\vspace{4pt}

Lucid          Suppress the 'Loaded plugin' message \textbf{ZPWR} uses this on every plugin. No spam. Nocompile      Skip .zwc compilation of the plugin Useful when zpwrRecompileFiles handles it globally. Nocd           Don't cd to plugin dir before sourcing Prevents p10k internals from polluting named dirs. Nocompletions  Don't install completions from this plugin Used for zsh-more-completions (fpath-only approach). As'completion' Load the file as a completion, not a plugin Used for OMZ completion snippets like \_ng, \_coffee. As'program'    Add the pick'd file to \$\textbf{PATH} as executable Used for fzf binary and git-fuzzy. As'null'       Don't source anything -- just run hooks Used for the compinit phase (atinit only). Pick'null'     Don't source any file from the plugin dir Used with as'completion' for OMZ comp snippets. Pick'bin/fzf'  Source or add this specific file With as'program', adds bin/fzf to \textbf{PATH}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOOK ICE MODIFIERS}\vspace{4pt}

Atinit         Code to run BEFORE the plugin sources atload         Code to run AFTER the plugin sources Example from \textbf{ZPWR}:

\begin{codebox}
zinit ice lucid nocompile wait atinit='zpwrBindOverrideOMZ;zpwrBindForGit'\\
zinit load MenkeTechnologies/forgit\\
\end{codebox}

Before forgit loads, zpwrBindOverrideOMZ runs to clear OMZ aliases that would conflict with forgit's git shortcuts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW \textbf{ZPWR} USES ICE BY CATEGORY}\vspace{4pt}

OMZ completions: lucid nocompile as'completion' pick'null' wait OMZ libs:        lucid nocompile wait atload='zpwrOmzOverrides' OMZ plugins:     lucid nocompile wait GH plugins:      lucid nocompile wait Programs:        as'program' lucid nocompile pick'bin/fzf' wait Syntax highlight:lucid nocompile wait"\$\{\textbf{ZPWR\_ZINIT}\_COMPINIT\_DELAY\}h"


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ICE STACKING}\vspace{4pt}

Multiple ices combine. Order doesn't matter (mostly). The = syntax for atload/atinit works with single quotes:

\begin{codebox}
atload='zpwrBindAliasesLate; zpwrCreateAliasCache'\\
\end{codebox}

Or with quoted strings:

\begin{codebox}
atinit'zpwrBindZdharma' atload'zpwrBindZdharmaPost'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Zinit Completions \& Compinit}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ZINIT COMPLETIONS \& COMPINIT}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // compinit is the most expensive call in zsh startup. defer it. //}

The completion system (compsys) needs compinit to initialize. But compinit scans all fpath entries and parses completion files. On a system with many registered completions, this is slow. \textbf{ZPWR} defers it behind turbo mode.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ZINIT COMPINIT OPTIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
ZINIT[COMPINIT\_OPTS]='-C -u'\\
\end{codebox}

-C skips security checks (don't re-verify file ownership). -u suppresses warnings about insecure directories. These flags make compinit fast but trust your fpath.

\begin{codebox}
ZINIT[ZCOMPDUMP\_PATH]="ZSH\_COMPDUMP"\\
\end{codebox}

Points compinit to zpwr's zcompdump location.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE DEFERRED COMPINIT}\vspace{4pt}

Compinit runs in the LAST turbo phase:

\begin{codebox}
zinit ice lucid nocompile nocd as'null'\\
wait"\{ZPWR\_ZINIT\_COMPINIT\_DELAY\}g"\\
atinit'zicompinit; zicdreplay; zpwrBindOverrideOMZCompdefs'\\
\end{codebox}

Zicompinit: runs compinit with the configured opts. Zicdreplay: replays all compdef calls that were recorded while compinit wasn't loaded yet. Plugins that called compdef during turbo load had their calls queued -- now they fire.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE OMZ COMPLETION SYMLINK HACK}\vspace{4pt}

OMZ completion snippets land in:

\begin{codebox}
ZSH/snippets/OMZP::coffee/\_coffee\\
\end{codebox}

But compinit needs them in the completions dir. \textbf{ZPWR} symlinks them using zsh/files builtin:

\begin{codebox}
zmodload -F zsh/files b:zf\_ln\\
zf\_ln -sfn ZSH/snippets/OMZP::\{p\%/*\}/... ZSH/completions/...\\
\end{codebox}

Zf\_ln is a builtin ln(1), no fork needed. Fast.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwrStaleZcompdump}\vspace{4pt}

The zcompdump cache gets stale when plugins update. zpwrStaleZcompdump checks the cache age. If it is older than \textasciitilde{}1 week, it triggers a full compinit rebuild. This runs automatically every shell startup (cheap check).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FORCE REBUILD}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr regenzsh\\
\end{codebox}

Nukes the zcompdump, re-runs compinit from scratch, and recompiles everything. Use when completions go missing or after adding new completion files to fpath.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR\_ZINIT}\_COMPINIT\_DELAY}\vspace{4pt}

Default: 0. Set in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_ENV/.zpwr\_env.sh.} Controls the numeric prefix of the wait ice for compinit. 0 = load immediately after prompt. Higher = more delay. On fast machines, 0 is fine. On slow machines, 1 or 2 lets other plugins finish first.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Plugin Troubleshooting}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PLUGIN TROUBLESHOOTING}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // when plugins misbehave, you need diagnostic tools. //}

Zinit ships with built-in debugging commands. When something breaks, these are your first responders.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DIAGNOSTIC COMMANDS}\vspace{4pt}

zinit report <plugin>      Show load status, errors, files.

\begin{codebox}
zinit report zsh-users/zsh-autosuggestions\\
\end{codebox}

Shows: source file, ice modifiers used, compdef calls, functions defined, aliases created, \textbf{PATH} changes. zinit times               Show plugin load times Sorted by load time. Find the slowest plugin. If something takes >50ms, investigate. zinit loaded              List all loaded plugins zinit unload <plugin>      Unload a plugin from current session Useful for debugging conflicts. Does not persist. zinit list                List all registered plugins zinit cd <plugin>          cd into plugin directory.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMMON ISSUES}\vspace{4pt}

Plugin not loading? - Check if 'wait' ice is set (turbo mode defers loading) - Run zinit report to see if it errored silently - The 'lucid' ice suppresses messages -- remove it to debug Completion missing after plugin install? - Run zpwr regenzsh to rebuild zcompdump - Check fpath: print -l \$fpath | grep <plugin> - Ensure the plugin uses as'completion' or adds to fpath Keybinding conflicts? - Load order matters. Plugins loaded later override earlier ones. - \textbf{ZPWR} uses wait'0a' through wait'0e' to control this. - zpwrBindOverrideZLE fires in the 0a phase to win conflicts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} ADDING CUSTOM PLUGINS}\vspace{4pt}

Add to \textbf{ZPWR\_GH}\_PLUGINS in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh}:

\begin{codebox}
ZPWR\_GH\_PLUGINS+=( your-user/your-plugin )\\
\end{codebox}

The .tokens.sh file is sourced before plugin loading starts (via zpwrTokenPre), so your additions join the array in time. For conditional loading:

\begin{codebox}
zpwrCommandExists myapp \&\& ZPWR\_GH\_PLUGINS+=( user/zsh-myapp )\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} UPDATING PLUGINS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr updatezinit\\
\end{codebox}

Runs zinit update --all to pull latest from all repos. After updating, run zpwr regenzsh to rebuild completions.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} NUCLEAR OPTION}\vspace{4pt}

If zinit is truly broken, nuke and rebuild:

\begin{codebox}
rm -rf ZPWR\_PLUGIN\_MANAGER\_HOME\\
\end{codebox}

Next shell start auto-clones zinit and re-downloads everything. Slow but thorough. The cockroach approach to debugging.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{ZSH INTERNALS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_deep_dive.png}
\end{center}

\section{Autoloading: Lazy Function Loading}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} AUTOLOADING: LAZY FUNCTION LOADING}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // 443 functions. zero parsing until you call one. //}

Autoloading is zsh's lazy-loading mechanism. Register a name, and zsh only reads the function body from disk on first call. This is why \textbf{ZPWR} can have hundreds of autoloaded functions with negligible startup cost.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW autoload -z WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

1. autoload -z funcname registers 'funcname' as autoloadable. 2. Zsh records the name but does NOT read the file yet. 3. On first call to funcname, zsh searches \$fpath for a file named 'funcname' (exact match, no extension). 4. The file is sourced. The function is now defined in memory. 5. The function executes with the original arguments. 6. Subsequent calls use the in-memory version. No more disk.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE GLOB IN .zshrc}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
builtin autoload -z ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\_COMMON/*(.:t)\\
\end{codebox}

The glob *(.:t) means: *    All files in the directory (.)  Qualifier: regular files only (not dirs or symlinks) :t   Modifier: tail only (strip directory, keep filename) So it registers every regular file's name as a function. Same pattern runs for \textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}\_DARWIN or \textbf{ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD}\_LINUX.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE fpath}\vspace{4pt}

Fpath is the function search path (like \textbf{PATH} but for functions):

\begin{codebox}
fpath=( ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\_COMMON ZPWR\_AUTOLOAD\_COMP\_UTILS ZPWR\_COMPS fpath )\\
\end{codebox}

Order matters: first match wins. \textbf{ZPWR} dirs come first so they can override system completions if needed. Fpath is NOT exported (typeset +x \textbf{FPATH}) -- child shells don't inherit it.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FUNCTION FILE FORMAT}\vspace{4pt}

Each file in the autoload dir contains one function. The file is named exactly like the function. Inside:

\begin{codebox}
function zpwrSomething() \{\\
\# function body\\
\}\\
zpwrSomething "@"\\
\end{codebox}

The last line calls the function with args passed through. This is the \textbf{ZPWR} convention. The function wraps itself.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY AUTOLOADING IS FAST}\vspace{4pt}

443 function registrations = 443 hash table entries. Cost: microseconds. No file I/O, no parsing, no execution. Compare to sourcing 443 files: hundreds of milliseconds. Autoloading turns O(n) startup into O(1) with lazy O(1) per call.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} autoload -Uz}\vspace{4pt}

-U suppresses alias expansion during loading (safety). -z forces zsh-style function loading (vs ksh-style). \textbf{ZPWR} uses -z for its functions, -Uz for system utilities like zrecompile, zmv, zargs, and compinit.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{ZLE: The Zsh Line Editor}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ZLE: THE ZSH LINE EDITOR}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the engine behind every keystroke. readline on steroids. //}

ZLE (Zsh Line Editor) is the subsystem that handles your command line input. Every key press triggers a ZLE widget. \textbf{ZPWR} rewires dozens of these widgets for custom behavior.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WIDGETS: NAMED \textbf{EDITOR} ACTIONS}\vspace{4pt}

A widget is a named action that ZLE can execute:

\begin{codebox}
.accept-line          Built-in: execute the current line\\
.backward-delete-char  Built-in: backspace\\
.self-insert           Built-in: insert the typed character\\
\end{codebox}

The dot prefix means 'built-in widget'. User-defined widgets can override them. \textbf{ZPWR} defines custom widgets like:

\begin{codebox}
zpwrMagicEnter         Custom Enter key behavior\\
zpwrExpandTerminateSpace Custom Space key behavior\\
zpwrAcceptLine          Custom line acceptance\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} bindkey: MAPPING KEYS TO WIDGETS}\vspace{4pt}

bindkey connects key sequences to widgets:

\begin{codebox}
bindkey '\textasciicircum{}M' zpwrMagicEnter    Enter key\\
bindkey ' ' zpwrExpandTerminateSpace  Space key\\
bindkey -v                     Switch to vi mode\\
bindkey -e                     Switch to emacs mode\\
\end{codebox}

\textbf{ZPWR\_BINDKEY}\_VI controls which mode: true=vi, false=emacs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW zpwrMagicEnter WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

On empty line (just pressing Enter): - If in a git dir: runs git status -u . - If not: runs ls -lh . - Resets the prompt via \_p9k\_precmd\_impl + zle reset-prompt On non-empty line: delegates to zle accept-line (zpwrAcceptLine).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW zpwrAcceptLine WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

The accept-line replacement does serious work: 1. Updates zconvey name with current command + TTY + PID 2. Detects commands that modify files (rm, mv, cp, mkdir...) and sets \textbf{ZPWR\_WILL}\_CLEAR=true for post-exec ls refresh 3. Handles sudo + grc colorization for aliased commands 4. Prevents global alias expansion in first position 5. Optionally expands aliases before execution 6. Calls .accept-line (the real built-in)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE Ctrl-F CHORD PREFIX SYSTEM}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} binds Ctrl-F as a prefix key for multi-key chords:

\begin{codebox}
Ctrl-F Ctrl-D   FZF file search\\
Ctrl-F Ctrl-I   FZF word search (ag/grep)\\
Ctrl-F Ctrl-G   FZF git operations\\
\end{codebox}

This gives you an entire second keymap layer without conflicting with normal editing keys. Like tmux prefix but for your command line.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Hook Functions: precmd, preexec, chpwd}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} HOOK FUNCTIONS: precmd, preexec, chpwd}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // zsh calls your code at key moments. event-driven shell. //}

Zsh provides hook points where you can inject code that runs automatically before prompts, before commands, and when directories change. \textbf{ZPWR} uses all three.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} precmd: BEFORE EACH \textbf{PROMPT}}\vspace{4pt}

Runs after a command finishes, right before the prompt draws. P10k's \_p9k\_precmd is the main user -- it updates git status, execution time, and every prompt segment. \textbf{ZPWR} adds zpwrPrecmd to the hook:

\begin{codebox}
function zpwrPrecmd()\{ export ZPWR\_OPTS="-" \}\\
\end{codebox}

This captures the current shell options before each prompt.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} preexec: BEFORE EACH COMMAND}\vspace{4pt}

Runs after Enter but BEFORE the command executes. Receives the command string as \$1. \textbf{ZPWR}'s zpwrPreexec is minimal (a placeholder hook). P10k uses preexec to record the start timestamp for command\_execution\_time segment.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} chpwd: WHEN DIRECTORY CHANGES}\vspace{4pt}

Runs whenever \$\textbf{PWD} changes (cd, pushd, popd, auto\_cd). \textbf{ZPWR}'s zpwrChpwd hook handles directory-change actions.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE \_functions ARRAYS}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh supports multiple hooks via arrays:

\begin{codebox}
precmd\_functions+=( myHook )      Add a precmd hook\\
preexec\_functions+=( myHook )     Add a preexec hook\\
chpwd\_functions+=( myHook )       Add a chpwd hook\\
\end{codebox}

Every function in the array runs at the hook point. Order matters: first in array = first to execute.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW \textbf{ZPWR} REGISTERS HOOKS}\vspace{4pt}

ZpwrBindPrecmd (runs during p10k atload):

\begin{codebox}
precmd\_functions=( zpwrPrecmd \{(@)precmd\_functions:\#zpwrPrecmd\} )\\
\end{codebox}

This puts zpwrPrecmd FIRST, removing any duplicate. The \$\{(@)array:\#pattern\} syntax removes matching elements. zpwrBindPreexecChpwd (runs during syntax-highlighting atload):

\begin{codebox}
preexec\_functions=( \{(@)preexec\_functions:\#zpwrPreexec\} zpwrPreexec )\\
chpwd\_functions=( \{(@)chpwd\_functions:\#zpwrChpwd\} zpwrChpwd )\\
\end{codebox}

These put zpwr hooks LAST (after p10k and other plugins).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE HOOK COUNT IN zpwr top}\vspace{4pt}

The zpwr top dashboard shows the total hook count:

\begin{codebox}
nhooks=((\{\#precmd\_functions\}+\{\#preexec\_functions\}+\{\#chpwd\_functions\}))\\
\end{codebox}

Typical value: 5-8 hooks total across all three arrays. If this number grows unexpectedly, a plugin is adding hooks you may not want. Check with: print -l \$precmd\_functions.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Emulate \& Options}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EMULATE \& OPTIONS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // zsh has 200+ options. emulate -L zsh resets them all locally. //}

Zsh options control fundamental behavior: globbing, word splitting, quoting, history. \textbf{ZPWR} functions use emulate -L zsh to guarantee a known environment regardless of user settings.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} emulate -L zsh}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
builtin emulate -L zsh\\
\end{codebox}

Resets ALL options to zsh defaults for the current function scope. -L means local: options revert when the function returns. Without this, a function might inherit noglob from the caller and break globbing, or inherit shwordsplit and break arrays. \textbf{ZPWR} functions start with this to be immune to the caller's state.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} KEY OPTIONS IN \textbf{ZPWR}}\vspace{4pt}

extendedglob      \#\# = one-or-more, (\#i) = case-insensitive glob \textasciicircum{}pattern = negation. Essential for \textbf{ZPWR} globs. glob\_dots         Dot files matched by * (no need for .*) globstarshort     **.c = **/*.c (recursive glob shorthand) braceccl          \{abcd0-9\} expands as character class rc\_quotes         '' inside single quotes = literal single quote rc\_expand\_param   Array expansion includes prefix: xx\$\{arr\} = xxA xxB nocaseglob        Globs are case-insensitive globally numeric\_glob\_sort  file1 file2 file10 sort numerically prompt\_subst       Allow \$(...) and \$\{...\} in prompt strings transient\_rprompt  Right prompt only on current line


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE noshwordsplit DEFAULT}\vspace{4pt}

Bash splits unquoted variables on whitespace by default. Zsh does NOT (noshwordsplit). This means:

\begin{codebox}
x="hello world"; print x  -> one argument in zsh, two in bash\\
\end{codebox}

If a function inherits shwordsplit (e.g., from emulate sh), arrays and parameter expansions break silently. Another reason for emulate -L zsh.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE local-IN-LOOP BUG}\vspace{4pt}

In zsh, 'local' inside a loop prints the variable value as a side effect when the variable already exists:

\begin{codebox}
for i in 1 2 3; do local x=i; done  \# prints x=1 x=2 x=3\\
\end{codebox}

This is because 'local' on an existing local acts like typeset, which prints. Declare locals BEFORE loops, not inside them.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} auto\_name\_dirs: REMOVED}\vspace{4pt}

This option auto-registers any parameter set to a directory path as a named directory. Sounds nice, but p10k/gitstatus set internal variables to paths, polluting the named dir table. \textbf{ZPWR} removed it. Explicit hash -d in zpwrBindDirs handles it. The comment in .zshrc: \# auto\_name\_dirs removed: pollutes named \# dirs with p10k/gitstatus internals.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Parameter Expansion Magic}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PARAMETER EXPANSION MAGIC}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // zsh parameter expansion is a programming language in itself. //}

Zsh's \$\{...\} syntax can do transformations that would require sed, awk, basename, dirname, tr, and cut in bash. No forks. No subshells. Pure in-process string manipulation.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PARAMETER EXPANSION FLAGS (the parenthesized letters)}\vspace{4pt}

\$\{(K)assoc\}          Keys of associative array \$\{(v)assoc\}          Values of associative array \$\{(kv)assoc\}         Key-value pairs interleaved \$\{(s:/:)var\}         Split string on / into array \$\{(j:,:)array\}       Join array elements with , \$\{(L)var\}            Lowercase the entire string \$\{(U)var\}            Uppercase the entire string \$\{(f)var\}            Split on newlines (lines to array) \$\{(F)array\}          Join with newlines (array to lines) \$\{(t)var\}            Type of variable (scalar, array, etc.) \$\{(q)var\}            Quote the value for re-use in eval \$\{(Q)var\}            Remove one level of quoting \$\{(z)var\}            Split into words like the shell parser \$\{(o)array\}          Sort ascending \$\{(O)array\}          Sort descending.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MODIFIERS (the colon letters)}\vspace{4pt}

\$\{Var:t\}             Tail: basename (/a/b/c.txt -> c.txt) \$\{var:h\}             Head: dirname (/a/b/c.txt -> /a/b) \$\{var:r\}             Root: remove extension (file.txt -> file) \$\{var:e\}             Extension: (file.txt -> txt) \$\{var:A\}             Absolute path (resolve symlinks) \$\{var:l\}             Lowercase (zsh 5.0+) \$\{var:u\}             Uppercase (zsh 5.0+)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SUBSTITUTION AND PATTERN MATCHING}\vspace{4pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} \$\{var//pat/rep\}       Global substitution}

\$\{Var/pat/rep\}        First match substitution \$\{var\#pat\}            Remove shortest prefix match \$\{var\#\#pat\}           Remove longest prefix match \$\{var\%pat\}            Remove shortest suffix match \$\{var\%\%pat\}           Remove longest suffix match.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} EXAMPLES}\vspace{4pt}

From zpwrBindDirs (writing hash dir cache):

\begin{codebox}
print -r "hash -d \{k\}=\{(q)v\}"  -- (q) quotes the value safely\\
\end{codebox}

From zpwrAcceptLine (parsing command buffer):

\begin{codebox}
mywords=( \{(z)cmdstr\} )           -- (z) shell-word splits\\
cmd=\{mywords[1]\#=\}                -- strip leading = from =cmd\\
\end{codebox}

From zpwrBindPrecmd (removing duplicates from array):

\begin{codebox}
\{(@)precmd\_functions:\#zpwrPrecmd\}  -- remove matching element\\
\end{codebox}

These are not academic exercises. They replace forks.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The .zwc Compilation System}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE .zwc COMPILATION SYSTEM}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // bytecode for your shell. because even scripts deserve a compiler. //}

Zsh can compile scripts to .zwc (Zsh Word Code) bytecode. These files skip the parsing phase on load, going straight to execution. \textbf{ZPWR} compiles 50+ files for speed.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT .zwc FILES ARE}\vspace{4pt}

A .zwc file is a binary representation of parsed zsh code. When zsh sources a .zsh file, it checks for a .zwc sibling. If the .zwc exists and is newer than the source, zsh loads the bytecode directly. No lexing, no parsing. Speedup: 2-5x for large files. Marginal for tiny ones.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zrecompile}\vspace{4pt}

The zrecompile utility compiles only if the source is newer:

\begin{codebox}
autoload -Uz zrecompile\\
zrecompile -p -U ZPWR\_ENV\_FILE\\
\end{codebox}

-p means 'check pairs' (source vs .zwc timestamps). -U suppresses alias expansion during compilation. If the .zwc is current, zrecompile is a no-op.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwrRecompileFiles: WHAT GETS COMPILED}\vspace{4pt}
