The function compiles two categories of files: Local files (\textasciitilde{}47): \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zshrc}, \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zshenv}, \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zlogout}, \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.zlogin} p10k-instant-prompt-*.zsh, p10k-dump-*.zsh \$\textbf{ZSH\_COMPDUMP}, \$\textbf{ZPWR\_ALIAS}\_FILE, \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PROMPT}\_FILE \textbf{\$ZPWR\_LOCAL/.tokens.sh}, \$\textbf{ZPWR\_ENV}\_FILE, \$\textbf{ZPWR\_RE}\_ENV\_FILE All .zsh/.sh in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_ENV}/, all .zsh in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_SCRIPTS}/ All plugin .zsh files under \$\textbf{ZPWR\_PLUGIN}\_MANAGER\_HOME/bin/ OMZ lib, plugin, theme, and custom files Global files (\textasciitilde{}8): \textbf{\textbf{/etc/profile}}, \textbf{\textbf{/etc/zshrc}}, \textbf{\textbf{/etc/zshenv}}, \textbf{\textbf{/etc/zprofile}}, etc. These need sudo (compiled with sudo zsh -c zrecompile).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} VERBS FOR COMPILATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr recompile       Compile all known files (zpwrRecompileFiles)\\
zpwr decompile       Remove .zwc files (zpwrUncompile)\\
zpwr refreshzwc      Remove + recompile (clean rebuild)\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE zsh/files MODULE}\vspace{4pt}

Zsh can load builtin file operations via zmodload:

\begin{codebox}
zmodload -F zsh/files b:zf\_ln b:zf\_rm b:zf\_mkdir\\
\end{codebox}

These are shell builtins that replace ln, rm, mkdir. No fork, no exec -- pure kernel syscalls from the shell process. \textbf{ZPWR} uses zf\_ln for the OMZ completion symlink hack. In a loop creating 12 symlinks, this saves 12 fork+exec pairs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} OTHER USEFUL MODULES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zsh/datetime    EPOCHREALTIME, EPOCHSECONDS\\
zsh/complist    Menu-select completion mode\\
zsh/zprof       Profiler (ZPWR\_PROFILING=true enables it)\\
\end{codebox}

Modules are loaded with: zmodload zsh/datetime.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{FZF ARCHITECTURE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/figlet_scan.png}
\end{center}

\section{FZF: The Neural Interface}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FZF: THE NEURAL INTERFACE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the universal adapter between data and decisions. //}

fzf (fuzzy finder) is a general-purpose command-line filter. Pipe anything into it, fuzzy-search interactively, get results. \textbf{ZPWR} wraps fzf into dozens of workflows: files, words, git, verbs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PATTERN: PIPE | FZF | ACTION}\vspace{4pt}

Every fzf integration follows the same architecture:

\begin{codebox}
<data source> | fzf [options] | <action on selection>\\
\end{codebox}

The data source generates lines. fzf presents them. The action consumes the selected line(s). Simple. Composable. Powerful.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF\_DEFAULT\_OPTS}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} sets global fzf defaults in zpwrBindFZFLate:

\begin{codebox}
FZF\_DEFAULT\_OPTS="ZPWR\_COMMON\_FZF\_ELEM --reverse --border --height 100\%"\\
\end{codebox}

Plus a Solarized Dark color scheme layered on top:

\begin{codebox}
--color fg:-1,bg:-1,hl:blue,fg+:base2,bg+:base02,hl+:blue\\
\end{codebox}

\textbf{ZPWR\_COMMON}\_FZF\_ELEM adds the logo prompt and navigation binds:

\begin{codebox}
--prompt='ZPWR\_FZF\_LOGO ' --bind=ctrl-n:page-down,...\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PREVIEW WINDOW}\vspace{4pt}

fzf's --preview flag runs a command for each highlighted item:

\begin{codebox}
fzf --preview 'bat --color=always \{\}'\\
\end{codebox}

\textbf{ZPWR} configures rich previews per context: Files:   bat with syntax highlighting, line numbers, grid Dirs:    stat output Packages: dpkg -I or rpm -qi Git:     diff preview, commit log If bat is installed, it's the default colorizer. Falls back to pygmentize with terminal256 output.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} fzf-completion (Tab)}\vspace{4pt}

fzf replaces the default Tab completion with a fuzzy finder. Type a command, press Tab, and fzf opens with completions:

\begin{codebox}
cd **<Tab>     fzf through subdirectories\\
kill <Tab>      fzf through running processes\\
ssh **<Tab>     fzf through known hosts\\
\end{codebox}

FZF\_COMPLETION\_OPTS controls the preview for these.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} fzf-tab PLUGIN}\vspace{4pt}

MenkeTechnologies/fzf-tab replaces zsh's menu completion with fzf. Any time the completion system would show a menu, fzf-tab intercepts it and shows fzf instead. This means: - Fuzzy matching for every completion, not just ** triggers - Previews for file completions, git branches, everything - FZF\_TAB\_OPTS configures the behavior (ansi, nth, delimiter) - FZF\_TAB\_COMMAND sets which fzf binary to use.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GLOBAL ALIASES FOR FZF}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
\{ZPWR\_GLOBAL\_ALIAS\_PREFIX\}z   Pipe into fzf: ls | ,,z\\
\{ZPWR\_GLOBAL\_ALIAS\_PREFIX\}zb  Inline fzf selection: vim ,,zb\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{16pt}

\section{FZF in Practice}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FZF IN PRACTICE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the theory is simple. the practice is where it gets good. //}

\textbf{ZPWR} ships dozens of fzf-powered workflows. Each follows the generate | fzf | action pattern. Here are the heaviest hitters.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILE SEARCH (zpwr filesearch / Ctrl-F Ctrl-D)}\vspace{4pt}

Pattern: find files | fzf --preview 'bat' | open in editor 1. Find/fd generates a file list (respecting .gitignore) 2. fzf shows files with bat-powered syntax-highlighted preview 3. Selected file opens in \$\textbf{EDITOR} at the matched location FZF\_CTRL\_T\_OPTS configures the preview command.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WORD SEARCH (zpwr wordsearch / Ctrl-F Ctrl-I)}\vspace{4pt}

Pattern: ag/rg search | fzf --preview 'context' | open at line 1. Ag (silver searcher) or rg searches file contents 2. fzf shows matches with file:line:content format 3. Selection opens the file at the exact line number FZF\_AG\_OPTS: delimiter ':', match on 3rd+ field (skip file:line).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GIT ADD (forgitadd)}\vspace{4pt}

Pattern: git diff --stat | fzf --preview 'git diff' | git add 1. Lists modified files with diff stats 2. Preview pane shows the actual diff for each file 3. Toggle files with Tab, confirm to stage them Part of forgit: the git + fzf integration plugin.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VERB SEARCH (zpwr verbsfzf)}\vspace{4pt}

Pattern: \textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS} keys | fzf --preview 'help' | eval 1. All \$\{\#\textbf{ZPWR\_VERBS}\}+ zpwr verbs piped into fzf 2. Preview shows the verb description and command 3. Select a verb to execute it immediately FZF\_ENV\_OPTS\_VERBS configures the preview for verbs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CUSTOM FZF KEY BINDINGS}\vspace{4pt}

The Ctrl-F prefix opens \textbf{ZPWR}'s fzf keymap layer:

\begin{codebox}
Ctrl-F Ctrl-D   File search (find + fzf + editor)\\
Ctrl-F Ctrl-I   Word search (ag + fzf + editor)\\
Ctrl-F Ctrl-G   Git search (git log + fzf)\\
Ctrl-F Ctrl-R   History search with fzf\\
\end{codebox}

These are ZLE widgets bound via zpwrBindOverrideZLE.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwrIntoFzf AND zpwrIntoFzfAg}\vspace{4pt}

Two core ZLE widgets that bridge the command line with fzf: zpwrIntoFzf     File-oriented fzf (Ctrl-T style) zpwrIntoFzfAg   Content-oriented fzf (ag/grep style) Both capture fzf output and insert it into the command line buffer (\$BUFFER). You select files/matches, they appear at your cursor. Seamless integration between search and input.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE COLORIZER CHAIN}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR\_COLORIZER} preference: bat > pygmentize. bat uses themes (BAT\_THEME=\$\textbf{ZPWR\_BAT}\_THEME). Pygmentize uses PYGMENTIZE\_COLOR style. Language-specific colorizers: FZF\_COLORIZER\_C, \_SH, \_YAML, \_JAVA.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Building Your Own FZF Verbs}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} BUILDING YOUR OWN FZF VERBS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // once you grok the pattern, everything becomes fzf-able. //}

The generate | fzf | action pattern is infinitely extensible. Here's how to build your own fzf-powered verbs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PATTERN}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
<generator> | fzf [--preview '<preview-cmd>'] | <consumer>\\
\end{codebox}

Generator: anything that outputs lines (find, git, docker, etc.) Preview: command that shows detail for highlighted line (\textbackslash{}\{\textbackslash{}\}) Consumer: action on selected lines (xargs, while read, eval)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXAMPLE: FZF THROUGH DOCKER CONTAINERS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
function fzf-docker() \{\\
docker ps -a --format '\{\{.ID\}\} \{\{.Names\}\} \{\{.Status\}\}' |\\
fzf --preview 'docker inspect \{1\} | head -50' |\\
awk '\{print 1\}' |\\
xargs -r docker logs --tail 100\\
\}\\
\end{codebox}

Generator: docker ps. Preview: docker inspect. Consumer: docker logs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXAMPLE: FZF THROUGH TMUX SESSIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
function fzf-tmux-session() \{\\
tmux list-sessions -F '\#\{session\_name\}: \#\{session\_windows\} windows' |\\
fzf --preview 'tmux list-windows -t \{1\}' |\\
cut -d: -f1 |\\
xargs tmux switch-client -t\\
\}\\
\end{codebox}

Generator: tmux list-sessions. Consumer: tmux switch-client.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE ACCEPT vs EDIT PATTERN}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} verbs come in two flavors: Accept verbs  Execute the selection immediately Example: verbsfzf selects a verb and runs it. Edit verbs    Insert the selection into \$BUFFER for editing Example: filesearch inserts the path into your command line. The difference is in the consumer. Accept uses eval/exec. Edit uses BUFFER=\$selection + zle redisplay.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MAKING IT A ZLE WIDGET}\vspace{4pt}

To bind your fzf verb to a key:

\begin{codebox}
function my-fzf-widget() \{\\
BUFFER=(my-generator | fzf)\\
CURSOR=\{\#BUFFER\}\\
zle redisplay\\
\}\\
zle -N my-fzf-widget\\
bindkey '\textasciicircum{}F\textasciicircum{}X' my-fzf-widget\\
\end{codebox}

Now Ctrl-F Ctrl-X triggers your custom fzf workflow.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TIPS FOR GOOD FZF EXPERIENCES}\vspace{4pt}

- Use --ansi if your generator outputs color codes - Use --delimiter and --nth to control what fzf matches on - Use -m for multi-select (toggle with Tab) - Use --header for context hints at the top - Preview window size: --preview-window=right:50\%:wrap - Test with: echo 'a\textbackslash{}nb\textbackslash{}nc' | fzf --preview 'echo \{\}'


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{TMUX DEEP DIVE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_mainframe.png}
\end{center}

\section{Tmux Architecture in ZPWR}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TMUX ARCHITECTURE IN ZPWR}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the terminal multiplexer. one screen, infinite shells. //}

Tmux is the backbone of the \textbf{ZPWR} workflow. Multiple shells, persistent sessions, split panes, and code execution across panes -- all orchestrated from \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SESSION / WINDOW / PANE MODEL}\vspace{4pt}

Session    Named group of windows (e.g., 'dev', 'zpwr-study') Window     A tab inside a session (like browser tabs) Pane       A split inside a window (like vim splits) Typical \textbf{ZPWR} layout: 1 session, 2-4 windows, 2-4 panes each. Prefix key: Ctrl-B (default). Followed by a command key.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SESSION PERSISTENCE}\vspace{4pt}

Tmux-resurrect   Manual save/restore of sessions Save: prefix + Ctrl-s Restore: prefix + Ctrl-r Saves: pane layouts, working directories, running programs. @resurrect-processes ':all:' -- saves ALL running programs. Tmux-continuum    Auto-save every 15 minutes Works silently in the background. No manual intervention. Combined with resurrect: your tmux state survives reboots.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE POWERLINE STATUS BAR}\vspace{4pt}

Tmux sources powerline bindings for the status bar:

\begin{codebox}
source-file \textasciitilde{}/.tmux/powerline/bindings/tmux/powerline.conf\\
\end{codebox}

The symlink \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.tmux/powerline} points to pip's powerline-status. Powerline-daemon runs in the background for performance. Status bar shows: session name, window list, hostname, time, battery level (via tmux-battery plugin).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}: THE CONFIG DIRECTORY}\vspace{4pt}

All tmux config lives under \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}:

\begin{codebox}
init.conf               Main config (bindings, options)\\
tmux-mac.conf           macOS-specific overrides\\
tmux-linux.conf         Linux-specific overrides\\
tmux\_ge\_21.conf         Tmux >= 2.1 features\\
tmux\_ge\_29.conf         Tmux >= 2.9 features\\
tmux\_ge\_31.conf         Tmux >= 3.1 features\\
four-panes.conf         Layout: 4 panes\\
sixteen-panes.conf      Layout: 16 panes\\
config-files.conf       Opens brc, vrc, trc, zrc in 4 panes\\
learn.conf              Study layout\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} init.conf HIGHLIGHTS}\vspace{4pt}

- 30,000 line scrollback (\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}\_HISTORY\_LIMIT) - Vi keybindings in copy mode - Prefix S: synchronize-panes (type in all panes at once) - Prefix q: display pane numbers for 5 seconds - 3-second repeat-time for directional keys


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Vim-Tmux Code Execution}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM-TMUX CODE EXECUTION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // select code in vim. execute in the pane next door. magic. //}

The killer feature of the \textbf{ZPWR} vim+tmux integration: write code in vim, send it to an adjacent tmux pane for execution. No copy-paste. No switching windows.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE TmuxRepeat FUNCTION}\vspace{4pt}

Defined in .vimrc, TmuxRepeat has three modes: 'file'    Save the file, execute it in the right pane 'visual'  Save visual selection to temp file, execute it 'repl'    Paste visual selection directly into right pane.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SUPPORTED LANGUAGES}\vspace{4pt}

TmuxRepeat detects file type by extension:

\begin{codebox}
sh, zsh, py, rb, pl, clj, tcl, vim, lisp, hs, ml,\\
coffee, swift, lua, java, f90, cr\\
\end{codebox}

For known types, it sends the file to zpwrRunner.sh which dispatches to the correct interpreter. For unknown types, it falls back to TmuxRepeatGeneric (re-run previous command).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW IT WORKS}\vspace{4pt}

1. Ctrl-V in normal/insert mode (or leader-vj): Saves the file, then sends:

\begin{codebox}
tmux send-keys -t right 'bash "ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/zpwrRunner.sh" "file"' C-m\\
\end{codebox}

2. leader-vk in visual mode: Yanks selection to temp file \$\textbf{ZPWR\_HIDDEN}\_DIR\_TEMP/.vimTempFile.<ext>, then sends that temp file to the right pane for execution. 3. leader-vl in visual mode (REPL mode): Yanks selection, loads it into tmux buffer 'buffer0099', then pastes directly into the right pane. For REPLs like python, irb, node -- code runs line-by-line in the REPL.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PANE TARGETING}\vspace{4pt}

Standard tmux: sends to pane named 'right' (the next pane). MacVim/GUI: sends to session 'vimmers:0.' (external tmux). The pane\_count check ensures a target pane exists.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} allPanes.zsh: BROADCAST TO ALL PANES}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/allPanes.zsh} sends commands to all tmux panes:

\begin{codebox}
prefix + Space   Run command in all panes (single mode)\\
prefix + v       Run command in all panes (multi mode)\\
prefix + b       Open URL in all panes\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SYNCHRONIZE-PANES}\vspace{4pt}

Prefix + S   Toggle synchronize-panes When enabled, every keystroke goes to ALL panes in the window. Useful for running identical commands on multiple servers, or setting up parallel environments simultaneously.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} GOOGLE FROM TMUX}\vspace{4pt}

Prefix + g   Google search from tmux Runs \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TMUX/google.sh} with the current pane content. Select text, press prefix+g, and your browser opens with a Google search for the selection.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Tmux Plugins \& Extensions}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TMUX PLUGINS \& EXTENSIONS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // tpm: the plugin manager for your multiplexer's plugins. yo dawg. //}

\textbf{ZPWR} manages 9 tmux plugins via tpm (Tmux Plugin Manager). They live in \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.tmux/plugins/} and add features that tmux does not provide out of the box.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TPM: TMUX PLUGIN MANAGER}\vspace{4pt}

Installed to \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.tmux/plugins/tpm.} Managed via prefix keys:

\begin{codebox}
prefix + I       Install plugins listed in .tmux.conf\\
prefix + U       Update all installed plugins\\
prefix + Alt-u   Clean plugins not in config\\
\end{codebox}

Plugins are declared in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_INSTALL/.tmux.conf}:

\begin{codebox}
set-option -g @plugin 'tmux-plugins/tpm'\\
set-option -g @plugin 'tmux-plugins/tmux-resurrect'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PLUGIN ROSTER (.tmuxplugins)}\vspace{4pt}

Tmux-plugins/tpm                Plugin manager itself tmux-plugins/tmux-sensible      Sane defaults for tmux tmux-plugins/tmux-resurrect     Session save/restore tmux-plugins/tmux-continuum      Auto-save sessions tmux-plugins/tmux-battery        Battery indicator tmux-plugins/tmux-prefix-highlight Highlight when prefix active tmux-plugins/tmux-yank           System clipboard integration MenkeTechnologies/tmux-thumbs     Quick copy with hints MenkeTechnologies/tmux-fzf-url    FZF through URLs in pane.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} tmux-resurrect: WHAT IT SAVES}\vspace{4pt}

- Pane layouts (splits, sizes, positions) - Working directories for each pane - Running programs (@resurrect-processes ':all:') - Command history is NOT saved (that's zsh's job) Save: prefix+Ctrl-s. Restore: prefix+Ctrl-r.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} tmux-continuum: AUTO-SAVE}\vspace{4pt}

Piggybacks on resurrect. Auto-saves every 15 minutes. @continuum-restore is commented out in \textbf{ZPWR} by default. Enable it to auto-restore on tmux server start.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} tmux-fzf-url: URL PICKER}\vspace{4pt}

Scans the visible pane for URLs, opens them in fzf:

\begin{codebox}
prefix + e   FZF through URLs, open selected in browser\\
prefix + u   FZF through URLs, search mode\\
\end{codebox}

Uses \textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}\_HISTORY\_LIMIT for scrollback depth.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CUSTOM SCRIPTS IN \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}}\vspace{4pt}

Layout scripts create multi-pane environments:

\begin{codebox}
prefix + D   control-window.conf (IDE layout)\\
prefix + F   four-panes.conf\\
prefix + G   eight-panes.conf\\
prefix + O   sixteen-panes.conf\\
prefix + T   config-files.conf (brc, vrc, trc, zrc)\\
prefix + M   learn.conf (study layout)\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} zpwr study: THE SPLIT-PANE LEARNING ENVIRONMENT}\vspace{4pt}

Creates a dedicated tmux session 'zpwr-study' with the encyclopedia on the left and a live shell on the right. Read the docs, try commands. Knowledge through practice.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{VIM DEEP DIVE}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_neural_link.png}
\end{center}

\section{Vim Plugin Architecture}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM PLUGIN ARCHITECTURE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // 15 plugins in .vimrc. each one a force multiplier. //}

\textbf{ZPWR}'s vim config uses Vundle as the plugin manager and ships 15 curated plugins that transform vim into a full IDE with completion, snippets, and tmux integration.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VUNDLE: THE PLUGIN MANAGER}\vspace{4pt}

Plugins live in \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.vim/bundle/.} Managed by Vundle:

\begin{codebox}
set runtimepath+=\textasciitilde{}/.vim/bundle/Vundle.vim\\
call vundle\#begin()\\
Plugin 'VundleVim/Vundle.vim'\\
... more plugins ...\\
call vundle\#end()\\
\end{codebox}

Install: :PluginInstall  Update: :PluginUpdate  Clean: :PluginClean.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} YouCompleteMe: SEMANTIC COMPLETION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'ycm-core/YouCompleteMe'\\
\end{codebox}

The heavyweight champion. Provides semantic completion for: C/C++ (via clangd), Python (via jedi), and more. Config: completion after 1 char, enabled in all file types, completes in comments. Uses C-n/C-p for selection.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} UltiSnips + vim-snippets: SNIPPET ENGINE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'SirVer/ultisnips'\\
Plugin 'honza/vim-snippets'\\
\end{codebox}

Tab expands snippets, Tab/Shift-Tab navigate tab stops. Hundreds of snippets for every language: type 'for<Tab>' in Python and get a complete for loop with cursor placement.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SuperTab: TAB COMPLETION GLUE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'ervandew/supertab'\\
\end{codebox}

Unifies Tab behavior: C-n cycles forward through completions. Coordinates with YCM and UltiSnips so Tab does the right thing in every context (snippet vs completion vs indent).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} vim-auto-save: NEVER :w AGAIN}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin '907th/vim-auto-save'\\
\end{codebox}

Saves on InsertLeave, TextChanged, and TextChangedI. Every edit auto-saves silently. No more 'Unsaved changes' panics. G:auto\_save\_silent=1 suppresses the notification.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} rainbow: MATCHING BRACKET COLORS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'luochen1990/rainbow'\\
\end{codebox}

Nested brackets get different colors: parentheses in blue, inner brackets in orange, deeper in cyan, deepest in magenta. G:rainbow\_active=1 enables it globally on startup.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} vim-multiple-cursors: MULTI-CURSOR EDITING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'TerryMa/vim-multiple-cursors'\\
\end{codebox}

Sublime Text-style multi-cursor. Ctrl-N selects next match, then edit all occurrences simultaneously.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} tmux-complete.vim: CROSS-PANE COMPLETION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'wellle/tmux-complete.vim'\\
\end{codebox}

Completes words from ALL tmux panes. Writing a command in vim that references output in another pane? Tab-complete it. Uses async.vim and asyncomplete.vim for non-blocking operation.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Vim Sessions \& Workflow}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} VIM SESSIONS \& WORKFLOW}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your vim state, frozen in amber. thaw it when you need it. //}

Vim sessions save your entire editing state: open buffers, window layouts, cursor positions. \textbf{ZPWR} integrates sessions with tmux for a seamless development environment.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VIM SESSION MANAGEMENT}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} configures vim sessions as manual-save only:

\begin{codebox}
let g:session\_autosave = 'no'\\
let g:session\_autoload = 'no'\\
\end{codebox}

No surprises. Sessions save when you tell them to. Save: Ctrl-D q (mapped to :SaveSession! In normal/insert mode).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONFIG SESSIONS: vrc, brc, zrc, trc}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} defines verbs for editing its own config files: vrc   Open .vimrc in vim brc   Open shell aliases file (.zpwr\_alias.sh) zrc   Open .zshrc in vim trc   Open tmux.conf in vim The tmux layout script config-files.conf opens all four in a 4-pane arrangement. Prefix+T triggers it. Each pane runs one of these verbs automatically.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE AUXILIARY VIM PLUGINS}\vspace{4pt}

Vim-autotag           Auto-regenerate ctags on save.

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'craigemery/vim-autotag'\\
\end{codebox}

G:autotagStartMethod='fork' -- non-blocking tag generation. Vim-online-thesaurus   Look up synonyms (visual mode \textbackslash{}K)

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'beloglazov/vim-online-thesaurus'\\
\end{codebox}

Vim-zsh-completion     Zsh syntax completion in vim.

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'Valodim/vim-zsh-completion'\\
\end{codebox}

Vim-minimap            Sublime-style code minimap.

\begin{codebox}
Plugin 'severin-lemaignan/vim-minimap'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE zpwr snapshot/restore INTEGRATION}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR} has verbs for full environment snapshots:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr snapshot    Capture terminal environment state\\
zpwr restore     Restore from snapshot\\
\end{codebox}

ZpwrRestore rebuilds shell state: directory stack, aliases, parameters, and more. This complements tmux-resurrect: resurrect restores the tmux layout, snapshot/restore restores the shell state within each pane.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FULL \textbf{ZPWR} VIM WORKFLOW}\vspace{4pt}

1. Open a tmux session with your project layout 2. Vim in left pane, shell in right pane 3. Edit code in vim, Ctrl-V to execute in right pane 4. Visual select + leader-vl to paste into a REPL 5. tmux-complete.vim grabs words from the REPL output 6. Auto-save means you never lose work 7. Prefix+Ctrl-s saves the entire tmux state This is not an IDE. This is a weapon system.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{TEMPRS — TEMPORARY FILE STACK MANAGER}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/temprs_help.png}
\end{center}

\section{temprs: The Stack Machine}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/temprs_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TEMPRS: THE STACK MACHINE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // your filesystem is a stack. you just didn't know it yet. //}

temprs is a Rust-based temporary file stack manager by MenkeTechnologies. Think of it as a LIFO/FIFO clipboard that persists across shell sessions using actual tempfiles on disk. Version 2.9.3. Every operation is instant. No databases, no daemons — just a master record file and a directory of tempfiles.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE STACK PARADIGM}\vspace{4pt}

temprs models your scratch data as a stack with both ends open: push (default), pop, shift, unshift — like a Perl array on disk.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CORE OPERATIONS}\vspace{4pt}

temprs              \# no args: create new tempfile, push to top temprs -o INDEX     \# read contents of tempfile at INDEX to stdout temprs -i INDEX     \# write stdin into tempfile at INDEX temprs -p           \# pop from top of stack (print + remove) temprs -s           \# shift from bottom of stack (print + remove) temprs -u           \# unshift: push stdin to bottom (silent) temprs -a INDEX     \# insert new tempfile at INDEX temprs -r INDEX     \# remove tempfile at INDEX.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE MASTER RECORD}\vspace{4pt}

temprs keeps a master record file that maps stack positions to actual tempfile paths on disk. The temprs directory holds the files themselves. Both are in your system's temp directory. temprs -d           \# list the temprs directory contents temprs -m           \# list the master record file path.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PIPELINE INTEGRATION}\vspace{4pt}

temprs reads stdin when available — perfect for pipelines:

\begin{codebox}
echo 'stash this' | temprs -u  \# silent push to bottom\\
curl -s api.io/data | temprs    \# push response to top\\
temprs -o 0 | jq .             \# read top, pipe to jq\\
\end{codebox}

The -u unshift flag reads stdin and produces no stdout — ideal for mid-pipeline stashing without breaking the flow.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} POSITIONAL ARGUMENT}\vspace{4pt}

temprs myfile.txt   \# read file into new tempfile on stack If both stdin and a file arg are present, stdin takes priority. Your data flows in, gets a stack address, and waits for recall.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{temprs: Advanced Operations}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/temprs_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} TEMPRS: ADVANCED OPERATIONS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // the stack goes deeper. every tempfile has a story. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LISTING \& INSPECTION}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -l           \# list all tempfile paths on the stack temprs -n           \# list numbered (index + path) temprs -L           \# list all tempfile contents temprs -N           \# list numbered with full contents temprs -k           \# count tempfiles on the stack temprs -I INDEX     \# show metadata for tempfile at INDEX.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EDITING \& TAGGING}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -e INDEX     \# open tempfile at INDEX in \$\textbf{EDITOR} temprs -w NAME      \# tag a tempfile with an alias name temprs -R OLD NEW   \# rename tag from OLD to NEW Tags let you retrieve tempfiles by name instead of index. Your stack positions shift; your tags don't.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SEARCH \& CONCATENATE}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -g PATTERN   \# grep through all tempfile contents temprs -C 0 2 5    \# concatenate tempfiles at indices to stdout The grep flag searches across every tempfile on the stack — like a personal knowledge base with instant full-text search.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STACK MANIPULATION}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -D 0 1      \# diff two tempfiles by index or name temprs -M 0 3      \# move tempfile from position 0 to 3 temprs -x INDEX     \# duplicate tempfile onto top of stack temprs -S 0 2      \# swap two tempfiles by index or name temprs --rev        \# reverse the entire stack order temprs -A INDEX     \# append stdin to existing tempfile.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOUSEKEEPING}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -c           \# clear/purge ALL tempfiles from stack temprs --expire 24  \# purge tempfiles older than 24 hours temprs --wc INDEX   \# print line count of tempfile temprs --size INDEX \# print byte size of tempfile temprs --path INDEX \# print the actual filesystem path temprs --sort mtime \# sort stack by name, size, or mtime.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MODE FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

temprs -q           \# quiet mode: suppress output on create temprs -v           \# verbose mode: extra diagnostic output.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE CYBERPUNK -h}\vspace{4pt}

Run temprs -h for the full ASCII art banner with the TEMPRS logo, status bar, and signal meter. Because even help text should look like a console from a Gibson novel. Also: --head INDEX N and --tail INDEX N for partial reads, and --replace INDEX PAT REPL for in-place sed-style edits.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{LSOFRS — ENHANCED LSOF}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/lsofrs_help.png}
\end{center}

\section{lsofrs: Network Intelligence}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/lsofrs_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LSOFRS: NETWORK INTELLIGENCE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // every open file descriptor is a signal. lsofrs reads them all. //}

lsofrs is a Rust-based enhanced lsof wrapper v4.7.0 by MenkeTechnologies. It wraps the venerable lsof with colorized output, structured formatting, and cyberpunk-grade aesthetics.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY NOT RAW LSOF?}\vspace{4pt}

Raw lsof dumps walls of monochrome text. lsofrs adds color coding by type, structured columns, and filtering that doesn't require piping through 5 layers of awk and grep.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SELECTION FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs -i [ADDR]   \# select internet connections ADDR format: [4|6][proto][@host|addr][:svc|port] lsofrs -c COMMAND   \# select by command name (prefix match) lsofrs -p PID       \# select by PID (comma-separated, \textasciicircum{}excludes) lsofrs -u \textbf{USER}      \# select by login name or UID lsofrs -d FD        \# select by file descriptor set lsofrs -g [PGID]    \# select by process group ID lsofrs -a           \# AND selections (default is OR)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} NETWORK FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs -n           \# inhibit hostname resolution (fast mode) lsofrs -P           \# inhibit port-to-name conversion lsofrs -N           \# select NFS files lsofrs -U           \# select UNIX domain socket files.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} INTEGRATION}\vspace{4pt}

The zpwr lsof verb pipes lsof output through fzf for interactive process selection and killing:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr lsof           \# fzf over lsof, select PIDs to kill\\
zpwr lsofedit       \# same but edit the kill command first\\
\end{codebox}

The zpwrLsoffzf widget (bound to \textasciicircum{}F\textasciicircum{}H in viins and vicmd) runs sudo lsof -i | fzf -m, extracts PIDs with awk, and inserts them into your command line. Scan the local network like a deck jockey, pick your targets with fzf.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILES \& DIRECTORIES}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs FILE...      \# list processes using these files lsofrs --dir DIR    \# open files in DIR (one level, like +d) lsofrs --dir-recurse DIR \# recursive (like +D)


\vspace{16pt}

\section{lsofrs: Practical Usage}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} LSOFRS: PRACTICAL USAGE}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // who's listening on port 8080? lsofrs knows. lsofrs always knows. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FINDING WHAT'S ON A PORT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
lsofrs -i :8080        \# anything on port 8080\\
lsofrs -i TCP:443      \# TCP connections on 443\\
lsofrs -i 4            \# all IPv4 connections\\
lsofrs -i 6TCP         \# IPv6 TCP only\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{DISPLAY} \& OUTPUT}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs -F [FIELDS]  \# machine-readable output fields lsofrs -J / --json  \# JSON output for piping to jq lsofrs -R           \# list parent PIDs lsofrs -t           \# terse: PID only (for scripting) lsofrs -0           \# NUL field terminator (xargs -0 friendly) lsofrs --color MODE \# auto, always, or never.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LIVE MONITORING}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs +r 2         \# repeat every 2 seconds lsofrs -W / --monitor \# live full-screen like top lsofrs --delta       \# highlight new/gone FDs in repeat mode lsofrs --follow PID  \# watch one process, highlight opens/closes lsofrs --top 20     \# live top-N processes by FD count lsofrs --watch FILE  \# watch who opens/closes a file over time.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DIAGNOSTICS}\vspace{4pt}

lsofrs --leak-detect \# detect FD leaks (poll every 5s, 3 rounds) lsofrs --stale       \# find FDs pointing to deleted files lsofrs --ports       \# listening ports summary (like ss -tlnp) lsofrs --tree        \# process tree with FD counts (pstree + lsof) lsofrs --summary     \# aggregate FD summary by type and user.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMPARISON}\vspace{4pt}

lsof    — the OG. Every Unix has it. Output is a wall of text. ss      — socket statistics. Fast but sockets only, no files. netstat — deprecated on Linux. Still works on macOS/BSD. lsofrs  — wraps lsof with color, JSON, live monitoring, leak detection, tree views, and the cyberpunk banner you get with lsofrs -h. Built for operators who stare at FD tables and like what they see.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} QUICK RECIPES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
lsofrs -t -i :3000 | xargs kill  \# kill whatever's on 3000\\
lsofrs -c node -a -i TCP          \# node's TCP connections\\
lsofrs -u (whoami) -a -i         \# your network connections\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{EZA — THE MODERN LS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/eza_listing.png}
\end{center}

\section{eza: ls Evolved}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/eza_listing.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EZA: LS EVOLVED}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // ls is dead. long live ls. //}

eza is the modern Rust replacement for ls, formerly known as exa. Maintained by eza-community after exa's author went AWOL. In zpwr, it's the default file lister for everything.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND — THE CONFIGURED INVOCATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
command eza --git -il --classify=always -H --color-scale -g -a --colour=always\\
\end{codebox}

Every flag earns its place: --git              \# git status per file (M modified, N new, I ignored) -i                 \# show inode numbers -l                 \# long listing format --classify=always  \# / after dirs, * after executables, always -H                 \# show hard link count --color-scale      \# gradient colors for file sizes and ages -g                 \# show group ownership -a                 \# show hidden files (dotfiles) --colour=always    \# force color even when piped.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY --classify=always?}\vspace{4pt}

Not just -F. Under p10k instant prompt, stdout gets redirected during init. eza's --classify auto-detects terminal capabilities and when stdout is redirected, it drops the indicators. The =always suffix forces classification regardless of fd state. Without it, your login banner loses all the / and * markers.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHERE IT'S USED}\vspace{4pt}

ZpwrClearList and zpwrListNoClear both eval \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND. The fallback chain: eza -> grc + gls -> raw ls. zpwrListNoClear checks for eza first, falls back to gls on macOS (with grc for colorization) or plain ls on Linux.

\begin{codebox}
alias eza="ZPWR\_EXA\_COMMAND"  \# bound in zpwrBindAliasesLate\\
alias lr="ZPWR\_EXA\_COMMAND -R --tree | less -rMN"\\
\end{codebox}

The lr alias gives you a recursive tree piped through less with line numbers — a full filesystem X-ray.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXTENDED ATTRIBUTES}\vspace{4pt}

Set \textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_EXTENDED=true in .zpwr\_env.sh to add --extended, which shows xattr metadata on macOS (com.apple.quarantine etc).


\vspace{16pt}

\section{eza: Colors \& Integration}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/eza_listing.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} EZA: COLORS \& INTEGRATION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // a file listing without color is just noise. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EZA\_COLORS ENVIRONMENT VARIABLE}\vspace{4pt}

eza uses EZA\_COLORS (or EXA\_COLORS for compat) to customize colors beyond the defaults. Format is colon-separated key=value:

\begin{codebox}
export EZA\_COLORS="di=34:ln=35:so=32:pi=33:ex=31"\\
\end{codebox}

Keys match LS\_COLORS extensions. eza also adds its own: da=date, sn=file size number, sb=file size unit, uu=current user, un=other user, gu=current group.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LS\_COLORS COMPATIBILITY}\vspace{4pt}

eza reads LS\_COLORS as a fallback. If you have a vivid or dircolors setup, eza inherits it. EZA\_COLORS overrides.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TREE VIEW}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
eza --tree          \# recursive tree (like tree command)\\
eza --tree -L 3     \# limit depth to 3 levels\\
eza --tree --git-ignore \# respect .gitignore in tree\\
\end{codebox}

The tree integrates with --git, so you see git status markers inline with the tree structure. New files glow green.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE FALLBACK CHAIN IN zpwrListNoClear}\vspace{4pt}

1. If eza exists: eval \$\textbf{ZPWR\_EXA}\_COMMAND 2. macOS + grc: grc -c \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/conf.gls} gls -iFlhA --color=always 3. macOS bare: ls -iFlhAO (O = macOS file flags) 4. Linux + grc: grc -c \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/conf.gls} ls -iFlhA --color=always 5. Linux bare: ls -ifhlA grc (Generic Colouriser) wraps ls output with conf.gls rules. It's the middle ground between raw ls and eza.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} VERBS USING EZA}\vspace{4pt}

ZpwrClearList clears the terminal then runs the listing. It's called on every cd via the chpwd hook — so you always see what's in the directory you just landed in. zpwrListNoClear does the same listing without clearing.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SORTING \& FILTERING}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
eza --sort=modified  \# sort by modification time\\
eza --sort=size      \# sort by file size\\
eza -r               \# reverse sort order\\
eza --group-directories-first \# dirs at top\\
eza -I '*.pyc|\_\_pycache\_\_'   \# ignore patterns\\
\end{codebox}

Combine with --git-ignore to hide everything in .gitignore. Your file listings become as curated as your code.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{BAT — CAT WITH WINGS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/bat_zshrc.png}
\end{center}

\section{bat: Syntax Highlighting for Everything}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/bat_sample.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} BAT: SYNTAX HIGHLIGHTING FOR EVERYTHING}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // cat is for reading files. bat is for understanding them. //}

bat is a cat clone with syntax highlighting, git integration, and automatic paging. Written in Rust. In zpwr it's the default colorizer: \textbf{ZPWR\_COLORIZER}=bat in .zpwr\_env.sh.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CORE FEATURES}\vspace{4pt}

Line numbers in the gutter. Git diff markers (+/-/\textasciitilde{}) in the margin. Syntax highlighting for 200+ languages. Automatic paging via less when output exceeds terminal height.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DISCOVERY}\vspace{4pt}

bat --list-themes    \# see all available color themes bat --list-languages \# every syntax bat can highlight.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW \textbf{ZPWR} USES BAT}\vspace{4pt}

bat is the preview engine for fzf throughout zpwr:

\begin{codebox}
fzf --preview 'bat --color=always \{\}'\\
\end{codebox}

The full zpwr colorizer invocation:

\begin{codebox}
bat --paging never --wrap character --color always\\
--style="numbers,grid,changes,header"\\
\end{codebox}

This is stored in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_COLORIZER} and expanded into: FZF\_COLORIZER          — preview for generic fzf FZF\_COLORIZER\_FILE     — preview for file-specific fzf FZF\_COLORIZER\_FILE\_TEXT — text file preview (uses -l ASP) When bat is unavailable, zpwr falls back to pygmentize.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr filesearch and zpwr wordsearch both use bat in their fzf preview windows. Vimfilesearch, vimwordsearch, and the emacs variants (emacsfilesearch, emacswordsearch) all inherit the same bat-powered preview pipeline.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STANDALONE USAGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
bat src/main.rs              \# highlighted source view\\
bat -l json data.txt         \# force language detection\\
bat --diff                    \# show only git-changed lines\\
bat -A                        \# show non-printable chars\\
bat --plain                   \# no line numbers, no border\\
bat -r 10:20 file.zsh        \# show only lines 10-20\\
\end{codebox}

The -r (--line-range) flag is perfect for surgical code review.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{bat: Configuration \& Tricks}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/bat_zshrc.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} BAT: CONFIGURATION \& TRICKS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // configure your cat. teach it new tricks. make it purr. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BAT\_THEME}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export BAT\_THEME="Dracula"\\
\end{codebox}

Set this in your env to change the default theme globally. Popular themes: Dracula, Monokai Extended, Nord, OneHalfDark, Solarized (Dark), TwoDark, ansi. Preview them all:

\begin{codebox}
bat --list-themes | fzf --preview='bat --theme=\{\} src/main.rs'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STYLE COMPONENTS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
bat --style=numbers,changes,header\\
\end{codebox}

Components: full, auto, plain, numbers, changes, header, header-filename, header-filesize, grid, rule, snip. Combine with commas. 'full' enables everything. 'plain' disables everything — just colored text, no chrome.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BAT AS MANPAGE COLORIZER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export MANPAGER="sh -c 'col -bx | bat -l man -p'"\\
\end{codebox}

This makes every man page syntax-highlighted. The -l man forces manpage language detection, -p is --plain (no line numbers cluttering man output). Col -bx strips backspace formatting.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SYNTAX CACHE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
bat cache --build    \# rebuild syntax/theme cache\\
bat cache --clear    \# clear and rebuild from scratch\\
\end{codebox}

After adding custom .sublime-syntax files to \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.config/bat/syntaxes/}, rebuild the cache to pick them up.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DEBIAN/UBUNTU GOTCHA}\vspace{4pt}

On Debian-based systems, the binary is batcat not bat due to a name conflict with an unrelated package. Fix:

\begin{codebox}
alias bat=batcat     \# or symlink: ln -s batcat \textasciitilde{}/.local/bin/bat\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BAT + DELTA FOR GIT DIFFS}\vspace{4pt}

delta is a syntax-highlighting pager for git diffs. It uses bat's syntax themes internally. Configure in .gitconfig:

\begin{codebox}
{[}core] pager = delta\\
{[}delta] syntax-theme = Dracula\\
\end{codebox}

delta adds line numbers, side-by-side view, and word-level diff highlighting. bat does files, delta does diffs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BAT\_PAGER}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export BAT\_PAGER="less -RFX"\\
\end{codebox}

Customize the pager bat uses. -R for color, -F to quit if one screen, -X to not clear screen on exit. Or set to '' to disable paging entirely — zpwr does this with --paging never.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{FD-FIND — FIND REPLACEMENT}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/fd_zsh_files.png}
\end{center}

\section{fd: find for Humans}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/fd_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FD: FIND FOR HUMANS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // find is powerful. fd is powerful AND usable. //}

fd is a fast, user-friendly alternative to find, written in Rust by David Peter (sharkdp — also the author of bat). It respects .gitignore by default and uses regex, not globs.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BASIC USAGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
fd PATTERN           \# search current dir recursively\\
fd PATTERN path/     \# search specific directory\\
fd '\textasciicircum{}test.*\textbackslash{}.zsh'  \# full regex support\\
\end{codebox}

No -name, no -type f, no quoting gymnastics. Just the pattern.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} KEY DIFFERENCES FROM FIND}\vspace{4pt}

1. Regex by default (find uses glob with -name) 2. Ignores .git, node\_modules, .gitignore entries automatically 3. 5-10x faster than find due to parallel directory traversal 4. Smart case: case-insensitive unless pattern has uppercase 5. Colorized output by default


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} COMMON FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

fd -e zsh           \# filter by extension fd -t f             \# files only fd -t d             \# directories only fd -t l             \# symlinks only fd -t x             \# executables only fd -H               \# include hidden files fd -I               \# don't respect .gitignore fd -u               \# unrestricted (-H -I combined) fd -g GLOB          \# use glob pattern instead of regex.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EXECUTION}\vspace{4pt}

fd -x cmd \{\}        \# execute command per result (parallel) fd -X cmd\{\}         \# execute once with all results as args.

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} Placeholders: \{\} = path, \{/\} = basename, \{//\} = parent dir,}

\{.\} = Path without extension, \{/.\} = basename without extension.

\begin{codebox}
fd -e jpg -x convert \{\} \{.\}.png  \# convert all jpgs to png\\
\end{codebox}

The -x flag runs in parallel by default — your cores earn their keep. -X collects all results and passes them as one batch. This is the find | xargs pattern without the pipe.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{fd: Integration with zpwr \& fzf}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/fd_zsh_files.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} FD: INTEGRATION WITH ZPWR \& FZF}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // fd feeds fzf. fzf feeds your brain. the pipeline of knowing. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF\_DEFAULT\_COMMAND}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr sets FZF\_DEFAULT\_COMMAND to use find + ag by default:

\begin{codebox}
export FZF\_DEFAULT\_COMMAND='find * | ag -v ".git/"'\\
\end{codebox}

You can override this with fd for faster results:

\begin{codebox}
export FZF\_DEFAULT\_COMMAND='fd --type f --hidden --follow'\\
\end{codebox}

fd is faster because it parallelizes traversal and skips .gitignore entries without needing ag to filter them out.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FD + FZF PATTERNS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
fd -e zsh | fzf                 \# fuzzy find zsh files\\
fd -t f | fzf --preview 'bat \{\}'  \# find + preview\\
vim (fd -t f | fzf)             \# find, select, edit\\
\end{codebox}

This three-tool combo (fd + fzf + bat) is the holy trinity of file navigation. fd finds, fzf filters, bat previews.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TEMPORAL \& SIZE FILTERS}\vspace{4pt}

fd --changed-within 1d  \# modified in last 24 hours fd --changed-before 1w  \# modified more than a week ago fd --size +1m           \# files larger than 1 megabyte fd --size -10k          \# files smaller than 10 kilobytes Units: b (bytes), k (kilobytes), m (megabytes), g (gigabytes). Time: s (seconds), m (minutes), h (hours), d (days), w (weeks).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FD + BAT}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
fd -e rs --exec bat\{\}   \# colorized find results\\
fd -e md -X bat          \# cat all markdown files at once\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FD vs FIND vs LOCATE}\vspace{4pt}

Find   — universal. Every Unix has it. Slow on large trees. Supports -exec, -newer, -perm, -prune. The Swiss Army. fd     — fast parallel traversal, sane defaults, .gitignore aware. Best for daily use in code repositories. Locate — instant results from a pre-built database (updatedb). Stale until the next cron run. No real-time accuracy.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DEPTH \& EXCLUSION}\vspace{4pt}

fd -d 3              \# max depth of 3 levels fd -E node\_modules   \# exclude a directory by name fd -E '*.min.js'     \# exclude by glob pattern Stack -E flags to build precise exclusion lists on the fly.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{RIPGREP — GREP AT LIGHT SPEED}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/rg_search.png}
\end{center}

\section{ripgrep: grep Reimagined}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/rg_help.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} RIPGREP: GREP REIMAGINED}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // grep -r is a campfire. ripgrep is a directed energy weapon. //}

Ripgrep (rg) is a blazing-fast recursive grep written in Rust by Andrew Gallant (BurntSushi). It respects .gitignore, skips binary files, and uses Rust's regex engine for raw speed. 5-100x faster than grep -r on large codebases. Not a typo.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} BASIC USAGE}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
rg PATTERN           \# search current dir recursively\\
rg PATTERN path/     \# search specific directory\\
rg 'fn main'         \# literal or regex — rg auto-detects\\
\end{codebox}

Output is colorized by default: filenames in magenta, line numbers in green, matches highlighted. Grouped by file.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} KEY FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

rg -i PATTERN       \# case insensitive search rg -l PATTERN       \# files with matches only (paths) rg -c PATTERN       \# count matches per file rg -w PATTERN       \# whole word matches only rg -F PATTERN       \# fixed string (no regex interpretation) rg -v PATTERN       \# invert match (lines NOT matching)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILE TYPE FILTERING}\vspace{4pt}

rg -t zsh PATTERN   \# search only zsh files rg -T js PATTERN    \# exclude javascript files rg -g '*.zsh' PAT   \# glob filter (include) rg -g '!*.min.js' PAT \# glob filter (exclude) rg --type-list shows all built-in type definitions.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONTEXT \textbf{LINES}}\vspace{4pt}

rg -A 3 PATTERN     \# 3 lines after each match rg -B 3 PATTERN     \# 3 lines before each match rg -C 3 PATTERN     \# 3 lines before AND after.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MULTILINE MATCHING}\vspace{4pt}

rg -U PATTERN       \# --multiline: match across lines.

\begin{codebox}
rg -U 'struct \textbackslash{}\{[\textbackslash{}s\textbackslash{}S]*?\textbackslash{}\}' \# match entire struct blocks\\
\end{codebox}

Without -U, patterns match within single lines only.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE COMPETITION}\vspace{4pt}

grep -r  — universal, slow, no .gitignore, no parallelism ag      — the silver searcher. Fast, .gitignore aware. rg is 2-5x faster on most benchmarks. Ack     — Perl-based, pioneered the "better grep" category. Slower than both ag and rg. Still has loyal users.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{ripgrep: Advanced \& zpwr Integration}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/rg_search.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} RIPGREP: ADVANCED \& ZPWR INTEGRATION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // rg --json | jq — structured grep for the data-obsessed. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STRUCTURED OUTPUT}\vspace{4pt}

rg --json PATTERN   \# JSON output: one object per line Each line is a JSON object with type (begin, match, end, summary), path, line number, match offsets. Pipe to jq:

\begin{codebox}
rg --json TODO | jq 'select(.type=="match") | .data.path'\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SEARCH \& REPLACE PREVIEW}\vspace{4pt}

rg --replace 'NEW' PATTERN  \# preview replacements This prints what the output WOULD look like with replacements but does NOT modify files. It's a dry-run. Capture groups work:

\begin{codebox}
rg 'foo(\textbackslash{}w+)' --replace 'bar1'  \# foo\_x -> bar\_x\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} STATISTICS}\vspace{4pt}

rg --stats PATTERN   \# show match stats after results Prints: N matches, N matched lines, N files searched, N files matched, bytes searched, duration. Quick audit.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CUSTOM FILE TYPES}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
rg --type-add 'zpwr:*.zsh' -t zpwr PATTERN\\
\end{codebox}

Define a custom type inline and use it immediately. Persist custom types in \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.ripgreprc} (set RIPGREP\_CONFIG\_PATH).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} INTEGRATION}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr grep uses ag (silver searcher) piped into fzf:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr grep            \# ag into fzf for interactive search\\
\end{codebox}

zpwr wordsearch searches file contents with fzf preview:

\begin{codebox}
zpwr wordsearch      \# search by word in subdirectory files\\
\end{codebox}

Both use bat-powered previews in the fzf window.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF + RG LIVE RELOAD}\vspace{4pt}

The ultimate interactive grep: rg feeds fzf, bat previews:

\begin{codebox}
rg --line-number PATTERN |\\
fzf --delimiter : --preview 'bat --highlight-line \{2\} \{1\}'\\
\end{codebox}

rg outputs file:line:match, fzf splits on :, bat highlights the matching line. Three tools, one seamless experience.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} CONFIGURATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
export RIPGREP\_CONFIG\_PATH=\textasciitilde{}/.ripgreprc\\
\end{codebox}

Put default flags in this file, one per line: --smart-case, --hidden, --glob=!.git, --max-columns=200 Also: .rgignore in any directory works like .gitignore — project-specific patterns that rg will skip. .ignore files (used by ag too) are also respected by rg.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{NEOVIM}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_neural_link.png}
\end{center}

\section{Neovim: The Next Generation}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} NEOVIM: THE NEXT GENERATION}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // vim, but the timeline where Bram had unlimited funding. //}

Neovim is the modernized fork of vim. Refactored internals, async everything, built-in LSP, Lua plugin API. In zpwr, the default editor: \textbf{ZPWR\_VIM}='nvim' in .zpwr\_env.sh.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE INIT.VIM BRIDGE}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_INSTALL/init.vim} is just three lines:

\begin{codebox}
set runtimepath\textasciicircum{}=\textasciitilde{}/.vim runtimepath+=\textasciitilde{}/.vim/after\\
let \&packpath = \&runtimepath\\
source \textasciitilde{}/.vimrc\\
\end{codebox}

This means neovim sources the same .vimrc as vim. One config to rule them both. All 80+ vim plugins work in either editor. The symlink is created by plugins\_install.sh:

\begin{codebox}
ln -sf ZPWR\_INSTALL/init.vim \textasciitilde{}/.config/nvim/init.vim\\
\end{codebox}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} NEOVIM-SPECIFIC FEATURES}\vspace{4pt}

Async job control  — run external commands without blocking the editor. vim 8 added this too, but nvim's API is cleaner (jobstart/jobstop). Built-in LSP       — nvim 0.5+ has a native LSP client. Configure with lua: vim.lsp.start(). No plugin needed for basic LSP. Lua plugin API     — write plugins in Lua, not just VimScript. Lua runs 10-100x faster than VimScript. :terminal          — built-in terminal emulator. Better than vim's :terminal — proper job control, scrollback, and buffer integration. True color         — 24-bit color out of the box. No termguicolors hacks needed (though the option exists).


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PROVIDER SUPPORT}\vspace{4pt}

nvim uses providers for python3 and node integration:

\begin{codebox}
pip3 install pynvim          \# python3 provider\\
npm install -g neovim        \# node.js provider\\
\end{codebox}

Run :checkhealth to verify all providers are working.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} VIM INFO}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr catviminfo and zpwr catrecentfviminfo read the nvim shada (shared data) file — the nvim equivalent of .viminfo.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Neovim: Configuration \& Tips}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} NEOVIM: CONFIGURATION \& TIPS}\vspace{6pt}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} // one vimrc. two editors. zero compromises. //}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SHARED CONFIG PHILOSOPHY}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr's init.vim just sources \textbf{\textasciitilde{}/.vimrc.} This means: 1. Every plugin in .vimbundle works in both vim and nvim 2. All keybindings, settings, and autocmds are shared 3. One file to edit: zpwr vrc opens .vimrc 4. No divergent configs to maintain across editors The .vimrc is the single source of truth. Init.vim is a shim.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} NEOVIM-SPECIFIC SETTINGS}\vspace{4pt}

Guard nvim-only settings with has('nvim'):

\begin{codebox}
if has('nvim')\\
set inccommand=nosplit   " live substitution preview\\
set pumblend=15          " popup menu transparency\\
set winblend=15          " floating window transparency\\
endif\\
\end{codebox}

Inccommand is a killer feature: :s/old/new shows replacements live as you type. No more guessing what your regex will do.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} :checkhealth}\vspace{4pt}

Run this in nvim to diagnose your setup. It checks: - python3/node providers (pynvim, neovim npm package) - clipboard support (pbcopy on macOS, xclip on Linux) - terminal capabilities, true color support - plugin health (many plugins register health checks) First thing to run when something feels wrong.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SESSION TRACKING}\vspace{4pt}

Tpope/vim-obsession (in .vimbundle) tracks sessions:

\begin{codebox}
:Obsession           \# start tracking session to Session.vim\\
:Obsession!          \# stop tracking and delete Session.vim\\
nvim -S Session.vim  \# restore session\\
\end{codebox}

Works in both vim and nvim since it's a VimScript plugin.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY \textbf{ZPWR} SUPPORTS BOTH}\vspace{4pt}

nvim is better locally — async, LSP, Lua, true color. But: - Remote servers often only have vim (or vi) - Docker containers ship vim, not nvim - macOS ships vim in \textbf{\textbf{/usr/bin/vim}} The shared .vimrc means your muscle memory works everywhere. nvim when you can, vim when you must, same config either way.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} \textbf{EDITOR} VERBS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
zpwr vrc             \# edit .vimrc (shared by both)\\
\end{codebox}

Both editors, one config, zero excuses. The way it should be.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{PERL IN ZPWR}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/perl_version.png}
\end{center}

\section{Perl Oneliners: The Secret Weapon}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/perl_version.png}
\end{center}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PERL ONELINERS: THE SECRET WEAPON}\vspace{6pt}

sed wishes it could do this. awk cries in a corner.

Perl is zpwr's scalpel for text surgery. Where sed and awk stumble on complex patterns, perl oneliners slice clean. zpwr uses perl throughout for text transformation, parsing, delimiter handling, and file manipulation.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE KEY FLAGS}\vspace{4pt}

-e 'code'     execute code -n            loop over input lines (like sed) -p            loop and print each line (like sed -p) -l            chomp newlines, add them back on print -a            autosplit into @F array (like awk) -F'sep'       set field separator for -a -i            in-place edit (like sed -i) -E            enable say, state, switch (modern perl)


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHITESPACE CLEANUP (scripts/lib.sh)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -pi -e 's@\textbackslash{}s+@\textbackslash{}n@g; s@\textbackslash{}x09@    @g'\\
\end{codebox}

Strips trailing whitespace, converts trailing tabs to spaces. The -pi combo: -p loops, -i edits in place. zpwr's file cleaner.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PRINT SEPARATOR \textbf{LINES}}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -le "print '\#'x80"\\
\end{codebox}

Prints 80 \# characters. The x operator repeats a string. Used in zpwrPrettyPrint for visual separators.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} DELIMITER TRANSFORMATION}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -F"ZPWR\_DELIMITER\_CHAR" -anE 'say ...'\\
\end{codebox}

Splits lines on \textbf{ZPWR\_DELIMITER}\_CHAR into @F fields. Used heavily in zpwrContribCount, zpwrTotalLines for reformatting git contribution data into tabular output.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILTER BLANK \textbf{LINES}}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -ne 'print if /\textbackslash{}S/'\\
\end{codebox}

Only prints lines containing non-whitespace. Used in zpwrRegenAllKeybindingsCache to clean keybinding dumps.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE PERL REPL ALIAS}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
plr (alias for rlwrap perl -wnE'say eval()//@')\\
\end{codebox}

Interactive perl REPL with readline. Type expressions, get results.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Perl Oneliners: Advanced Patterns}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} PERL ONELINERS: ADVANCED PATTERNS}\vspace{6pt}

When you need a chainsaw, not a butter knife.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{PATH} QUOTING (zpwrGetFound)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -pe 's@\textasciicircum{}([\textasciitilde{}]*)([\textasciicircum{}\textasciitilde{}].*)@1"2"@;s@\textbackslash{}s+@ @g'\\
\end{codebox}

Wraps non-tilde paths in double quotes and collapses spaces. Handles filenames with spaces for safe shell consumption.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FZF RESULT FORMATTING (zpwrAgIntoFzf)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -pe 's@\textasciicircum{}(.*?):(\textbackslash{}d+):(.*)@+2 "PWD/1"@'\\
\end{codebox}

Converts grep output (file:line:match) into vim args (+line "file"). The brain behind zpwr grep -> fzf -> vim pipeline.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} VERB LIST FORMATTING (zpwrVerbsFZF)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -e '@a=<>;for(@a)\{print "zpwr 1" if m\{\textasciicircum{}(\textbackslash{}S+)\textbackslash{}s+\}\}'\\
\end{codebox}

Reads verb list, extracts first word, prepends 'zpwr '. Builds the command string that fzf executes on selection.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} IN-PLACE RENAME (zpwrChangeNameZpwr)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -i -pe 's@\textbackslash{}bOLDNAME\textbackslash{}b@NEWNAME@g' **/*(.)'\\
\end{codebox}

Renames all occurrences across all files. The \textbackslash{}b word boundary prevents partial matches. The **(.) glob hits all regular files.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} LOGIN COUNTING (zpwrLoginCount)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -e '"'"'print qx(last -f "\_") for </var/log/wtmp*>'"'"'\\
\end{codebox}

Reads all wtmp files and runs last on each. The diamond operator <> globs and iterates. Then pipes to:

\begin{codebox}
perl -lane '"'"'print [0] if /\textbackslash{}S+/ \&\& !/wtmp/'"'"'\\
\end{codebox}

Extracts username (first field) from non-empty, non-header lines.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} FILE COUNT DEDUP (zpwrEnvCounts)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
perl -lne '@l=<>;@u=do\{my \%seen;grep\{!seen\{\_\}++\}@l\};'\\
\end{codebox}

Reads all lines, deduplicates with a hash, then checks if each unique path is a real file. The densest oneliner in zpwr. Counts unique zpwr files across all tracked locations.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} IP ADDRESS EXTRACTION (ipa alias)}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
ifconfig | perl -lane 'do \{print \textbackslash{}[1]=\textasciitilde{}s/addr//r;exit0\} if /inet\textbackslash{}s/ and !/127/'\\
\end{codebox}

{\small\itshape\color{dimtext} Finds the first non-loopback inet address. The s///r flag}

Returns the modified string without changing the original.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY PERL OVER SED/AWK?}\vspace{4pt}

1. Perl regex is more powerful (lookahead, backrefs, /x) 2. -i flag for in-place edit is reliable across OS 3. Complex logic in one line (if/unless/for/do blocks) 4. @F autosplit is awk-like but with perl's full stdlib 5. Perl is installed everywhere (even minimal containers)


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{HIDDEN SUBSYSTEMS}


\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{/Users/wizard/.zpwr/docs/screenshots/art_mainframe.png}
\end{center}

\section{Zconvey: Terminal Communication}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} ZCONVEY: TERMINAL COMMUNICATION}\vspace{6pt}

Every terminal screams its identity into the void.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT IS ZCONVEY?}\vspace{4pt}

MenkeTechnologies/zconvey - a zinit plugin Allows sending commands between terminal sessions. Your terminals can talk to each other. Yes, really. Loaded via zinit in \textbf{\$ZPWR\_INSTALL/.zshplugins}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} HOW \textbf{ZPWR} USES IT}\vspace{4pt}

Every time you press Enter, zpwrAcceptLine fires. It calls zc-rename to label the current terminal with: TTY:\$\{TTY\} PID:\$\{\$\} CMD:\$BUFFER \textbf{PWD}:\$\{PWD\} DATE:\$(date) This broadcasts your terminal's state to the zconvey bus. Every pane, every tab, every session - all labeled.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE CONVEY VARIABLES}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{ZPWR\_CONVEY}\_NAME      the full label string sent to zconvey \textbf{ZPWR\_CONVEY}\_LAST\_CMD  the last command (for zconvey commands) When you run a zc-* command, it preserves the previous label so your terminal doesn't get renamed to "zc-send ls".


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE RENAME LOGIC}\vspace{4pt}

\begin{codebox}
if ! [[ (zpwrExpandAliases BUFFER) = zc* ]]; then\\
ZPWR\_CONVEY\_NAME="TTY:\{TTY\} PID:\{\} CMD:..."\\
zc-rename ZPWR\_CONVEY\_NAME\\
\end{codebox}

If BUFFER starts with zc*, keep the old label. Otherwise, stamp the terminal with the new command.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHY THIS MATTERS}\vspace{4pt}

tmux status bar can show what each pane is running. You can identify panes by their last command and \textbf{PWD}. Remote debugging: see exactly what terminal ran what. It's like process accounting, but for your shell sessions.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SENDING COMMANDS BETWEEN TERMINALS}\vspace{4pt}

Zc-send 2 'cd /tmp \&\& ls'  send command to terminal 2 zc-all 'echo hello'        broadcast to all terminals Your terminals are a mesh network. Act accordingly. SIGNAL: zpwrAcceptLine is the heartbeat of zpwr. Every Enter keypress: rename terminal, check for clear, detect file-modifying commands, send tmux keys. Zconvey makes that heartbeat visible across sessions.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The 24-Hour Updater Loop}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE 24-HOUR UPDATER LOOP}\vspace{6pt}

Your system updates itself while you sleep.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE INFINITE LOOP}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/autoUpdater.sh} runs forever. Every 24 hours it wakes up and updates everything.

\begin{codebox}
while true; do\\
bash -l updater.sh -e\\
\# sleep in 10-minute chunks until 24h elapsed\\
done\\
\end{codebox}

Uses Perl Time::Piece to calculate the next update time. Logs the time diff on each wake to \$\textbf{ZPWR\_LOGFILE}.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} WHAT GETS UPDATED}\vspace{4pt}

Updater.sh orchestrates the full update: zpwrUpdater      runs the main zpwr update sequence zpwrUpdateDeps   updates all package manager dependencies Homebrew, npm, pip, gem, go - everything gets refreshed. History files backed up. Logs rotated. Deps pinned.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SYSTEMD SERVICE (LINUX)}\vspace{4pt}

On Linux, autoUpdater.sh runs as a systemd service. zpwr restart      restart the zpwr systemd service zpwr serviceup    start and enable the systemd service zpwr servicedown  stop and disable the systemd service On macOS, \textbf{\$ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/updaterForLaunchCtl.sh} is used.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} EMAIL NOTIFICATIONS}\vspace{4pt}

\textbf{\$ZPWR\_SCRIPTS/updaterEmail.sh} Pipes the update log to email via mutt. Wake up to a full report of what changed overnight. Your system admin is a bash script. It never sleeps.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SLEEP STRATEGY}\vspace{4pt}

Doesn't sleep for 24h straight (that would drift). Sleeps in 10-minute intervals, checks elapsed time:

\begin{codebox}
((timediff > ((3600 * 24)))) \&\& break\\
\end{codebox}

This handles laptop suspends and clock adjustments. WARNING: The updater keeps zpwr and all deps current. If something breaks after an update, check the log: zpwr taillog  to see what the updater did last. The machine maintains itself. You just write code.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{The 32-Pane REPL Layout}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE 32-PANE REPL LAYOUT}\vspace{6pt}

Every language, one screen, zero excuses.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE TMUX KEYBINDINGS}\vspace{4pt}

Prefix R  source \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TMUX/thirtytwo-panes-repl.conf} prefix O  source \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TMUX/sixteen-panes.conf} prefix G  source \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TMUX/eight-panes.conf} prefix F  source \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TMUX/four-panes.conf} One keystroke splits your terminal into a REPL army.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE 32 REPLs (prefix R)}\vspace{4pt}

Pane 0:  lua          pane 1:  swift        pane 2:  ghci pane 3:  clisp        pane 4:  ocaml        pane 5:  clojure pane 6:  pry (ruby)   pane 7:  bpython      pane 8:  php -a pane 9:  groovysh     pane 10: csharp       pane 11: kotlinc pane 12: node         pane 13: coffee       pane 14: tclsh pane 15: ksh          pane 16: bash         pane 17: csh pane 18: perl -dE1    pane 19: gore (go)    pane 20: jshell pane 21: mycli        pane 22: pgcli        pane 23: python2 pane 24: nslookup     pane 25: mongo        pane 26: irb pane 27: scala        pane 28: utop         pane 29: iex pane 30: bc           pane 31: gforth 32 languages. 32 panes. One tmux window. Absolute madness.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE VIM-TMUX EXECUTION PIPELINE}\vspace{4pt}

Write code in vim, send it to a REPL pane: Supported languages for vim-to-tmux execution: zsh, bash, python, ruby, perl, node, go java, scala, R, octave Select code in vim, fire it into the matching pane. Edit-run-edit-run without ever leaving vim.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} PROGRESSIVE LAYOUTS}\vspace{4pt}

Don't need 32 languages? Scale down: prefix G  8 panes   - quick polyglot session prefix O  16 panes  - serious multi-language work prefix R  32 panes  - full REPL matrix (needs big screen) Each layout is a tmux conf file in \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TMUX}/ NOTE: prefix R on a 13" laptop is an act of hubris. You'll need at least a 27" monitor to read anything. But hey, it works. And it's glorious.


\vspace{16pt}

\section{Cross-Platform Installer}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} CROSS-PLATFORM INSTALLER}\vspace{6pt}

One repo to rule them all, one script to install them.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} INSTALLER SCRIPTS IN \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL}}\vspace{4pt}

Formulae.sh        Homebrew formulae (macOS/Linux) casks.sh           Homebrew casks (macOS GUI apps) mincasks.sh        minimal cask set for lean installs taps.sh            Homebrew tap repositories npm\_install.sh     Node.js packages via npm npm.sh             npm package list gems.sh            Ruby gems pip\_install.sh     Python packages via pip pip.sh / pip3.sh   pip package lists go\_install.sh      Go packages rustupinstall.sh   Rust toolchain via rustup.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{EDITOR} \& PLUGIN INSTALLERS}\vspace{4pt}

Neovim\_install.sh       Neovim setup vim\_install.sh          Vim from source vim\_plugins\_install.sh  Vim plugin manager + plugins zsh\_plugins\_install.sh  Zsh plugins via zinit tmux\_plugins\_install.sh Tmux plugin manager + plugins spacemacs\_install.sh    Spacemacs (Emacs) setup.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE}\vspace{4pt}

Common.sh  shared installer functions used by all scripts zpwrInstall.sh  the master installer orchestrator Supports 20+ Linux distros, macOS, and FreeBSD. Detects your OS and calls the right package manager.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} \textbf{ZPWR} VERBS FOR INSTALLATION}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr install            run configure, make, make install zpwr uninstall          uninstall all zpwr configs zpwr regenconfiglinks   regen symlinks from \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL} to \$\textbf{HOME} regenconfiglinks recreates dotfile symlinks after a reinstall.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} THE SYMLINK STRATEGY}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr doesn't copy config files to \$\textbf{HOME}. It symlinks them. .vimrc, .tmux.conf, .gitconfig - all point back to \$\textbf{ZPWR\_INSTALL}. Edit once in the repo, git commit, and every machine gets it. zpwr regenconfiglinks rebuilds them if they break. PHILOSOPHY: Package lists are just text files. Add a line to formulae.sh, and your next machine gets it. Your entire dev environment is version-controlled.


\vspace{16pt}


\chapter{QUALITY ASSURANCE}

\section{The Test Suite}


\vspace{10pt}
{\large\bfseries\color{neoncyan} THE TEST SUITE}\vspace{6pt}

60 test files standing between you and chaos


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TESTING FRAMEWORK}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr uses zunit - a testing framework written in zsh. Config lives in \textbf{\$ZPWR/.zunit.yml} Tests dir: \$\textbf{ZPWR\_TEST}  Output: \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TEST/\_output} Support helpers: \textbf{\$ZPWR\_TEST/\_support}


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} TEST CATEGORIES}\vspace{4pt}

T-autoload-*       verify all autoloaded functions load, defined, existence, fpath, whence, type, body Every autoload function must: exist, load, have a body. T-autoload-subdir-* same checks for subdirectory autoloads t-env-*            verify environment variables defined, exported, comprehensive, type checks Every \textbf{ZPWR}\_* var must be defined and exported. T-scripts-*        verify script files executable, readable, non-empty, have shebangs, valid syntax t-verbs-*          verify zpwr verb definitions key format, non-empty values, callable, type checks.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} MORE TEST FILES}\vspace{4pt}

T-functions.zsh     test core shell functions t-callable-fns.zsh  verify functions are callable t-navigation.zsh    test directory navigation t-install-files.zsh verify installer file integrity t-repo-files.zsh    verify repo structure t-plugin-counts.zsh verify plugin load counts.


\vspace{12pt}
{\bfseries\color{neonyellow} RUNNING TESTS}\vspace{4pt}

zpwr test     run all zpwr tests zpwr testall  run ALL env tests (comprehensive sweep) fail\_fast: true means first failure stops the run. Verbose: true means you see every assertion. GUARANTEE: Every autoload loads. Every env var defined. Every script executable. Every verb callable. 60 test files verify zpwr won't break your shell. If tests pass, your environment is sound. Ship it.
